Tech analyst Philip Klöckner in conversation with Conor McNamara

Stripe(108.0K)·41:45·304 views·2026-06-30
Philip KlöcknerInvestor and tech analyst Conor McNamaraEMEA CRO, Stripe
TL;DRThe rapid integration of AI is driving a profound economic paradigm shift—exemplified by Anthropic’s unprecedented 500% customer revenue expansion—that will redefine software development, organizational scale, and global labor dynamics.
To remain globally competitive,...

TL;DR

The rapid integration of AI is driving a profound economic paradigm shift—exemplified by Anthropic’s unprecedented 500% customer revenue expansion—that will redefine software development, organizational scale, and global labor dynamics. To remain globally competitive, Germany and Europe must transition from optimizing legacy structures to aggressively fostering a parallel, engineer-led startup ecosystem supported by open-source AI and unified digital market frameworks.

The AI Paradigm Shift and the "SaaSpocalypse" Reality Check

  • Unprecedented Revenue Expansion: Tech analyst Philip Klöckner highlighted that Anthropic’s net revenue expansion rate is currently north of 500%, meaning the average customer spends five times more year-over-year—a metric completely unheard of in traditional software.
  • The "SaaSpocalypse" Narrative is Exaggerated: Despite market sell-offs of prominent SaaS companies, licensing established platforms remains more economical for enterprises than "vibecoding" (AI-generating) custom software.
      • The Cost of Maintenance: Generating a custom tool with AI may be cheap initially, but maintaining security, adapting to changing compliance, and updating code is still more expensive than a standard software license.
      • System of Record Moats: Heavy enterprise systems like SAP possess deep specialized value—such as managing complex tax regimes across 210 countries—making them nearly impossible for AI-generated applications to displace in the short term.
  • AI-Native Organizational Design: The true disruption will occur in newly formed startups built AI-first from scratch. These companies will operate with radically smaller headcounts (e.g., 12 people instead of 1,200) and completely bypass the need for traditional enterprise resource planning (ERP) and HR software.

Workforce Displacement, Demographics, and "AI Natives"

  • Demographic Alignment in Germany: While critics worry about job displacement, Germany's severe demographic crisis—a structural deficit of 500,000 workers annually—means that automating 1% of jobs per year via AI and robotics is a necessary economic stabilizer rather than a crisis.
  • Supply-Side Career Shifts: Traditional entry-level roles are facing disruption, but the shift is heavily driven by candidate supply rather than just employer demand.
      • Declining STEM Pipeline: US computer science university enrollments fell from 660,000 to 600,000 over the last two years as students proactively avoid career paths they assume will be automated.
  • The "AI Native" Cohort: A sharp divide has emerged between legacy workers seeking excuses to avoid AI and "AI natives" (current university graduates) who use AI as a standard, baseline utility. Conor McNamara noted that Stripe data confirms once users adopt AI tools, they "tool hop" but never return to non-AI workflows.

Agentic Commerce: Hype vs. Practical B2C and B2B Realities

  • B2C Shopping Limitations: Autonomous shopping agents will scale slowly because they conflict with core human shopping behaviors. Humans enjoy "window shopping," comparison, and selecting from a visual matrix of options.
  • High-Value Early Consumer Use Cases: Agentic commerce will succeed first by taking over high-friction administrative tasks rather than product discovery.
      • Consumer Claims: Automatically filing delayed travel claims (e.g., against Deutsche Bahn) to recover refunds.
      • Local Services: Booking appointments with dentists, doctors, hair salons, and restaurants.
  • Rapid B2B Procurement Adoption: Unlike consumers, businesses prioritize efficiency over experience. B2B agentic commerce will scale rapidly to handle tactical corporate spending, high-frequency procurement, and API-to-API transactions.

Revitalizing German and European Innovation

  • Transitioning from Administration to Engineering: Klöckner noted that German business education trains graduates to optimize existing processes by 5% to 10%. To compete globally, Germany must empower engineers who build entirely new business models that render old processes redundant.
  • Eliminating Growth Inhibitors: Europe must resolve several structural barriers to foster a competitive tech ecosystem:
      • Digital Single Market: Startups are severely hindered by navigating 20+ distinct tax regimes, languages, and company formation rules across Europe.
      • Talent Acquisition Friction: European founders struggle with long visa wait times for foreign engineers. Countries must implement fast-track, 4-week visas for company-vouched talent.
  • Capitalizing on Biotech and Life Sciences: Germany cannot win in low-cost robotics or heavy manufacturing due to high energy costs and a lack of natural resources (areas dominated by China). Instead, Germany should leverage its world-class academic institutions to build "the next ten BioNtechs" using AI-driven pharmaceutical research.
  • The Imperative for Open Source AI: To avoid a wealth transfer to US tech monopolies, Europe must heavily support a robust open-source LLM framework. This ensures European enterprises can build sovereign AI tools without being locked into expensive foreign proprietary models.

Notable Quotes

"If you would take AI away from me again, I would be mad, I would be crazy mad, and I would be begging to pay you 2,000 euros per month to let me continue to use it." — Philip Klöckner
"We are trained to improve some process by 5 to 10% and extract 5 to 10% more productivity... But engineers are trained to build something entirely new, and we need more of these engineers who don't think how to extract efficiency from an existing process, but how to rebuild it entirely." — Philip Klöckner
"As we don't have a sovereign German player in LLMs, we need to hope that there will be freely, publicly, openly available models that we can employ... If not, if we become more and more dependent on US companies, history will repeat itself and the same that happened in the digital advertising market will happen." — Philip Klöckner
Philip, welcome. My pleasure, thank you. Awesome to have you here. Thanks for having me. What did you think of that video? I would agree that it depends on how you define singularity. Is AI taking off and becoming totally independent? I don't think we're there yet. But what I do see are the hints or the bellwethers of a huge paradigm shift. If you look at new business formation, and if you look especially at how AI accelerates itself—how software builds the next version of the AI, and that version builds the next version—the production velocity that companies like OpenAI or Anthropic have is incredible. If you look at the revenue expansion of Anthropic, it is unheard of. In our podcast, I said Anthropic will be the first company that will have a revenue expansion rate north of 200%. It turned out it's actually north of 500%, which means the average Anthropic customer spends five times more compared to last year with them. That is completely unheard of. Every software vendor would kill for such a revenue expansion, and that's what I mean by paradigm shifts. Stuff that was true before needs completely new benchmarks and metrics. So, I do see this moment in time, and 2026 is a year that will change a lot of things, for sure. For sure. 500%, wow. We could go many ways with this conversation, so we'll stick to maybe software development to start with. We see it at Stripe a lot. What's your perspective on software development and the changes that have happened as a result of AI? Do you think that the world as a whole has digested the implications of the pace at which software is getting built today versus 24 months ago? I think most people haven't. There are a few people, mostly working in startups or founding startups, building new businesses, who are well aware of the possibilities and make maximum use of them. Then, there are also people who maybe work in incumbent companies in the German Mittelstand who still try to find excuses why not to use AI, why their piece of software couldn't be built by AI, or why their job couldn't be done partly by AI. I think that dichotomy—the world of AI haves and have-nots—will continue to exist. We're going to stick with that for at least a few more years. We saw the same with digitalization and the internet. For some people, usually the younger generation, it became completely normal; they were digital natives, they were internet natives. Now, you will have AI natives. People leaving university right now are AI natives; they have done their exams with AI. On the other hand, there are people who still try to find excuses not to use AI, which is stupid, I think. And especially with software development, which is probably what makes Anthropic such a good company—they focused on the perfect entry market from a go-to-market perspective. Software development is for sure the area where you will have autonomous agents building pieces of software. It doesn't mean it's perfect yet, of course. You still need a human in the loop, and you still need to check stuff and redo it. But you can't deny you're three or four times more effective or efficient building software; if you aren't, you're just doing it wrong. I mean, just look at how people personally use AI. There are a few skeptics now who say that AI is a bubble that will burst, and they almost act like it's going to go away again. We did the exact same with the internet and digitalization, of course. Don't fight gravity. Exactly, it's not going to go away. People have started using AI. A good test for what a good product is: would you take it away from me? Would I regret it? If you were to take AI away from me again, I would be mad. I would be crazy mad, and I would be begging to pay you 2,000 euros per month to let me continue to use it. Yeah, and we see that actually in our data at Stripe. We see quite a lot of users moving from one AI tool to another, but we don't see people who start on one AI tool going back to non-AI tools. There is a lot of tool hopping happening, but not necessarily like, "I'm going back to the way I used to do it two years ago." That's just not happening at all, which I think reinforces your point. That's what I envy you for. That data—I would obviously give my firstborn to get that kind of data. I'm very happy for the Ramp data, which gives us some indication about B2B usage, but obviously I would love to see who's canceling their contracts. We'll take a boat for a point later on and I'll tell you some more. Awesome. Just to go back to your point around technology being a democratizing force in the world, I worked in cloud for ten years before joining Stripe and that was a big story. But actually, when you look at a lot of the data, in some cases technology is not necessarily an economic equalizer. At least in the short term, it can create a dichotomy between the haves and the have-nots. What's your view? Where are you on the whole stance of AI? Is it going to create further divergence in the world in terms of economic prosperity, or do you see a future where it's going to lift all boats? Excellent question. I would say both. I would always argue it's probably a very low-barrier technology, so almost everyone potentially has access to free AI. If you use Google Gemini or Meta, you can basically access it for free. Access to information and to knowledge was never so readily available for people at almost zero cost. That should be a huge equalizer and at least offer a chance for everyone. Still, I think that there might be monopolies in some of the AI markets again, and lots of the value will accrue to very few people, potentially, unless we find ways to distribute the value more evenly. But what you can say for sure is that even if the economic value—basically the money that is earned—accrues to very few people, the kind of consumer benefit that you have is very evenly distributed. Everyone can use it, most people will be able to use it for free, and everyone who wants to can reap enormous benefits personally from it. You basically have access to an executive coach now, to a therapist—stuff that's really hard to get, at least here in Germany, is in your pocket now at a decent quality, and it's getting only better over time. Even if another trillion-dollar company is minted by this, every one of us is able to benefit from it a lot. So, these things can be true at the same time, I think. Agree, agree. No, that all makes sense. Therapist, fitness coaches, financial coaches, who knows? Awesome. I want to talk to you a bit about—again, I don't think Patrick has said this before—the SASpocalypse. It's a discussion like, you know, AI is software eating the world, or almost AI is eating software in some ways. And then Patrick has used this analogy of a pizza recently, where he's saying software should now be almost like a pizza—you know, make it once, use it once, and move on to the next thing. Where are you in this whole SASpocalypse thing? Is it overdone, or is it a reality? For some pieces of software it will be a reality, but on average, generally I think it's an over-exaggeration. The sell-off of software companies—I don't think certain companies are worth half or only 25% of what they have been worth before, stuff like Monday.com or ServiceNow. I think it's always overdoing some emotional reaction if you sell off a company by 75% in a few weeks or a few months. People are not aware of how software purchasing in corporates actually works. Because, of course, I could vibecode my own ServiceNow now—or ServiceNow is a bit harder, let's take Monday.com or Asana. You could vibecode that, even tailored a bit toward your own needs, which would be good. However, you still need someone maintaining it. You need someone to care for the security and safety of the product, and you need to continue to develop it, so it will still take some cost. With AI and vibecoding, it will be much cheaper than it has been to build the first Monday.com, but you don't need to be cheaper than Monday.com; you need to be cheaper than one license of Monday.com to make it economically feasible. If not, it doesn't make sense to build your own piece of software. So, as long as one license of ServiceNow or Monday.com is cheaper than vibecoding it—which it will be for a long time because you still have to maintain the software, continue to improve it, adapt to tax regulation, compliance, etc.—I don't think most companies will ditch their existing software products. Then there's the thing about agents: why wouldn't agents use software as well? An agent that tries to shop for something needs to connect to APIs, use other services, etc. That's Jensen Huang's theory—that as we have billions of agents, they will actually increase the use of software. This brings me to the last point, which is ultimately that this separation between SaaS software and AI doesn't make sense. It might make sense in the short run, but ultimately all AI is software, obviously, and all software will include some kind of AI. So, I think the so-called SASpocalypse—I find the word really hard, I just kind of mumble through it, hopefully no one will notice—is rather a buying opportunity for the good pieces of SaaS. For example, those that basically store the data for the clients, like the SAPs, the Oracles, and the Salesforces—the systems of record. It will be really hard to get those systems out of companies. In Germany, if you want to change your SAP system, you know that better than me probably, that's a two to four-year project, and usually one C-level person loses their job doing that. Only one? Yeah. I want to see the company switching from SAP to a vibecoded ERP now. Obviously, the value of SAP isn't the ugly frontend, but it's knowing the tax regimes of 210 countries in the world. You might argue that has also become a bit easier with AI to maintain, but I think especially the more complex parts of software have a good market. However, and that's a big difference, there we are with the haves and have-nots and the K-shaped economy. I think there will be fewer startups ever purchasing an SAP or growing into that stage where they would typically use an SAP. They might never need one because they will never have such a large HR department. They probably won't use SAP SuccessFactors because their organization will be 12 people, not 1,200 people. These companies that are built AI-first from scratch won't need all the processes that are only built for humans to exist. Then, you might never need an ERP. A friend of mine reported to me about her first AI use case. I asked, "What did you build?" and she automated a report that she would get every Monday to check on ad bidding or something. But the thing is, that report only exists for her. In an AI-native company, no one would ever build that report or process. That's going to be the huge advantage of companies being built right now. They will use the technology to build companies in an entirely different way: with less overhead, fewer people just doing measuring and counting, and more automation from the very beginning. You don't need to constantly think of the human in the loop; you can just build an ecosystem that works in itself. For those companies, they might never buy certain software products. But they still need a Twilio or a Cloudflare to host the stuff, so even those will continue. Maybe we've completely gone off-topic here, but that's always a sign of a good fireside chat when we can go all over the place. But what about workforce displacement in general? On that topic, it sounds like you see the average size of a business, headcount-wise, getting smaller over time as a result of AI. We certainly see that in our data. What do you think when you look at the future of the workforce and the bear cases and bull cases around what AI will do for the future of work? What's your view on it, and what are going to be the emerging areas of growth for people that will require a more human workforce? Yeah, a few theses. First, workforce displacement will be much slower than expected. Much slower, because while there are these young companies that would never hire certain people, we always underestimate the inertia—the *Beharrungskräfte*, to use the German word—of large enterprises and public enterprises. The analogy is digitalization, which has taken 20 years and is still ongoing. The same will be true for AI. Big companies will be relatively slow in adopting AI, so job displacement can't happen that fast unless startups actually take away huge parts of the market from them, which won't happen overnight either. The other thing is that this AI, automation, and robotics era couldn't hit us at a nicer time, because we have a strategic gap in the German job market of 500,000 people. That doesn't mean we are just lacking 500,000 workers today; it means we are lacking 500,000 more workers every year because of the demography of Germany. So, we need 500,000 new workers—either from immigration, or if you try reproduction, that takes a few years to build a workforce. We either need to automate stuff, or we need to let in a lot more immigrants. In that regard, if AI automates 1% of jobs per year, that's perfectly fine because those are exactly the people we need to replace anyway. I'm quite optimistic that we won't have riots or huge displacements, at least in the beginning. Apart from a very few key industries, things look stable. I wouldn't tell my daughter to become a fashion model anymore—though she probably doesn't have the genes for it anyway. On the other hand, if you look at jobs for catalog models at Otto or whatever, that is down 63% already in a few years. Customer service and photographers might be the same. But especially for developers or lawyers—Goldman Sachs said 44% of legal work could be automated in the short term, making them the second most exposed branch to AI—lawyer hirings are at an absolute high. You won't find a lot of scientific data that supports job displacement right now. The studies that you can see from Brynjolfsson at Stanford basically show that it is early. You can listen to it in the podcast tomorrow; they will explain it in more detail. That was a plug for the podcast—very subtle, very well done. No, but in short, there is a narrative that entry-level, junior jobs and early career steps are falling away faster, or that they are falling away while seniors are still being hired. I think this misses several points, like data selection bias, because the data is only pulled from small and medium-sized companies that embrace AI very early. I think what actually happens is not that companies don't choose to employ young people anymore, but that young people are not choosing these jobs anymore. You can see computer science enrollments in the US have already gone down from 660,000 to 600,000 in the last two years. I think the actual problem is not companies not wanting to hire junior people, but people coming from university, using a lot of AI, and deciding, "I will never become a software engineer or a customer service agent because that doesn't have a future." I think that's what is actually happening. Interesting. Yeah. Okay, so it's on the supply side rather than on the demand side. Okay, switching gears: agentic commerce. What's your view on agents becoming actors in a trusted ecosystem on the web where non-humans are purchasing on our behalf? How bullish or bearish are you in terms of the future outlook for it, and what use cases, if any, will be the ones that emerge first? Yeah, I think we're in the very early days, and this will also move much slower than you would think. I'm the worst keynote speaker in the world because usually people try to make these paradigm shifts and tectonic shifts look like they will happen within two or three years. But if you look at the data, the truth is that every big shift takes time. The mobile shift is still going on: 1% of people shift from desktop to mobile every year. That's not because they shift their behavior, but because older people who would never use a smartphone for certain things pass away, and young people who use a smartphone for everything enter the market. That's why it shifts by only 1% or 1.5% every year. This is how usage shifts usually happen, and that's why I think agentic commerce will be much slower. So change doesn't happen, people just— It does happen, but you will find very few technologies that penetrate a whole market in a very fast manner. WhatsApp is a good example; our grandparents are using it, and our kids are using it, so that has quickly penetrated lots of people. But on average, people are not great at fast adoption—distribution is much more important. Coming back to agentic commerce, if it addressed a natural need of humans, then first-generation tech like Alexa would have been much more successful. Use cases like ordering consumer staples, like toilet paper, were already possible with Alexa. The new LLMs are not a game-changer in terms of talking to Alexa. The payment infrastructure has become better, I admit that, but I think people just don't want to shop that way. If they wanted to shop that way, the high streets in our cities would have bells and no windows. People want window shopping. They don't want to talk to someone and say, "I want to order something." They want to window shop and compare. To make a decision on what you want to buy, you need to see the options you don't want. You want to see the luxury stuff you can't afford, the cheap stuff where you say, "I'm better than that," and then you decide on the option you love. That's why these chat interfaces right now rebuild exactly that: they basically live-craft a comparison website inside ChatGPT because that's how people want to shop. Once that has been done, people at some point might continue to use an automated way to bypass the shopping basket and have an agent do the rest. That I believe in. I think the really pesky stuff, like consumer claims—imagine your train is delayed and you have a claim against Deutsche Bahn—is perfect for this. I don't want to do that myself; I want an agent who automatically checks every plane and train travel I have and files my claims. I'm happy to share 10% of the payout just to get rid of the hassle. Today I can vibecode it myself; I don't even need to share with services like Flightright. So, consumer rights might be an area where this enters rather early. The whole services and appointments space also makes a lot of sense. Booking a table by the window at your favorite Italian restaurant, booking a treatment with a massage parlor, hairdresser, dentist, or doctor—doing that in an agentic way is highly practical. Sometimes it involves commerce; if it's treatments, there is a payment included. If it's doctors, maybe not, but we shouldn't have people sitting in doctors' offices just to answer the phone. That is the stuff I see first. Booking travel or doing larger purchases—I don't see that yet, or if so, only for a small fraction of consumers. That is so helpful. How do you think about B2B? Obviously, we are stablecoin-pilled at Stripe. We see stablecoins as a complementary rail to existing rails in terms of promoting transactions that just weren't financially viable on traditional rails. We believe there is a whole host of agentic, agent-to-agent transactions that will happen online in the future—again, a very small percentage today. What's your view on that and the degree to which that will happen in the future? I see that as much more likely. Generally, businesses are faster at adopting new technologies. You can see that with AI right now; consumer adoption is much lower compared to business adoption. Tactical spend and procurement make a lot of sense for automation. That's basically a cohort of processes made to deal with certain human limitations and compliance requirements, which is much better handled by agents. For example, I'm a keynote speaker. If I speak at a conference for Deutsche Bank, SAP, or Robert Bosch, to be on stage for 30 minutes, I have to go through a four-hour procurement process. That obviously doesn't make sense, just so they can procure my services. But all the small stuff that a company buys—tactical spend, and even larger purchases—I think that might well be agentic in the future. Maybe the transaction margins will be a bit smaller there given the high repeat rate and large volumes, but the total market size is much bigger, so for sure, for sure. Okay, let's keep going. Let's talk about Germany and the German economy. I was debating—I know I'm on dangerous ground here—asking you if there are any similarities between the state of the German football team and the state of the German economy. I wasn't sure if that was the right way to go, but let's go there and see. The only consolation I was told when mentioning the German football team was that at least the Dutch also lost. So, at least there is that. I speak as an Irish man, so I know I'm not on higher ground here; we haven't been in the World Cup since 2002, I think. Anyway, what's your perspective on the German economy and where we are right now? What role can AI play? You already alluded to it a little bit when you talked about supporting what is a gap in terms of workforce availability to support economic growth. But what are your broader perspectives on where Germany is, and what is the upside and downside case for the country? Yeah, where to start. The example with the national team might hold up quite well. This morning, my girlfriend asked me, "Haven't we been one of the best teams? When I was a child we were someone; we would be playing the best teams and sometimes we would win, and now we're not participating in the final rounds anymore." The same is true for Germany, if you will. We've been leading in a few industries—very early on in life sciences, engineering, then car manufacturing, and machinery in general. But we haven't made the transition to the next technologies in certain industries, or to new industries at all. I think maybe it's because we're too well off. Most of us are still living in a very comfortable manner. The incentive to build something new is really small in Germany because if you study at a good university and get a good grade, you can earn 80,000 or 90,000 euros working for Porsche, Mercedes-Benz, or Google. Why would you build something on your own? You can already plan when you want to buy your own home after five years of working, so why take the risk? We don't take enough risk, I think. The few people who really take enormous risks are oftentimes immigrants. You can see that much more in the US, where almost all of the pivotal companies were founded by immigrants or children of immigrants. Those are the people who change the world, and we have another mindset problem in Germany, I think. We are trained—and I studied business administration, so I'm one of these guys and can say this—to optimize. If you study business administration, you are trained to improve a process by 5% to 10% and extract a bit more productivity. But engineers are trained to build something entirely new. We need more engineers who don't think about how to extract 10% more efficiency from an existing process, but how to rebuild that process entirely, or how to build a completely new business that makes the old process redundant. If you look at the past in Germany, it has always been like that. All the big German companies—the car manufacturers, the Siemens of the world—were built by engineers. Even in the US, the ten richest people are all engineers by training, not business people. The same was true for Europe; we had that engineering DNA before. Even Bernard Arnault, the owner of LVMH and the richest person in Europe, is an engineer by training. He's a very good business person, but he is an engineer first. I think we need to stop having business guys like me consulting, advising, and optimizing stuff. We need people who build stuff from the ground up, smarter, using the newest technology. If we just use AI to transform existing companies bit by bit, that's not going to change the fate of Germany. I think we need to spend as much effort as we currently spend preserving our incumbent car industry on new business formation instead. We need to focus on building the next chapter, the next wave of companies that will use entirely different tech stacks and employ a different breed of people. I think that's the only way you can reignite growth. We talked about this backstage: EU Inc, the 28th regime, and Mario Draghi's report, which has been doing the rounds for a couple of years now. I was in Brussels recently as well, and I see a lot of re-articulation of the problem statement, but not necessarily solutioning toward the future. You referenced engineering talent and that DNA as being one key area, but if you had to rank the most critical inhibitors to Germany getting back on track as an innovation-led, founder-led country, what needs to happen first? I think we would benefit enormously from a larger digital single market. The EU tries to build a digital single market, but we still have twenty different tax regimes, languages, and company formation rules. That makes it hard. One of the biggest advantages of the US is that they have this huge domestic market of 350 to 400 million people with strong purchasing power. If we put all of Europe together, we would have the same or even more, but we are basically still very separated, small states. It would help a lot if you could easily found a company in Benelux, Denmark, or Germany, and service every European country from day one. It would also help to mobilize more capital. However, I think saying we don't have enough capital in Europe is a bit of an excuse, because if something meaningful is built here, capital arrives within milliseconds. If someone builds a truly compelling business, there are plenty of international and national investors who want a part of it. The hard truth is that we are simply not building enough exciting companies—Revolut and Stripe might be exceptions to the rule. Furthermore, getting talent into Germany needs to be much easier. Founders I invest in tell me they wish it were simpler to bring in engineers from India, the Philippines, or the former Soviet Union. Right now, the process takes months or even years. We need the best talent, but the very best minds already go to the US. If we can't even let talent from the rest of the world in, it's a big inhibitor of growth. We need foreign talent, and we should make it so that if a company vouches for someone, they can hire them within four weeks by taking on that risk. I completely agree. With Stripe Atlas, which is our incorporation service for businesses in Delaware, we see a large percentage of founders coming from outside the US. A big chunk of them come from Europe, specifically because they know they can get access to a single market overnight in a couple of hours, as opposed to days or weeks here. Looking to the future, which sectors of the German economy do you think have the best position to lead, or perhaps which historical strengths could Germany reclaim with the support of AI to become a global leader again? Are there any particular industry segments that stand out? To lay the groundwork, we need to understand what the world might look like in ten or fifteen years. If AI takes over more white-collar jobs and robotics take over more blue-collar jobs, then the two ultimate resources will be energy and raw materials like iron, copper, and coal. Robots will build themselves, and AI will build itself over time. The only thing that will matter is who has the cheapest energy and who has access to natural resources. As you know, Germany is not in the best position there; we don't have cheap energy or abundant natural resources. Even worse, the countries with the cheapest energy and the most natural resources are China and Russia. We won't win in robotics or manufacturing; that is definitely going to China. China is already producing 80% of the components that go into robots, and they can build them for less than $10,000, so they will win that space just like they won electric cars. Developing software and AI will also be hard for us. Therefore, we have to find something where we don't need massive amounts of energy or natural resources. I think biotech, life sciences, and pharmaceuticals would be a great focus because they rely mostly on great science and human capital, which we do have. We still have excellent universities. Many of the leading AI and life science researchers are actually trained in Germany. Germany was the "pharmacy of the world" in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, when companies like Bayer, BASF, and Hoechst were created. Chemicals are too energy-intensive now, so we won't get that back. But for pharmaceuticals, biotech, and life sciences, we need to build the next ten BioNtechs. We had a hundred-billion-dollar company in Germany for a few weeks with BioNtech—which, by the way, was also built by immigrants. We need another ten BioNtechs, and I think we can do that because there are so many new possibilities leveraging AI for pharmaceutical development and research. I think that's the best chance we have. We should also diversify and not bet everything on one card, but this is the most promising industry. It's also one that is threatened, of course, because Google DeepMind has their own life sciences department and is trying to build the next cancer drug as well. Okay, we're getting close to wrapping up here. I know drinks are waiting downstairs in the Expo hall for everyone. Let's do one or two last questions. Is there one startup in Germany that is potentially under the radar right now, but that you are personally excited about because they are going about it the right way and might set the world alight? Under the radar is hard. Taktile just announced a new round led by Tiger—I think they raised 130 or 160 million, so they are likely a unicorn that not many people know about. They are in the regulated financial industry, doing AI for KYC processes and so on. I think that can be very impactful. They could well become a billion-dollar company, or maybe they already are one, and they could become a decacorn at some point if they continue to serve that specific industry well. Through a friend, I actually made a tiny investment in them. Unfortunately, I already sold part of it in secondaries because the round before the previous one was done by Tiger, and I thought if Tiger is investing, I might cash in on a few of my shares. I regretted it a lot. Well, you've still got some skin in the game, though, it sounds like. Awesome. Okay, very last question, and we'll try to leave people on a high note, right? That's what you're told to do in these fireside conversations. If we are sitting here at Stripe Tour Berlin in a much, much, much, much, much, much bigger auditorium in 2030, with maybe 9,000 people in attendance, what needs to be true for Germany to be back at the forefront of the global economy—both from an AI standpoint and from a traditional industrial powerhouse standpoint? What needs to happen? It takes a lot to do it, but what will be essential, I think, is a very strong open-source ecosystem. Much of the technology we use today is open source—web servers, CMSs, databases. Not all software is commercial. Cell phone towers, Android, browsers—all of that is open source. I hope AI, at least the LLM and foundation model layers, will become open source. Right now, most of the leading open-source models are coming from China. Since we don't have a sovereign German player in LLMs, we have to hope there will be freely, publicly, and openly available models that we can employ. We can then create value by applying those open-source models to our existing industries. I think that will be super important. If not, and we become entirely dependent on US companies, I'm afraid history will repeat itself. What happened in the digital advertising market will happen again, which is not good for the tech and tax situation in Europe. So, open source is vital. I also hope we continue to shift our pension system toward being capital-based and privatized, so that people invest more in our own economy. That is good for citizens, because they can participate in business formation and capital growth, but it is also great for businesses seeking new capital. In Germany, we have been really terrible at this up until now, but a lot of work is being done right now to change that, which is great. As I said before, I hope we invest almost as much effort in fostering a new class of emerging startups as we put into preserving our existing incumbent industries. If we get that right—if we let people build the next version of this economy in parallel—then we still have a chance. I don't believe in trying to transform large, slow companies. You have to build a fast startup economy in parallel, just like has happened in the US over and over again. We still have excellent human capital; we have the smartest people. If you look at the AI research papers in the US, many of those are European names. Almost everyone in the US came from somewhere else at some point. We have the manpower, and we just have to get going and start building stuff again. We must grasp the opportunity. Exactly. Okay, awesome. Alright, Philip, with that, thank you very much again for sharing your insights. We really appreciate it. What a great way to round out Stripe Tour. Thank you very much. Thank you.

The AI Productivity Boom with David Solomon

The Economic Club of New York(13.4K)·25:33·1.1K views·2026-06-30
David SolomonChairman and CEO, Goldman Sachs Becky QuickHost, CNBC
TL;DRWhile AI will cause near-term job dislocation and require significant labor upskilling, it is poised to unleash a generational productivity and economic growth boom supported by massive capital market liquidity, even as businesses face rising cyber risks and macroeconomic pressures.

TL;DR

While AI will cause near-term job dislocation and require significant labor upskilling, it is poised to unleash a generational productivity and economic growth boom supported by massive capital market liquidity, even as businesses face rising cyber risks and macroeconomic pressures.

The AI Productivity Boom and Labor Dynamics

  • Overblown Job Apocalypse: David Solomon argues that a doomsday employment narrative is counterproductive. He anticipates a major productivity and economic growth boom over a five-to-ten-year horizon.
  • Labor Force Adaptability: The US economy is highly dynamic, historically creating and destroying an average of 30 million jobs annually.
      • Service-Driven Economy: Approximately 65% of US job creation comes from small and medium-sized enterprises, largely focused on human-to-human service roles that are less vulnerable to immediate automation.
      • White-Collar Disruption: AI will primarily disrupt white-collar roles. While this dislocation will move faster than past technological shifts, history shows the labor force can successfully adapt.
  • Shared Mitigation Responsibility: Because the pace of AI deployment is rapid, business and government have a joint responsibility to establish policies and upskilling programs to soften the transition for dislocated workers.

Enterprise AI Adoption and Infrastructure Constraints

  • Permitting and Power Bottlenecks: For data center builders, capital constraints have been replaced by regulatory hurdles, community pushback, and power infrastructure limits.
      • Solomon warns that the surge in energy demand from AI must not increase the cost of power and utility services for average Americans.
  • Non-Linear Compute Demand: Despite aggressive projections, the demand for compute will not follow a straight, compounding line. Advancements in chip efficiency, manufacturing, and distribution costs will continually alter the trajectory.
  • Cautious Enterprise Spending: Many corporations will adopt AI slower than markets expect due to high token and operational costs.
      • Margin Protection: Low-margin businesses will be highly cautious with experimental AI spend, as even fractional margin changes significantly impact their bottom line.
      • Base Model Utility: Rather than deploying expensive, high-powered frontier models, many enterprises will opt for cheaper base models to improve standard operational processes.

Capital Markets, Liquidity, and the "Greed vs. Fear" Dynamic

  • Abundant Market Liquidity: The capital required for the AI infrastructure build-out is readily available. Global equity markets hold approximately $150 trillion, US equity markets stand at $100 trillion, and US money market funds hold $8 trillion.
      • Alphabet’s Mega-Deal: Alphabet's $80 billion follow-on equity raise—the largest in history—is trading exceptionally well, proving the market's capacity to absorb massive funding rounds.
  • Greed Outpacing Fear: Solomon notes that global markets are in a phase dominated by greed over fear. His foundational advice to capital-consumptive firms is to secure funding now while capital markets remain highly robust and receptive.
  • The Cycle of Exuberance: Drawing a parallel to former Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan's 1996 "irrational exuberance" speech, Solomon reminds investors that market optimism can continue for years (as the Nasdaq did until 2000) before undergoing a major retracement.

Macroeconomic Headwinds and Escalating Cyber Risks

  • S&P 500 Concentration: Market performance is highly concentrated, with just 10 mega-cap stocks driving returns and representing over 30% of the S&P 500's market capitalization. The remaining 490 stocks have largely trended sideways.
  • Macroeconomic Pressures: Persistent inflation, rising energy prices, and supply chain friction are beginning to impact consumer wallets (with gas accounting for roughly 14% of the average American consumer's spending). This could shift consumer sentiment and slow economic growth in late 2024.
  • Cyber Security as the Top Threat: Over 60% of surveyed CEOs identify cyber security as their primary risk.
      • Systemic Banking Vulnerabilities: While major financial institutions have the capital to invest heavily in cyber defense, medium and small-sized banks lack the same capacity, creating systemic vulnerability if a breach occurs.
  • Model Security and Testing: Goldman Sachs is currently testing Anthropic's Mythos model. Solomon emphasizes the critical need for testing transparency and collaborative government guardrails to secure both proprietary and open-source models before wide deployment.

Notable Quotes

"I do not believe that we're going to have massive structural unemployment, but I do believe that AI is going to interrupt jobs and dislocate and it's moving at a very, very quick pace... But I really do believe as we get ten years out... we will have a very, very productive economy and in fact, I believe this is going to unleash a growth boom." — David Solomon
"Just because a job can be replaced doesn't mean it will be." — David Solomon
"We are definitely in a moment where there's more greed than there is fear... If you're advising companies that need capital, one of the pieces of base advice from 42 years of doing this: when capital's available, if you're capital consumptive and it's available, take the capital." — David Solomon
I'm Becky Quick of CNBC and your host of The Forum. I'll be guiding you through exclusive conversations among some of the world's global leaders, conversations previously held behind closed doors but today, we invite you in. A nonprofit 501(3), the Club is a 115-year-old platform for the conversations that help shape the future of our world. The Economic Club of New York: brightest minds, critical conversations, catalyst for innovation. David, you recently wrote an op-ed in the New York Times where you said the AI jobs apocalypse is overblown. You know, what made you write that? What kind of spurred that op-ed idea for you, and what were you hoping to really get across there? Well, you know, first of all, I'd just say that I tend, as a CEO and somebody that's running a big business, to think in five or ten-year increments. And so, I'm spending a lot of time thinking about how the world's going to unfold over the next five to ten years, and how Goldman Sachs is going to support our clients and participate in that. I think about how the evolution of the world shifts what we need to do to be successful as an enterprise. And so, you know, I think over five to ten-year increments. And I would just say I am extraordinarily excited about the opportunity, particularly for our country, to really grow productivity and grow the economy as this technology is deployed and it gets wrapped into enterprises, and, you know, helps people be more productive in the context of what they can do. That doesn't mean that it's going to be a straight line and going to be simple. In fact, I'm sure, and I stated in the op-ed, that there's going to be dislocation, as there always has been as technology is scaled and it's deployed, you know, into our economy, and as there are economic shifts over time. I decided, you know, to write an op-ed. You're putting yourself out there. There are a number of people that are talking about this, and, you know, I felt they weren't creating a constructive discussion about the gives and the gets and the balance. And I decided that it was an important discussion. There's a lot of change in the world that's happening very, very quickly, and I thought it was important for Goldman Sachs, you know, to weigh in on some of that evolution. And so, I don't believe that we're going to have massive structural unemployment, but I do believe that AI is going to interrupt jobs and dislocate, and it's moving at a very, very quick pace. We're going to have to upskill people. The labor force is going to have to adjust. But I think it's important to really step back and understand the labor force and understand the construction of the labor force in the United States when you think about these things, because then we can make good decisions as we go forward. But I really do believe as we get ten years out, just as other technologies have disrupted, we will have reasonably full employment. We will have a very, very productive economy, and in fact, I believe this is going to unleash, you know, a growth boom and a productivity boom that could be quite constructive, and I think it's really a generational thing. You know, we've seen this before with other technologies. I think one of the different things here is the pace of change, the pace at which it's moving, which can make it more dislocating. And have you seen, as a leader of a very dynamic organization with lots of people and lots of different types of jobs, have you seen AI replace any of them yet? Well, I, you know, I think, Leslie, the way you frame that, it's just a little bit, to me it's a little bit overly simplistic. Now let's start with the fact that we live in a service economy, and 65% of all the jobs that are created in this country are in this sector. This country is a very dynamic economy. Over the last 25 years on average every year, we've created and destroyed 30 million jobs. Sixty-five percent of all the job creation comes from small and medium-sized enterprises, a lot of which are service-based. There's an enormous amount of the economy that has to do with people serving people, okay, and that's not shifting, you know, anytime very quickly. So, we're talking about—what's different is we're talking about certain white-collar jobs, okay, and, you know, that's a different disruption. But we've seen disruption to other jobs that people strove for. If you look and you go back 25 to 30 years ago as we opened up the global manufacturing economy, we dislocated a number of people. I think there are a lot of things that we can learn, you know, from those dislocations, and let's hope as there's change here we find better ways, you know, to manage through some of the dislocation that will come. But the economy is very nimble, new jobs are created, new skills develop, and people will evolve appropriately. And it's just not black and white. People want black and white answers. We were talking in the back room there about writing as a career and, you know, it's, it's not going away. It's not going away. And so, it's, it's just not simple. It's much more nuanced, and I think the economy's dynamic, I think people are dynamic, and I think people, you know, will adapt, but they need time, they need help, and it's our job to figure out, you know, as that comes, how to operate. And we shouldn't be so sure about how everything plays out. There are a lot of people that are very sure; I'm very unsure. But I think the direction of travel is going to be quite exciting and quite positive when you look at it holistically over a moderate to longer-term period of time. Yeah, and one thing you mentioned in your op-ed is just because a job can be replaced doesn't mean... Doesn't mean it will be. Doesn't mean it will be. Doesn't mean it will be. We've talked a little bit about the social impact of AI, but what about kind of beyond jobs? If things got more complicated, you know, in the Middle East, of course, that could play through. You know, one of the big risks that I have talked about for years and continue to worry about, and it obviously changes with these models and it's getting more attention, is cyber risk. And, you know, when you look at—you know, there's been a lot of talk with these new models—when you look at the largest companies in the industries that are most vulnerable to this, the largest companies have been investing a lot in cyber. Think about financial services; the large banks have been investing a lot in cyber protection. That doesn't mean that we don't have vulnerabilities. But when you get into the medium-sized banks and the smaller banks, they don't have the same capacity to invest in protection. And we know that if you had a problem in a medium-sized bank, it would filter or create fear, you know, in other institutions. We've seen that at other points in history. So I think cyber risk is a, is a big risk. The Conference Board actually did a survey in the last couple weeks where they asked a broad group of CEOs, "What is the number one thing that you worry about from a risk factor?" and cyber, 60-some percent, you know, put cyber as a significant risk. So that's something that's getting a lot of attention. You could have a cyber incident that could change confidence and could shift the way we look at markets or the growth trajectory of the economy. There are a lot of things out there. But you also have to ask: instead of what can possibly go wrong, what could possibly go right? And at the moment, there's a lot from a market perspective that's possibly going right. And in the distribution of outcomes, I think there are more distributions where, given the technology investment, this continues to go for a period of time. But, you know, we're at Goldman Sachs, you know, on guard, humbly focused on, you know, all the different distributions, trying to talk to our clients as much as we can about things we think they have to think about in what's become an ever more complicated landscape. And you've been one of the firms that's testing Anthropic's Mythos model, right? What have you learned from that, and especially in the context of the future of cybersecurity and the vulnerability of the system? They're very, they're very, very powerful models. You know, what I, what I think I can say about it is there are a large group of companies that are doing a lot of testing and, you know, I think it's important that that testing's done. I think it's important that the information gets shared more broadly so that other companies in the economy can learn. It's one of the things a number of us have been talking about in Washington: how do we get the information that the 40 or so companies have gleaned broadly disseminated to other companies and other enterprises to create more, you know, more protection. But a lot of this is going to be correlated to kind of consistent long-term investment in cyber protection, and obviously, bigger companies do better with that and as you move down the chain, people are more vulnerable. And so I do think—I heard that the President signed an executive order that was a little bit, you know, I haven't had a chance to really read it but it was a little bit watered down. It talked about a voluntary 30-day, you know, process for testing these models. I think we want transparency and information and protection before this stuff goes out. But we also have to understand there are open source models that are all over the place, and they have the same capacity very quickly to do the same things. And so, you know, this is a place where I do think thinking thoughtfully about guardrails and how, you know, these companies that are producing this can work with the government and the government can help create some guardrails is something we should be thinking a lot about. But to your point about open source, I mean, you just went to China with the President. We can set guardrails here in the US, but there are other countries, China, working on these models as well. How concerned are you about, you know, the ability to set the guardrails and really have them protect the system? You know, it's all, everything's just, everything's Leslie, kind of, you know, a matter of degrees. You know, at the end of the day, the models exist and the models, when in the wrong hands, okay, can do bad things. There are bad actors in the world, there have been bad actors in the world, and there are going to continue to be bad actors in the world. What we have to do is try to do everything we can, you know, to protect against that. And it's not a straight line, and it's not perfect, and it's not, and it's not easy. I'm curious: as you kind of reflect on your tenure as CEO, you've seen a transformation of the firm leaning into consumer, pulling back from consumer, you spearheaded the firm through COVID, and now you've got this generational shift with AI and technology. What's been the most surprising thing for you in holding this position? It's always something. I mean, the thing is, I'm eight years in, you know, I woke up at the beginning of this year and I said, "This is going to be eight years in; this is going to be the first normal year where we're going to just run the business." Okay. Well, so much for that. A war pops in, and I mean, it, it's such a dynamic world. We run a big, complicated global business. We have extraordinary people, you know, all over the world. You know, there's, there are always things going on; it's always changing. But our job, my job as the CEO of the firm, is to set a strategic plan to grow the firm at the core things that we do. We serve clients, we want to do it at the highest level of excellence that we possibly can, and we want to do it in a differentiated way compared to people that we compete with. And we have a responsibility because we have, we have $110 billion of other people's capital. We have a responsibility to drive good returns on that capital. And it's very hard to be a public company and drive reasonable returns on the capital that you control if you don't grow your business. If you don't grow your business, it's very, very hard. And so I think one of the things we're most proud of is that we've really invested in growing the firm, and growing our client franchise, and growing our business. And we've successfully been executing on that. It has not been perfect. It has not been a straight line. I think one of the most powerful things we did is that there were things that were really working and there were things that weren't, and we were willing to say, "Not working; we're going to pivot, we're going to change." And you know, I think when you do that, you know, you gain more credibility with your people, with investors, etc. And so we're making good progress. But it's an incredible firm. I feel incredibly lucky to have a great leadership team around me where we're stewarding it together and, you know, I'm excited about the world. There are lots of problems and I wring my hands over problems and I'm concerned about a lot of things going on in the world, but I think the glass is half full, and, you know, we have an incredible resilience and ability, you know, to grow, to do more, to do better, you know, when you start thinking about 10, 20, 30 year periods. Opening keynote | AI Now Summit 2026

Mistral(15.1K)·42:44·1.1K views·2026-06-30
Arthur MenschCo-founder & CEO, Mistral AI Guillaume LampleCo-founder, Mistral AI Timothée LacroixCo-founder, Mistral AI
TL;DRAt its inaugural keynote, Mistral AI announced a fully verticalized enterprise strategy spanning proprietary GPU data centers, advanced agentic software ("Vibe"), and physics-informed models designed to give organizations full control and maximum ROI on their AI transformations.

TL;DR

At its inaugural keynote, Mistral AI announced a fully verticalized enterprise strategy spanning proprietary GPU data centers, advanced agentic software ("Vibe"), and physics-informed models designed to give organizations full control and maximum ROI on their AI transformations.

Strategic Vision: Full-Stack Ownership & Deep Verticals

Arthur Mensch (Co-Founder & CEO) outlined the core beliefs driving Mistral AI’s roadmap:

  • Full-Stack Ownership: Deploying AI successfully at the enterprise level requires controlling the complete stack—from underlying compute, chips, and models up to custom business applications.
  • Vertical Integration: Rather than acting purely as a horizontal technology provider, Mistral is building specialized, domain-focused solutions to solve complex physical, financial, and sovereign challenges.

Infrastructure and Compute Modernization

Timothée Lacroix (Co-Founder & CTO) introduced major advancements in Mistral's physical and serverless infrastructure aimed at addressing global hardware supply constraints:

  • Bruyères-le-Châtel Data Center: Built in partnership with Eclairion near Paris, this 40-megawatt (MW) facility has been active since early 2026 and will be fully operational by the end of the summer.
  • Borlänge Facility (Sweden): Scheduled for development throughout 2027, this site will host NVIDIA's next-generation "Vera Rubin" architecture to pioneer advancements in processing efficiency and speed.
  • Inference-Dedicated Expansion: A new 10 MW facility in southern Paris will go live this summer specifically to host inference workloads, securing dedicated capacity for enterprise clients.
  • Koyeb Integration: Following its acquisition, serverless provider Koyeb is fully integrated into Mistral, offering developers a seamless environment to host Model Context Protocol (MCP) connections, sandboxes, and GPU runtimes.

Next-Generation Models and the Resurgence of Efficiency

Guillaume Lample (Co-Founder & Chief Scientist) detailed Mistral’s ongoing commitment to open-source, highly compressed, and multimodal models:

  • The Value of Customization: Fine-tuning open models on proprietary corporate data delivers significant performance leaps over general, closed-source models which lack company-specific context.
  • Small & Agent-Ready Models: While once unfashionable, small and highly compressed models are seeing a massive resurgence because multi-step agentic workflows are incredibly token-hungry and require high execution speeds.
  • Key Product Updates:
      • Mistral Medium 3.5: Natively integrates previous specialized releases—including vision (Pixtral), reasoning (Magistral), and coding (Devstral)—along with advanced agentic search capabilities that connect to enterprise files (Google Drive, Slack, Notion) while drastically reducing hallucination rates.
      • Mistral OCR: A highly specialized 1-billion-parameter (1B) model capable of transcribing thousands of complex document pages per minute on a single GPU—outperforming massive models in both speed and cost-efficiency.
      • Voxtral Ecosystem: Moving beyond Automatic Speech Recognition (ASR) to Text-to-Speech (TTS) with voice cloning. A real-time, duplex voice-to-voice model will launch within the next two months.
      • Mistral Large 4: Scheduled to launch by summer 2026, featuring dedicated optimizations for cyber defense and industrial engineering.
      • Multilingual Expansion: Upcoming foundation models will support over 200 distinct languages.

Enterprise Platforms: Vibe, Studio, and Forge

Mistral announced a cohesive product suite designed to bridge frontier models with day-to-day enterprise operations:

  • The Vibe Family: Transitioning the consumer chatbot "le Chat" into an agentic system.
      • Vibe for Work: A web application allowing workers to schedule daily routines, automate inbox summaries, and execute complex workflows.
      • Vibe for Code: Launching on the web and as a VS Code extension, allowing developers to run and monitor dozens of concurrent code-generation and debugging sessions on any device.
  • Studio: The centralized governance portal where enterprise administrators control API connections, segment data layers from inference layers, and deploy models on-premise.
  • Forge: A localized tuning tool installed directly on customer infrastructure to adapt foundation models to highly specific company niches, legacy Domain Specific Languages (DSLs), and custom sensor modalities.

Deep Industry Partnerships and Case Studies

Arthur Mensch highlighted concrete outcomes across three primary target sectors:

1. Industrial Engineering & Physical AI

To empower R&D engineers working on physical designs, Mistral acquired Emmi AI, a company specialized in training models to understand physical laws and simulate real-world system behavior (e.g., computational fluid dynamics and molecular dynamics).

  • ASML: Co-developed custom agents and diagnostics engines, achieving a 120x faster diagnostics loop with the same precision as human teams.
  • Airbus & BMW: Partnerships established across engineering, design, and manufacturing divisions, specifically using physical AI to accelerate complex simulations like car crash testing at BMW.

2. Financial Services

  • BNP Paribas: Partnered with Mistral to deploy on-premise models, launching custom compliance (KYC) agents in Belgium that reduced incomplete compliance files from 80% to just 10%, cutting processing times from weeks to days. Their LLM platform has already scaled to 65,000 corporate and institutional banking users.
  • HSBC & Abanca: Deploying on-premise systems for secure, proprietary trade floor and portfolio decision-making.

3. Sovereign AI & Public Sector

Mistral is actively partnering with international governments—including Luxembourg, Singapore, France, Slovakia, Greece, and Morocco—to build citizens' services that respect local laws, cultural nuances, and regional languages (such as Darija and Amazigh). Additionally, Amazon is partnering with Mistral to handle high-quality non-English queries for Alexa+.

"One of the reasons we've been doing all of this and developing all of these data center capacities is to secure compute capacity not only for ourselves, but also for our customers." — *Timothée Lacroix*

And with this, I'll leave it to Guillaume to tell you the latest on our models. Thank you, Timothée. Hi everyone, I'm Guillaume. Thank you for being here today. I'm going to talk a little bit about our models—what we have built so far, and also what's coming next. Every time we train models, we have a lot of things we care a lot about. One of them, for instance, is open source, as Arthur mentioned before. We have been releasing the weights of pretty much all of our models so far. It was something we have been doing since day one, since the release of our first model, Mistral 7B. It was also something we were doing even before Mistral. This mission is really important to us. It's one of the missions of the company to give AI to everyone, putting AI into the hands of everyone so that everybody can benefit from its power. And one nice thing you can do if you release the weights of your model, if you open source your model, is that you can customize them. You can take your model and you can train it on all your company's data. Sometimes, if you have a company that is several years old or potentially has been there for decades, you have a tremendous amount of information that you have been collecting through the years. What people don't realize before they do it is how much better a model can be if you fine-tune it—if you basically continue the pre-training of this model on the data of your company. It makes a very big difference compared to an off-the-shelf closed model that is going to be the same model used by millions of people. The issue if you use a closed model is that every time you start a new conversation with it, or every time you start a new workflow, this model will know nothing about your company. So, you will have to give it all the context every single time. It's going to work—these models are smart—but it's going to be very inefficient. While if you train your model on all your company's data, in a sense, the model will be trained on potentially millions of documents and it's going to know your company better than literally anyone else in the company. These models can encompass and integrate a huge amount of information. They will know the company very well and be very efficient. That's really something that we encourage people to do, and that we do with many of our customers because there are massive gains when you actually do this customization and fine-tuning. Something related to this is also having small and efficient models. Since the very beginning, we have worked on compressing the intelligence into our models as much as possible—trying to get a model as small as possible but as good as possible. When we released Mistral 7B, it was the best open-source model of this size, better than much larger models. When we released Mistral 8x7B, it was the best open-source model, much better than much larger models as well. So, making small and efficient models has always been a focus for us. It was not that important a couple of years ago. If you remember, people were saying back then that models don't need to be this fast because they were only using them for chatbots or multi-turn instruction-following conversations. But today, the situation is very different. Today, we are building these agentic workflows. These models are running in the background, doing a lot of actions and a lot of tool calls, so they are extremely token-hungry—much more than before. So, what we are seeing today is actually a comeback of these small and efficient models. It was not something people were interested in before, but now it's very different because of these new workflows that we are seeing today and these agentic capabilities. Another thing that we really care a lot about is multilingual capabilities. Usually, what we have seen is that people train models on the languages they speak, the languages in the country they come from. For instance, a US model would be very English-centric, and one in China would be trained on English and Chinese. For us, we really try to train our models on as many languages as possible since the very beginning. The first Mistral models were trained on dozens of languages. The next ones that we are going to release are trained on more than 200 languages—basically, every language our classifier can identify. And this is really important; it makes a big difference for many of our customers. It's also cheaper if you actually take this into account from the very beginning. Because if you are dealing with a new language, a language that the model has not been trained on, not only is it not going to be as good, but also it's going to be very inefficient. Your tokenizer is not going to be used to this language, so you will need a lot of tokens to process a very small amount of text. So yes, we have always put a lot of energy into basically making sure our models are really good in all European languages, Asian languages, and so on. The first models we released were text-only models, so they were fairly simple. Models a couple of years ago were much simpler compared to what we have today. There were no agentic workflows, and there was no multimodality. The first modality we added was giving the models the ability to process images, which is very important when you want to process research papers, academic documents, or industrial papers with a lot of figures, complex diagrams, and so on. Or even if you want to have your model be able to process slides and read them. Sometimes you cannot linearize the information into text, so you really want the model to be able to read this information. So now, all our models are natively multimodal. An example of a model that is multimodal, very small, and efficient is Mistral OCR. It's a model that is very simple: it does only one thing. It takes a document and converts it into a list of text and images that you can feed into your large models. When we actually do this fine-tuning of models on top of our customers' documents—when they have millions of PDF files or Word documents they want to train the model on—the first thing we have to do is convert all of this into a format the model can read. And this is where we use Mistral OCR. It's a very small model, with 1 billion parameters. It can process thousands of pages per minute on a single GPU. Here, if you work with some of our competitors, what they will recommend you do is actually use a large model, asking it, "Can you transcribe this document?" It's going to work—it's not a difficult task—but it's going to be extremely inefficient. You will be using a model that is composed of hundreds of billions of parameters. Here, it's a 1B model, so it is extremely small and extremely fast, and it makes a big difference. Another example of a modality we added, and a small model as well, is Voxtral. It was our first audio model, and we released the first version last year. Initially, it was just an ASR model that could do automatic speech recognition, transcribing at 24B, which was really good. A few months after this, we released a model that is a 3B model—ten times smaller, but also able to process more languages. We also added a lot of features important for many of our customers, such as language identification and speaker diarization, so you can know who is saying what at which moment. And yes, we also added some real-time capability. Now, you don't have to wait for the end of a speech to be able to process it; you can process it on the fly. The transcription will appear as you speak, which is also useful if you want to do some live translation, for instance. A few months ago, we released our latest speech model, Voxtral TTS, which is a model that can generate speech. It takes text as input and generates the voice. It can take any voice that you want, and you can even clone your own voice. It's really efficient, and now that we have done all of these different things—speech in, speech out, and real-time—we are combining all of these capabilities into a single model that we are going to release in a couple of months. It's going to be our final Voxtral, which will be a duplex model that you can talk to, and it will talk back to you in real-time. Here is another example of a model, Mistral Medium 3.5, that is a combination of many different models and capabilities. As I mentioned before, we had a multimodal model called Pixtral. Pixtral no longer exists; it was a model that was really good at processing images, but now all our models are natively multimodal. We no longer have a separate model for images because it is going to be part of all of our models. There is another model that we released one year ago called Magistral. It was a model really good at doing reasoning, such as processing complex math questions or physics questions. Now, we no longer have Magistral; this model is deprecated because all our models will natively be doing reasoning. We also had a Devstral model, which was a model for developers, really good at coding, writing code, writing PRs, finding issues, and so on. Here, we did the same: we integrated these coding capabilities into Mistral Medium 3.5, so we no longer have a Devstral model. The latest thing that we integrated is the agentic search capability, which basically gives the model the ability to do search in very complex environments. For instance, if you deploy Mistral Medium in an enterprise and ask a question like, "Can you summarize this topic?" or "Tell me information about this or that," what the model can do is use API calls to search for information on Google Drive, Slack, Notion, or wherever it might be—even potentially in your emails. This is also why it's good if it's on-premise or on your own server, so you don't have to worry about confidentiality and privacy. This model will be really good at identifying if the information is there. If not, it will search further—sometimes searching for maybe ten minutes and doing dozens and dozens of tool calls. What we found is that by training the model to do this, we also reduced hallucinations a lot because the model is trained to detect if it found the solution or not. If it didn't find the solution, it will tell you that it doesn't have it. Reducing hallucinations is very important if you want to deploy this model in specific verticals and industries. Now, we are continuing to add capabilities to all our Mistral models. We are working now on the next large version: Mistral Large 4 will potentially be there in a couple of months, at most during the summer. We are adding many capabilities, for instance, in industrial applications such as fluid dynamics, computational chemistry, and computer-aided design. We are also adding cyber defense, cyber attack capabilities, and even finance. Yes, a lot of things are coming soon that we'll be able to talk about more in a couple of months. And now, I'm going to give the floor back to Timothée, who can tell you a bit more about the products powered by our models. Thank you, Guillaume. It was then the face of the Vibe CLI, hanging around in your command line interface.
