"I've Never Said This In An Interview Before" | JD Vance On Faith, Iran & AI

Michael Knowles(2.6M)·64:33·110.1K views·2026-06-30
JD VanceVice President Michael KnowlesHost, Michael Knowles
TL;DRVice President JD Vance discusses his spiritual journey from atheism to Catholicism, outlines his philosophical critique of meritocracy, and explains how modern conservatism is shifting from Friedmanite laissez-faire capitalism to a Hamiltonian…
economic model that...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance discusses his spiritual journey from atheism to Catholicism, outlines his philosophical critique of meritocracy, and explains how modern conservatism is shifting from Friedmanite laissez-faire capitalism to a Hamiltonian economic model that prioritizes family and human dignity.

***

JD Vance’s Spiritual Journey and Return to Faith

  • From Pentecostal Roots to Atheism: Raised primarily by his grandmother ("Mamaw") in an unchurched household with Pentecostal influences, Vance lost his connection to Christianity when his grandmother died in 2005. By the time he was at Yale Law School, he identified as an atheist.
  • The Search for Virtue: Despite achieving elite meritocratic success, Vance found himself unfulfilled and felt he was becoming a "bad person." His relationship with his future wife, Usha Vance, reoriented him toward character, marriage, and fatherhood.
  • The Appeal of Catholicism:
      • Institutional Stability: Vance was drawn to the unchanging nature of Catholic Mass and doctrine across geographic and cultural divides, contrasting it with his unstable, unchurched childhood.
      • Generational Continuity: He felt the Catholic Church’s historical depth was an antidote to the existential dread of cultural decay and societal stasis.
  • Providential Moments ("The Touch of God"):
      • The Shattered Glass: During a 2019 theological debate with conservative writer Ross Douthat in a hotel basement, a wine glass inexplicably jumped off a shelf and shattered, which Vance interpreted as a divine sign.
      • The Confession Green Lights: Waking up late for a 7:15 AM confession at St. Cecilia's Church in Cincinnati's Oakley neighborhood, Vance caught every single traffic light green on a normally 15-minute drive, completing it in eight minutes.
  • Secular Echoes of Liturgy: Vance and host Michael Knowles observe that secular progressives mimic Catholic rituals without God or grace:
      • Nicene Creed: Progressive yard signs (e.g., "In this house, we believe...") mimic the cadence and function of the Creed.
      • Confession: Modern therapy acts as a secular confessional, though it lacks the concepts of guilt, forgiveness, and personal agency.
      • Baptism: The transgender transition process, including the concept of "dead names," functions as a secular form of baptism.

The Failure of Meritocracy and Modern Ambition

  • Ambition vs. Mimetic Desire: Drawing on 20th-century Catholic writer René Girard, Vance argues that modern meritocracy warps healthy ambition (the desire to build great things) into the vice of "ambition for ambition's sake" (the desire to simply get ahead of other people).
  • The Devaluation of Relationships: In elite spaces like Yale Law School, individuals are conditioned to brag about credentials and corporate jobs rather than their spouses, children, or friendships.
  • The Illusion of Corporate Liberation: Vance criticizes modern corporate feminism for internalizing exploitative capitalist standards.
      • Goldman Sachs Cubicles: He argues that defining personal liberation as working 80 hours a week in a corporate cubicle is a civilizational scam that opposes basic human well-being.

The Hamiltonian Realignment of Conservative Economics

  • The Populist Shift: Vance argues that the economic baseline of the Republican Party has permanently shifted under Donald Trump. Policies like tariffs and strict immigration restrictions to protect native worker wages are now mainstream GOP consensus.
  • Hamiltonian Developmentalism: Vance predicts that 21st-century conservative economics will reject Milton Friedman's laissez-faire model in favor of Alexander Hamilton's developmentalist approach, which utilizes state-supported infrastructure to foster national industry.
  • Servicing Human Dignity: Under this framework, economic success is measured not by pure GDP growth, but by how easily a citizen can raise a family, earn a living wage, and participate in their community.
  • The Loss of Christian Guardrails: Vance notes that laissez-faire capitalism only worked in the 1980s because it operated within a society protected by robust Christian institutions. Globalized liberalism has since hollowed out those guardrails.
  • Historical Precedents:
      • Margaret Thatcher: While praising Thatcher's historical significance, Vance argues her policies ultimately dismantled the local, family-owned shops and communities she intended to preserve.
      • Brent Bozell: Vance highlights Brent Bozell (who ghostwrote *Conscience of a Conservative*) as one of the most predictive, unsung thinkers of 20th-century conservatism, who foresaw the limitations of fusing pure libertarianism with social conservatism.

Middle East Foreign Policy and Strategy on Iran

  • The Trump "Reshuffle" Strategy: Vance describes the administration's foreign policy approach as "reshuffling the deck" to actively identify pressure points and exploit diplomatic leverage.
  • A Weakened Iran: Due to shifting economic and military pressures, Iran's regional influence is weaker than in previous months, prompting direct, technical talks between the U.S. and Iran.
  • Lebanon-Israel Peace Dynamics: Vance notes that a potential peace deal between Lebanon and Israel, paired with a U.S.-Iran Memorandum of Understanding (MoU), relies on fundamentally respecting Lebanon's territorial integrity.
  • US Diplomatic Goals:
      • Denuclearization: Establishing durable, verifiable, and inspected commitments from Iran to completely dismantle its nuclear program.
      • Multilateral Stability: Creating a lasting regional framework that harmonizes interests between the U.S., Iran, Israel, Lebanon, and the Gulf Cooperation Council (GCC).
      • Strait of Hormuz: Resolving the highly disputed transit status of the Strait of Hormuz, where Iran and Oman have attempted to enforce unrecognized maritime tolls.

***

Notable Quotes

"What I have been afraid of... is this idea that the continuity across generations is going to stop with us. That we inherited something, and instead of building upon it and passing it on, it's just going to die with us." — JD Vance
"What a meritocracy in 21st-century America has done, is taught people to want to be better than everyone else... If you're orienting yourself not to some objective truth, but to how other people are performing and behaving, you're not your own person, and you're certainly not God's person." — JD Vance
"If you think that it is liberating for you to sit in a cubicle at Goldman Sachs, you have been had, and we all got to just admit that before we can make any progress as a civilization." — JD Vance
"The economy is a tool to service the dignity of the human person. If a set of economic policies make it easier for a person to raise a family, to earn a living wage, to give back to their community... that is the sort of thing that we want to be supportive of." — JD Vance

Well, I think a couple of things. I mean, first of all, I think that, you know, I think Aristotle once said that virtue lies in the mean. In fact, I think that if you're ambitious because you want to build, you know, a rocket that goes to the Moon, right? Ambition not to build something beautiful, but to get ahead of other people. Ambition not to make an amazing product, but ambition to make a lot of money for money's sake. I think that when it becomes warping and disorienting is when it becomes ambition for ambition's sake. And what a meritocracy—I think the modern meritocracy in 21st-century America has done—is taught people to want to be better than everyone else. And I think that has two really, really big problems. First of all, if you're orienting yourself not to some objective truth, but to how other people are performing and behaving, you're not your own person, and you're certainly not God's person. You're fundamentally following the crowd. This is like an insight from Rene Girard. So, at some level, meritocracy is fundamentally derivative of other people. That's a bad thing. Sameer Samat on Android 17 and the Future of Intelligent Computing

Google for Developers(2.7M)·40:10·2.1K views·2026-06-30
Sameer SamatPresident of Android, Google Logan KilpatrickHost
TL;DRGoogle is transitioning Android from a traditional operating system into an "intelligence system" driven by user intent rather than manual inputs.
This shift is headlined by the upcoming summer release of the Gemini Intelligence suite and multimodal, vision-enabled AI...

TL;DR

Google is transitioning Android from a traditional operating system into an "intelligence system" driven by user intent rather than manual inputs. This shift is headlined by the upcoming summer release of the Gemini Intelligence suite and multimodal, vision-enabled AI integration in vehicles like the unreleased Volvo EX60.

Shifting from Operating Systems to Intelligence Systems

  • The "Micromanager" Constraint: For over 40 years, computing has forced users to act as micromanagers—breaking down high-level goals into discrete command-line or GUI inputs.
  • The Intelligence Paradigm: Google is steering Android toward a system where users simply articulate a goal, and the platform manages the context, resources, and execution to deliver the outcome.
  • Focusing on Benefits Over "AI" Hype:
      • During the recent Android Show, Google purposely avoided using the word "AI" to prevent consumer alienation.
      • Features like *Circle to Search* succeeded rapidly because marketing focused entirely on user utility (e.g., identifying clothing or landmarks on-screen) rather than the complex machine vision powering it.
  • On-Device Safety and Performance:
      • Scam Detection: Android features an on-device Google DeepMind (GDM) model that can listen to calls from unknown numbers, recognize requests a bank would never make, and alert the user to hang up.
      • Tiered Optimization: Google targets premium flagship hardware to push the boundaries of system resources, subsequently optimizing and "flowing down" these capabilities to lower-end devices via server-side support or specialized lightweight models.

Gemini Intelligence: Summer Feature Suite

Google introduced *Gemini Intelligence*, a suite of premium features coming to high-end Android devices:

  • Rambler (Voice Dictation):
      • An upgraded keyboard feature that allows users to verbally "ramble."
      • Instead of literal transcription, it instantly distills messy, conversational audio (removing "ums" and "ahs") into a polished, structured text message or document.
  • App Automation:
      • Users can ask Gemini to execute complex workflows inside third-party apps (e.g., compiling an Instacart shopping cart based on a barbecue guest list).
      • Virtual Window System: For privacy and security, Android runs automated tasks in a containerized, virtual environment. The AI cannot access external apps, and the process pauses before committing any financial transaction.
  • Super Fill:
      • An evolution of Autofill that securely retrieves complex personal data (such as passport numbers, driver's licenses, or license plates) from Google Photos or Gmail to instantly populate mobile forms.
  • Generative Widgets:
      • Enables users to prompt Gemini to build custom UI elements on the fly (e.g., a real-time F1 lap time tracker pulled from public web sources).
  • Android Halo:
      • A dedicated location in the status bar where AI agents running long-running background tasks can seamlessly update the user or request necessary feedback.

The Future of Apps: Experiential vs. Transactional

  • Debunking the "App-pocalypse": Sameer Samat argues that the rise of agentic AI will not make apps obsolete, though it will reshape their architecture:
      • Experiential Apps: Users spend 70% to 80% of their mobile time in experiential spaces (e.g., YouTube, TikTok, gaming, messaging). These apps will remain highly manual and immersive, as users do not want agents consuming entertainment on their behalf.
      • Transactional Apps: Apps focused on utility (e.g., booking flights, ordering food) will bifurcate. They will offer a rich "experiential" mode for deep user engagement alongside a background "agentic" mode optimized to answer machine-to-machine queries.

Multimodal Mobility: Gemini in the Volvo EX60

In a live demonstration inside the unreleased Volvo EX60 featuring Android-powered Google built-in systems, Samat showcased deep AI integration on the road:

  • Direct Infotainment Integration: Gemini features high-level control over the car's native software, modifying cabin climate or turning on lane-keeping assist via conversational voice commands.
  • Multimodal Vision Capabilities:
      • With user permission, Gemini can access the car's front-facing cameras via local buffers.
      • In the demo, Gemini successfully identified real-world landmarks outside the vehicle—including Google's "Gradient Canopy" solar-roof building, "The Orb" public art installation, and the Shoreline Amphitheatre—explaining their history in real-time.

"What we have an opportunity to go from is operating systems to intelligence systems... an intelligence system is one that you articulate your goal and the system's job is of course managing resources but also managing context and figuring out how to take your goal... and produce the action that you want." — Sameer Samat, President of Android at Google
"I think it's really important we've learned to not talk about the tech as the lead-in but rather to talk about the benefit... We didn't say the word AI once in the show. A lot of the features are definitely AI-powered. And I think the reception was better that way because people are like, 'Okay, you're actually telling me why this is good and helpful in my life.'" — Sameer Samat, President of Android at Google
"I don't think apps go away... I don't think that we want our agents to be watching the TikTok or the YouTube for us. I think users still want to kick back and do some of that." — Sameer Samat, President of Android at Google

What we've been doing in computing for, like, the last 40-plus years is we have created systems that put the user in the role of the micromanager of the system. It's—but someone has to build that widget for you. Yeah. Our concept here is: what if you could just say to Gemini, like, "I want to see what the latest lap times are for this—for the F1 race that was yesterday, and I want you to keep that updated, you know, build me that widget," right? And no one's maybe built that one, and it'll go off and crank for a while and then basically give you a widget that's exactly that, pulling from publicly available sources of information. And I think it's the beginning of generative UI for your operating system, you know? I think that evolves over time where more of the UI is built for you on the fly. I love that. I feel like—we have this—I—I don't—you—I don't even know who has the saying anymore, but Android is the platform for builders. What might go here?" and then it tries to like retrieve a bunch of stuff? Like, if you have that DMV registration in your email where it gave you—I search my pictures every time to try to find it, you know? And so, like, a good example of this is—like, let's say you—you're parking somewhere, and the parking app that—you know, the QR code that you scan in that—in that parking lot is, like, "enter your car's license plate," and you're already walking out of the parking lot. You're like, "Oh, man," you know? And so you're either opening up photos or Gmail or whatever, and you're searching for it. Now, it detects that and, like, it will tell you to fill that. So all that's coming this summer. We have a system which has gone and retrieved those entities; it has them pre-ready, if you will, in a—in a centralized repository. When the system on the device detects that you're looking at a form which has the need for one of those entities, it will make a secure request to pull that information and then present it to you in the keyboard and say, "Hey, you know, do you—do you want to autofill this in?" and if you do, then it—it goes and grabs it and puts it in the field for you. Why Hardware-Software Co-Design Is AI's Real 100x: Dylan Patel of SemiAnalysis

Sequoia Capital(227.0K)·70:15·30.8K views·2026-06-30
Dylan PatelFounder, SemiAnalysis
TL;DRThe most significant gains in AI efficiency do not come from faster silicon alone, but from hardware-software co-design—the simultaneous optimization of models, software kernels, and hardware architectures—which is driving an unprecedented 60x annual reduction in the cost of AI quality.

TL;DR

The most significant gains in AI efficiency do not come from faster silicon alone, but from hardware-software co-design—the simultaneous optimization of models, software kernels, and hardware architectures—which is driving an unprecedented 60x annual reduction in the cost of AI quality.

## The Power of Hardware-Software Co-Design

  • The Multiplicative 100x Effect: Isolating optimizations to raw silicon, software, or model layers yields modest ~2x gains at each step. By co-designing across all three layers, the improvements compound multiplicatively, resulting in 100x leaps in overall performance and efficiency.
  • Silicon Matchmaking: A model's architecture is deeply tied to the silicon it was trained on.
      • DeepSeek's Hopper Optimization: The shapes of the experts in DeepSeek v3 were engineered specifically for Nvidia's Hopper architecture (and v4 is optimized for Blackwell and Huawei silicon). As a result, TPUs perform poorly when running DeepSeek.
      • The TPU Ecosystem: Conversely, Google's Gemini and Anthropic's Claude are optimized for Google's TPUs on the pre-training side, allowing them to achieve massive efficiencies that do not translate well to standard Nvidia GPUs.
  • Architectural Divergence: Top labs are diverging in their model architectures. OpenAI utilizes highly sparse models, while Anthropic relies on denser structures. These architectural choices dictate distinct network topologies and hardware requirements.

## InferenceX: Demystifying Performance and Cost

  • Living Benchmarks vs. Static Audits: Traditional, point-in-time benchmarking is obsolete because the AI software stack (e.g., PyTorch, vLLM, SG-Lang) updates twice a week. SemiAnalysis founded *InferenceX*—a live, automated benchmarking platform utilizing over $50 million in donated hardware (expanding to over $100 million) across 15 different chip types.
  • The Key Trade-Off: The fundamental metric of inference performance is the throughput-interactivity curve (latency vs. batch size).
      • High Latency/High Throughput: Batch-processing thousands of documents at once maximizes hardware utilization and cuts costs by roughly 4x.
      • Low Latency/Low Throughput: Real-time conversational applications require low batch sizes and techniques like speculative decoding, which are highly expensive but necessary for instant user feedback.
  • The 60x Annual Drop: Continuous benchmarking shows that the cost of serving a model of equivalent quality is dropping by roughly 60x annually, alongside a 40x annual increase in intelligence-per-watt.

## Mega-Buildouts and the Realities of Infrastructure

  • The Myth of the Plateau: Critics claiming that AI progress has hit a wall or lacks ROI are misinterpreting saturated benchmarks. While older benchmarks are maxed out, new tests show rapid, ongoing capability growth. The economic value of the tasks these models can perform is expanding faster than new compute can be built.
  • Sizing the Power Crunch: Dylan forecasts massive power demands by the end of the decade.
      • 2030 Projections: OpenAI and Anthropic alone will require over 100 gigawatts of combined power.
      • 2040 Projections: Total AI compute requirements will scale into the terawatts.
  • Space-Based Data Centers: While space data centers will represent less than 1% of incremental compute by 2030, the sheer difficulty of securing terrestrial land, permits, and power could push more than 50% of incremental compute growth into space by 2040.

## The Silicon Duopoly and Nvidia's Multipolar Strategy

  • The Fall of the CUDA Moat: The traditional belief that Nvidia's software ecosystem (CUDA) is an impenetrable barrier is fading. Modern frontier models (like Claude and Codex) are highly proficient at writing custom, low-level kernels for non-Nvidia hardware.
  • The New Moat (Upstream Optimization): The real moat has shifted to the model layer; open-source models are pre-optimized for Nvidia GPU shapes. If a developer wants to run an open-source model out of the box, they are effectively forced to use Nvidia hardware.
  • Jensen Huang's Multipolar Strategy: Nvidia's CEO actively funds and allocates GPU supply to "neoclouds" (e.g., CoreWeave, Crusoe) and independent AI labs. He wants to prevent a consolidated future where three hyperscalers (Microsoft, Google, Amazon) control the entire market and replace Nvidia GPUs with their own custom ASICs (TPUs/Trainium).
  • The Neocloud Advantage: Neoclouds have seized market share from traditional hyperscalers because:
      • Performance Optimization: Hyperscalers optimized their networks for CPU tenant isolation and security, which actively degrades GPU cluster performance.
      • Execution Speed: Neoclouds operate with hyper-levered equity structures, highly incentivizing them to build physical data centers faster than massive corporate hyperscalers.

## Notable Quotes

"The real breakthrough innovation is when you leapfrog a few layers, you co-optimize and co-design them, and now all of a sudden you've taken what could have been a 2x here, 2x here, and 2x here, and instead of being multiplicative to 8x, it's actually 100x because you've optimized across all three layers." — Dylan Patel
"Jensen absolutely hates a world where all the hyperscalers have all the power... He wants to create a multipolar world. A world where OpenAI, Anthropic, and Google models are the only models is one in which he's screwed." — Dylan Patel
"The line has been up and to the right in terms of capabilities this entire time, and [skeptics] are like, 'Look, this benchmark didn't improve.' That's because it's at 90 percent. Look at the new benchmark." — Dylan Patel

Anyways, I was very much obsessed with posting and things like that. Eventually, around that time, I got into an argument with someone on the internet, and they doxed me—they publicly revealed my identity from my anonymous account. At the time, I was like, "Oh no, I'm scared." I stopped posting for like three weeks, and then I was like, "What am I doing? Why do I care?" So then I just started posting under... I had blogs and stuff as well. I made a real blog, SemiAnalysis, and on my 24th birthday, I posted two blogs. From there, it was not a newsletter, but I got so much traction because now, instead of posting under an anonymous name, it was a real name. I put a lot more effort into those two posts than I usually did; instead of like shitposting on the internet, it was real effort into the blog. You can actually go back and read those if you want. They're not that great, but they were good for the time. They were the best stuff you could find on the internet about semis. And I just kept posting, posting, posting. I started getting a lot of consulting business. In 2020, I was again crashing out and didn't know what I wanted to do, so I packed everything up. I took my truck, bought a tent that fits on the back of the truck, bought an air mattress, and drove around all these National Parks all around America. Two or three, maybe four days of the week, I'd stay in a random motel where I negotiated the price to be like $30 a night for a room. I'd work on SemiAnalysis stuff, and then on the weekends, I'd read books, oftentimes reading textbooks while in some random National Park or hiking. I listened to audiobooks about semiconductors, about AI, about all the things that I cared a lot about, and got way more educated over these six months where I was just going to every National Park. The whole time, I was posting. I was alone the whole time I was posting blogs, and everyone was like, "Dylan, what the f*** are you doing?" This was pre-Starlink—the very early days of Starlink. "Pre-Starlink?" "Pre-Starlink." Yeah, so it was very much like, "What are you doing?" I traveled around LATAM again for a year, initially with my friend and then with my ex, for about a year. And then in '22, '23, '24—end of '21, '22, '23, and '24—I was completely... I'm still completely homeless since mid-2020, right? But I'm traveling around to every conference in the world. I go to 40-plus conferences a year. No matter where in the supply chain it is, I'm like, "Oh, that looks interesting, I guess I'll go to that." I went to one conference like, "Wow, this is amazing." You get to talk to the experts, and they're going to talk to you because you're so excited. In the case of semiconductors, everyone's a boomer, so they don't see young people who are excited about it. They're really happy to tell you stuff, and so you just keep doing that. "Okay, I have to ask on this: was there a part of the supply chain or one of these conferences that particularly changed your view of the semi world, or that you felt then, or feel now, is particularly underrated?" I think the trade shows and conferences range really widely. Obviously, the ones I have the most fun at include NeurIPS. Why is that? Because it's 20,000 AI researchers, and they're generally in my age range, so it's a lot of fun. But they're also leading AI researchers, and you learn a lot. There are also a lot of parties. And it ranges all the way to, you know, some random chemical conference in Japan where it's 300 Japanese dudes, with about 20 guys from ASML, 20 guys from TSMC, and 20 guys from Intel, and those are the only people who speak English. Everyone else speaks only Japanese, and you're like, "I guess it's still pretty interesting and fun." I think one skill set I have is that I'm able to bond with anyone, regardless of their background and who they are. I'm able to talk to them and find something interesting to talk about—oftentimes it's the tech stuff. So, I think the most interesting conferences are often the really big ones because that's where the biggest stuff is happening. But the niches that are really, really exciting are things like SPIE. There's IEEE, which is international electrical engineering, and then there's SPIE, which is another ecosystem. SPIE conferences are super, super deep in the details. Every single one that I went to, especially like SPIE Advanced Lithography or SPIE Photomask, I went to them the first time and I didn't even understand 90% of what I heard. Then I read, read, read, read. I built some context, of course, and then the next time I went, I understood like half of it. The third time I went, I understood like 75% of it. Even now, I go and I'm like, "F***, I still don't understand everything that's going on." Whereas, you go to NeurIPS a couple of times and you can understand, okay, what's neurosymbolic reasoning, what's this, what's that. You can kind of get a mapping of what everything is pretty quickly. But some parts of the supply chain are so arcane, so deep, and so technical, it takes a lot of time for you to even understand what's happening on everything for every research paper. You go to a conference for a few reasons, right? You understand the research that's being published, but what you really care about is understanding how that research intersects with technology and how it differs from what's there today. None of these research papers tell you what's happening today. But then you just ask people, and you build contacts, and you learn. You learn about the supply chain, like, "Oh, this company supplies this company," even though it's not publicly stated anywhere. Or you learn that this chemical costs about this much, and a tool uses about this much. You hear the horror stories, like, "This chemical had a shortage, and it totally threw off this part of the supply chain." It turns out there are only three companies in the world that make that chemical. My favorite one is: I learned from a Japanese guy at that specific Japanese conference where almost no one spoke English. In very broken English, he told me about how his father worked in this industry in the 1980s. The only factory in the world that built this chemical burned down, and that caused memory prices to double or triple. I was like, "Wow, not too different from today." "Not at all. It's crazy." "Inference is going to be the biggest market on Earth, and the biggest market beyond Earth. Agree or disagree?" I mean, obviously, the use of tokens is going to be the biggest market, and the value that's created from tokens is going to be the biggest market. I think "tokenomics"—the use of tokens and the adoption of AI—is the most important thing that's happening. Inference, whether it's open models or closed models, will be one of the biggest markets in the world—much bigger than oil, I think, and much bigger than many other parts. Inference of AI will be many percentage points of the GDP, right? "What you've done with InferenceX, I think, is the industry standard. Maybe say a word on why you started it, what it does, and what people misunderstand about performance benchmarking on inference?" Yeah, so to zoom back, at SemiAnalysis we do a lot of stuff. A lot of it is research for institutional clients and our subscription-based products, but a lot of it is also like, "Hey, you know, this would just be cool to figure out. Let's figure out how to figure it out and just post it publicly," and that begets more and more scale. We've done this with a lot of GPU benchmarking, testing, training performance, and inference performance. But ultimately, we saw that inference benchmarking was just point-in-time. You test it, you take some time, and you release it, and then it's slow, arcane, and outdated because models change all the time. I feel like every week there's a new model, whether it's a Chinese model, or today Mithos 5 Fable dropped, and new models are coming out all the time. On the software layer—PyTorch, vLLM, SGLang, new drivers—something drops every week. In fact, the update cycle for most of these libraries is twice a week, so you basically have the software updating all the time and therefore performance changing. New inference optimizations are coming out, and those get updated. I feel like it's a relentless breakthrough after breakthrough after breakthrough that keeps driving efficiency and cost down, which is why we've seen model costs drop for equivalent quality by like 60x a year. It's incredible. But to stay on top of that, you can't have point-in-time benchmarking. You need to have benchmarks be living and breathing—i.e., constantly running on the latest hardware and on the latest models. And so we embarked on a project, and we got a lot of buy-in from the ecosystem. This was only possible because we had enough aura with some of the ecosystem where we were able to get CoreWeave, Crusoe, Nebius, Oracle, Microsoft, Amazon, Google, and OpenAI to contribute compute to us. Then we were able to work with SGLang and vLLM, and now Radix-Ark and Infereact, which are the private companies leading those open-source efforts, to collaborate with us. We were able to get Nvidia, AMD, Google, and Amazon to collaborate because we're adding TPUs and Trainium. Now we've got all these people collaborating, and we've got over $50 million of hardware donated to us. Once we launch TPUs and Trainium, it'll actually be over $100 million of hardware—maybe about 15 different chip types, all running these benchmarks every single day on all the latest models. We run benchmarks on the best model from Moonshot, the best model from Alibaba, about five different Chinese models, the best open-source models from the best Chinese labs, and also the best US open-source models like LLaMA, Nemotron, etc. We are running these benchmarks every day in an automated fashion, and they run on these servers that are dedicated to us for inference benchmarking. We sweep across so many different configurations and optimization types. What it creates is a database where all the results and configurations are public. Now we have the Pareto optimal curve. A lot of times when people are comparing inference performance, they are taking a sub-optimal curve or point for someone else and comparing it to their own optimal one. It's like, well, yeah, if I drove a Porsche versus some race car driver, obviously I'd drive it slower. It's the same thing with inference benchmarking. And so, what we did is we created open-source containers for the optimal points across every point on the interactivity (i.e., how fast is it responding to me) versus batch size (i.e., how many users am I simultaneously serving) curve. Now, anyone who wants the optimal point can just go to InferenceX, download it, and run that as the optimal point. They can check every day if they want, or they can even auto-download the most optimal point for that model, and their inference performance will be near peak. "Is that curve, the throughput-interactivity curve, the most important one in your opinion?" Yeah, I think most things in hardware, infrastructure, the model, and the application layer—everything is downstream of that curve. Is it something that needs to be super-super fast with super-low latency where I don't really care about the cost, so I make the batch size very low and use techniques like speculative decoding or multi-token prediction heavily? Or is it something where I'm batch processing a ton of documents and don't really care about all these things, so I don't use these techniques that are worse on cost efficiency but help you with speed for an individual user because I just want to pack a bunch of users? I don't care if the document takes all night to process, right? Right now, the way we treat AI infrastructure is like it's one-size-fits-all. But over time, we're going to get to the point where you have batch workloads or you need instant response, and the whole curve is going to matter for users. We see this with Anthropic, right? Claude Code fast mode costs way more than regular mode. Same with OpenAI's priority queue thing. "Sorry, dumb question: how does cost factor into this chart?" So, let's say, as an imaginary example, I have a batch size of 100, and I can do 10 tokens per second per user. In total, I'm doing a thousand tokens per second off of that one piece of compute. That's one side of the curve—super slow, 10 tokens per second. The other side is I have 500 tokens per second, but I can only have one user, or maybe 250 tokens per second with one user. Then there are points in the middle that are more Pareto optimal. The average person actually wants like 50 or 100 tokens a second, and maybe a certain number of users can be batched together. So the curve is, okay, a thousand tokens total per second or 250 tokens total per second depending on how many users I batch, and there's a curve in the middle. Ultimately, some workloads will actually want the 4x cost decrease because the same unit of hardware can do a thousand versus 250. And for some users, I'll pay 4x more because I don't care about the price; I care about time because the person using the tokens is expensive, or the feedback loop that I have here is expensive. "If you had to guess—you choose the timeframe, ten years or fifteen years—what percent of inference compute do you think will happen in space?" "Sonia coming in high, 99%." "This is a tough one." "So you choose the timeframe, ten years, fifteen, whatever timeframe." So, I think the non-consensus—or at least, again, the SpaceX thing... I love SpaceX, by the way, and I totally would buy the IPO if I could buy stocks. "Not investment advice." Thank you, thank you, not investment advice, Sequoia there. I don't think that space data centers will really matter in the next three to five years. With that said, I think in 20 years, the vast majority of compute will be going to space. The real factor there is the cost of building power on terrestrial land and how much power you are going to be able to do on terrestrial land. I think, obviously, my views of how many gigawatts or terawatts are devoted to inference are on a crazy curve. "What's your forecast? How many gigawatts?" Yeah, I think by 2030, just OpenAI and Anthropic will have over 100 gigawatts combined. And then you'll add Meta, Google, and so on and so forth. It's a humongous amount of compute that will be dedicated to inference. By like 2040, it'll be terawatts. The curve of productivity that we're going to get is massive, and so inference deployment is going to be huge. If we look at 2040, I think probably more than half of incremental compute will be going to space, but if you look at 2030, I think it's sub-1%. "Do you think intelligence per watt has been increasing? It seems like there's still a giant gap between where we are in intelligence per watt versus human biology. Do you think we are close to closing that gap, and if so, where is that gain going to come from?" Yeah, I think it often depends on what you're doing, too. A TI-84 has way more intelligence per watt in terms of doing math than us, and it's like 30 years old, right? So obviously, this is a dumb comparison for general intelligence. But general intelligence-wise, one of the things InferenceX does is we also measure the power and cost of all of this hardware. So we offer not just throughput versus interactivity, but we offer cost versus interactivity, and we offer power versus interactivity. As far as whether intelligence per watt has been increasing, I mentioned it's been a 60x cost decrease for the same benchmark level. We've also seen the same on intelligence per watt. It's not been exactly 60x; it's been closer to like 40x, as some of the efficiencies are in non-power ways. But there's been a humongous improvement in intelligence per watt on an annual basis—at least so far this year, last year, the year before, and the year before. I expect that to continue. As far as where we are relative to the human brain, we're many orders of magnitude away. Thankfully, it doesn't really matter. We can devote a lot of power to computers; it's much easier to power computers than human brains, where we have sickness, disease, food preferences, and sleep. "Sleep, exactly." "Let me just ask one more question on this general theme. In my opinion, in terms of intelligence per watt or intelligence per dollar—any of these metrics—I think there are kind of three levels of input. You can get hardware improvements where the hardware is more efficient, low-level systems optimizations like kernel-level improvements or matrix multiplication libraries, or you can get high-level model or algorithmic improvements at the highest level. It seems to me that in the last three years, mostly the gains have come from the hardware level, and some from the model level. Would you agree with that? Do you think that's what the future looks like? Do you think there's still a bunch of juice to squeeze in, say, the kernel level?" "Yeah, Sean, I completely disagree with you, by the way." "Great, great—that's why I'm asking the question." Okay, so I think one way is to look at it as these three different layers. In that sense, from Hopper to Blackwell—which is all we've had over the last three years—there's roughly a 30x improvement on DeepSeek on the most optimized deployment, which you can see on InferenceX. But over the last three years, we've had way more improvement in intelligence per watt, with a lot of that coming from the model layer. If you look back three years, it was GPT-4. Now, a smaller model like Qwen, which is 27B parameters total and 2 billion active, is way better. So, you've got this huge improvement on the model layer, and you've got this pretty sizeable improvement on the hardware, but it's really about that co-design layer. I think that's what is important. If you look at the architecture of any of these models—and DeepSeek is the most famous one that is public—DeepSeek got huge efficiency gains from co-optimization and kernel-level optimizing memory. Yes, I think it's about kernels, of course, but it's actually about building the hardware architecture for the chip. If you look at the shapes of all the experts in DeepSeek v3, they were all optimized for Hopper. And if you look for v4, they're optimized for Blackwell and Huawei's chip. What's interesting is, despite the fact that TPUs are objectively an amazing chip—they run all of DeepMind and do all the training for Anthropic on the pre-training side—TPUs suck at running DeepSeek. But they are really, really great at running other kinds of models that don't run well on Nvidia. There is a level of deep optimization being done, whether it's shapes, network I/O patterns, how you do the collectives, or how you do things around the arithmetic intensity of the attention mechanism. All of these different things are co-optimized between the model, the hardware, and the infra software in between, and it's hard to say you can disentangle the gains. "Do you think that—my understanding is that China has done this a lot better than the West the last few years. DeepSeek was one of the first models to really do this." I don't necessarily think so. I think it's more so that the West doesn't tell people what they do. OpenAI didn't tell people how sparse GPT-4o was, what the shape size was, or any of those things. But GPT-4o is roughly the same size, slightly smaller than DeepSeek v3, and 4o came out a little bit earlier, if I recall correctly. "So is your view that all three of these things have been happening simultaneously at roughly the same rate, and the biggest gains are when you just co-optimize?" Yeah, I would say there have been more gains on the model layer than on the software infrastructure layer and the hardware layer, but there have been innovations on every layer. Really, the biggest gain and the beauty of the best labs is when they co-optimize all three. Even though Anthropic uses many different kinds of hardware, they don't really inference too much on TPUs—they mostly train on TPUs on the pre-training side—and they inference a lot on GPUs. GPUs are more of the jack-of-all-trades, but they've optimized their hardware, they've optimized their model, and they've optimized everything so they can do that. OpenAI's prior models were optimized more for Hopper, and now they're more optimized for Blackwell. As you step forward through time, you see these labs doing the same thing. Google optimized Gemini 2 for the TPU v6e, Gemini 3 was, and then the next Gemini that's coming out is really optimized for the TPU v7. A lot of these things are being co-optimized, and when you pull that model and run it on old hardware, it's really not that great. So I think this co-optimization is the most important thing. It's called hardware-software co-design, and that's what is really exciting. In my day-to-day, it's great to look at one layer and see all these innovations, but the real breakthrough is when you leapfrog a few layers, co-optimize, and co-design them. Now, all of a sudden, instead of taking a 2x here, 2x here, and 2x there, which would multiplicatively be 8x, it's actually 100x because you've optimized across all three layers. That's what is really exciting about what you see at the labs and at a company like Nvidia, which is co-optimizing from the model layer all the way downstream to the silicon. Or you look at a company like TSMC: they're co-optimizing not just fabrication, but all the way from the components, consumables, and tools upstream to what their customers are designing. It is this co-optimization across many layers of the abstraction stack. "There will always be bottlenecks somewhere in that optimization, right? They lag behind and then need to get pulled forward, with band-aids to cover them. If you had to predict, at any level of the stack—literally anywhere—what are some of the bottlenecks you're tracking most acutely for next year? Not necessarily in the supply chain or in terms of scale, but in terms of technology. It can be in the supply chain too, but just things like memory improvements, or is it just scaling laws?" Memory is an easy one that everyone has talked about, but I'm not going to talk about it from a supply chain angle; I'm going to talk about it from a technology angle. Memory capacity and bandwidth have been improving very slowly. The NAND cell was invented like 25 years ago, and the DRAM cell was invented like 40 years ago, and there's been no major breakthrough in cell technology. Even over the last five years, all we've really done is make HBM faster with more stacks. But there are new innovations coming in the next few years where, instead of stacking the HBM separately from the chip, you stack the memory directly on the chip, and that makes your bandwidth explode. There are interesting companies in that space and interesting POCs that companies are trying to do there. I think memory bandwidth is one of the biggest bottlenecks. Another one is that for the history of silicon—for the last two decades at least—how many watts a chip is can be easily predicted just by looking at its size. For a data center or desktop chip, it peaks at one watt per millimeter squared. So, if a chip is 100 square millimeters, generally the power consumption is around 100 watts or a little bit less. If you look at the newest Nvidia silicon and the newest TPU silicon, they are still in that range of one watt per millimeter squared. Power chips are now getting to 1400 watts, and the next generation is 2000 watts for Nvidia with Blackwell. As you move forward to Rubin Ultra, it's going to be like 4000 watts. But really, they're just increasing the amount of silicon. What's exciting is we're now finally doing things—and it's in development right now—where you actually can pump the amount of power into the silicon to be way more than one watt per millimeter squared. That all of a sudden means you need less silicon. Obviously, it's running at higher power, and it's less efficient in some cases, but you reduce the amount of silicon and are able to overcome certain things. "Would that be related to thermal issues?" Thermal issues, electrical interference issues—there are all sorts of different issues that crop up. That's why it's a hard engineering problem and why we've stuck at about one. But what's exciting is the world is trying to change these things. Interestingly, in a different part of the supply chain, people talk about how energy is hard and how we have energy bottlenecks. It's like, yeah, but there are actually very simple solutions you could think of. Take the millions of diesel engines for trucks that the US has the capacity to make. You can very trivially convert them to use natural gas in the assembly line and then hook them up to an electrical motor—like back-driving it, so the electrical motor generates electricity rather than the motor causing the rotation of the wheel. Now you've generated electricity by pumping gas into something the US can make millions of. Then people say, "Okay, well, that sounds like a pain in the ass to service, right? Because now you have to have hundreds of these on a data center site." Well, actually, you can just pull people out of car mechanic shops and have them run around and repair truck engines. It's actually pretty trivial—I don't want to say it's trivial, I couldn't do it. "I think you're making a really good point, which is that because the West wasn't really thinking about semis or even hardware more broadly for the last 20 or 30 years, we didn't have as much innovation. We didn't have the best minds thinking about how to improve these things." Why would you want to go work in hardware when you can build ads and sell ads? "Yeah, exactly." "Okay, I'm dying to ask: Nvidia versus TPU, what are your thoughts?" I think everyone wants to pick one or the other, but it's really a function of... Look, two years from now, Google is going to make 10-plus million TPUs through their supply chain, and Nvidia is going to make many millions—tens of millions—of GPUs. Both are going to be $100-plus billion markets. Google is going to have $100-plus billion of TPUs created a year, and Nvidia will be $500-plus billion, or whatever it is. I'm not making a specific revenue forecast; this is just a thought experiment. "Our research has that." "Tells you you've been media-trained so well." Absolutely, you know, getting ready for the SpaceX IPO. "Are you guys big in SpaceX?" Okay, so that makes sense. "We're very lucky to be very large investors." Awesome, awesome. So, I would say in the case of Google TPUs versus Nvidia GPUs, they both have points that are really in their favor. Nvidia will be like, "Oh, well, we have switches, and we're general purpose," and TPUs will be like, "Well, we're more optimized, we're actually more energy-efficient, and our network is more optimized for certain types of network architectures." You have these counterpoints that both would really get into. I could, with a straight face, argue with you that GPUs are way better than TPUs, or that TPUs are way better than GPUs. But it comes down to hardware-software co-design. Actually, the way OpenAI's models are headed, it would be a terrible decision for them to use TPUs, potentially. And the way that Anthropic's and Google's models are headed, it's actually a terrible decision, potentially, for them to train with GPUs. I mean, it would be fun for them to train, but... "How? Why is that? What is the fundamental difference there?" There are various things, right? The size of the matrix multiply unit is different, as a very simple thing. And therefore, the shape of the matrix multiply you do, the attention mechanism you use, the way that attention mechanism is structured, and the way the experts are structured. "So you think OpenAI and Anthropic are converging to very different model architectures?" I think they have quite different model architectures, in fact. OpenAI's are much more sparse, and that has benefits. Anthropic's are—you know, they're still sparse, but more dense in general, and that has different benefits. There are many other things, like the network topology. With Nvidia, all of their chips are connected to NVLink switches. For Google, they have no switch, but what they've done is... well, Nvidia's NVLink can only connect 72 GPUs. For Google, their ICI can connect 8,000 chips at super-high bandwidth, but you have to pass through other chips to get there because there's no switch, so there are tradeoffs there. There are positives and negatives, and that influences the model architecture. It's not necessarily that you should claim one is better than the other, because, at the end of the day, how do you say that this is better than that when you can't measure them in isolation? It also extends up to the model layer, right? "But I remember for a long time thinking, first, about the programmability of Nvidia and just CUDA as such a big moat. It seems that narrative has kind of changed, at least in my mind, over the last three to six months. Model companies no longer care. They're like, 'If we have to write custom kernels for this other chip, so be it, we'll work with four or five chips if we have to.'" Claude and Codex are actually quite good at doing a lot of that optimization work. And so, it's not like there are 10,000 model companies that each need programmability. There are on the order of tens, maybe, of model companies. So it seems to me that the fundamental premise of tens of thousands of big customers needing CUDA compatibility—that kind of thesis is changing. Yeah, I mean, certainly the CUDA moat and software moat are at least partially disentangled because models are just great at coding, and all software gets commoditized in that case. I do think there is some level of open source where what people call the "CUDA moat" has nothing to do with CUDA. Instead, it's the fact that DeepSeek, Kimi, Zhipu AI, Alibaba, Tencent—all these companies, and Xiaomi had an awesome model recently—their models are all co-designed for GPUs. Therefore, if I want to run them on TPUs, in some cases, they don't run very well on TPUs. Now Google just has to create their own open-source model ecosystem or open-source models themselves, so they have the Gemma models. And so you end up with... well, that's not really CUDA as a moat; it's that the downstream product is more optimized for Nvidia. In these cases, these companies are just open-sourcing them—like Nemotron is open-sourcing—and then the users of it, the inference API providers, the RL companies that are trying to take open models and customize them for businesses, they are all downstream of that fact. They're like, "Okay, well, I guess I need to use Nvidia because the ecosystem uses Nvidia," even though they don't particularly care about using CUDA kernels because the models are great at that. But it's about the shape, like, "Well, this expert, the hidden dimension is this," right? Therefore, it's better to run on Nvidia GPUs than it is on TPUs, and vice versa. If Google were to actually open source really good models, this would be the same thing. People would take their models and say, "Oh, wow, these don't run that well on Nvidia GPUs. I should actually just rent TPUs or buy TPUs and do it on there." For small teams, you're going to want to use all the open-source software like vLLM, SGLang, PyTorch, and all that stuff. But the big labs, they don't necessarily need to use all that. OpenAI forked PyTorch long ago, and Anthropic and all these other people don't necessarily rely heavily on the open-source implementation of these things. They forked things or built them on their own already, so they don't need to rely on the open source. Therefore, now it's more like, "I'll choose the best hardware, and I'll co-design my model and infrastructure software through and through for that hardware which is the best and most cost-efficient, and I'll have AI help me write all that software." "What do you think of Cerebras?" I think Cerebras is a really innovative company. In some spots of the market, they're really, really good—very fast inference. I think that's a big market. We use fast mode almost exclusively at SemiAnalysis, by the way. "I love how disciplined you've been about accounting for—I don't know if that was one exhibit you did or if you do it consistently—but accounting for the dollars spent and the ROI on each task. It's awesome analysis." Yeah, we do it pretty diligently, so thank you. That was the Dark GDP article that we wrote. We also track everyone's token spend per day. If someone spikes up, I'm like, "What did you do?" And it's like, "Okay, thank you for telling me, that seems worth it. Cool, on with my day." I think fast mode is obviously worth a lot for high-end tasks. I could just see so many different use cases where super-fast tokens are worth it. I think ultimately, supply chains matter, what technology you can bring in matters, and more and more as the industry gets bigger, supply chain diversification happens. Right now, everyone's chip more or less looks the same. It's a big logic compute die in the center, and there's some HBM on the right and left, and on the top and bottom. The top side is networking, and then the bottom side is PCIe and other I/O. That is the exact same structure for Trainium, TPU, NVIDIA chips, and most of the startups. Not Groq and Cerebras—they're doing weird shit, but that's cool, you know. I think as you step forward, we're going to get more bifurcation of hardware architecture and model architecture. Therefore, people are going to co-optimize them, and some of them will end up in local minima, right? If this is like gradient descent, people are trying to go to the most optimized solution. Some people will race to a local minima, and then the question is, how do you scoot back over to the absolute minimum? To some extent, NVIDIA will always be more general-purpose than anyone else's chip in general, at least on a parallel AI compute basis, because they have so many customers who care about different things and who will always give them feedback in the design. The specialized minima will always be better than them, but is that minima a local minima? Like, is the TPU, Trainium, Groq, Cerebras, or whoever's design optimized awesome for here, but in the end state actually you've got to go over here, and so they're wrong? Maybe they make a great time, they're great for a little bit of time, but then they end up being wrong. That's the real question. And so, I think there will be a big market for general-purpose AI compute, because you talk to people at labs, they don't even know what architecture they're going to be doing in a year. " structure, robust fiscal...

TL;DR

The "Byzantine Empire" is an artificial historical misnomer for what was legally, culturally, and politically the unbroken continuation of the Roman Empire, which survived for over a millennium in the East due to its unique "monarchic republic" structure, robust fiscal institutions, and resilient Roman-Orthodox identity.

***

The Myth of "Byzantium" and the 2,200-Year Roman Timeline

  • The Historical Misnomer: Anthony Kaldellis, historian of the Roman/Byzantine Empire, asserts that the term "Byzantine Empire" is a post-collapse historical invention.
      • Direct Continuation: The citizens of the Eastern Empire legally and culturally identified only as Romans (*Romanoi*), and their state was the direct, unbroken continuation of the ancient Roman Empire.
      • Cultural Erasure: Western European historians historically renamed the East the "Empire of the Greeks" and later "Byzantium" for political reasons, effectively excluding the Eastern half from Western cultural genealogy.
  • The Long Roman Continuum: The Roman state survived for over 2,200 years (from 753 BC to the fall of Constantinople in 1453 AD) through three distinct phases:
      • The Roman Kingdom (753 BC – 510 BC): A localized regime ruled by legendary kings.
      • The Roman Republic (509 BC – 27 BC): Governed by an aristocratic senate with popular cooperation; this was Rome's most expansionist and imperialistic era.
      • The Imperial Monarchy (27 BC – 1453 AD): Transitioned from a western focus in Rome to an eastern focus in Constantinople, remaining a monarchy for 1,500 years.
  • The Ship of Theseus Metaphor: While the empire gradually shifted from Latin-speaking and pagan to Greek-speaking and Orthodox Christian, there was never a systemic rupture. The political community evolved so gradually that contemporaries never felt they had ceased to be Roman.

The "Monarchic Republic" and the Threat of Violent Deposition

  • No Right to the Throne: Unlike other dynastic empires, the Eastern Roman Empire had no legal or religious "right of succession." The emperor emerged from a republican matrix as a public servant, meaning their legitimacy was subject to a perpetual referendum.
  • Extreme Political Vulnerability: The threat of violent overthrow served as the primary check on imperial power.
      • Statistics of Violence: Approximately 46% of Constantinople's emperors were violently overthrown, blinded, or mutilated. Over its 1,000-year Eastern history, the empire experienced roughly 120 swift, non-ideological civil wars over who was best fit to rule.
      • The Imperial Persona: To maintain goodwill and prevent rebellion, emperors projected a highly consistent persona of being tirelessly hardworking, proactive, responsive to petitions, and "sleepless" (exemplified by the workaholic Justinian).
  • The Hippodrome as a Democratic Forum: Holding between 30,000 and 100,000 citizens, the Hippodrome was not just for entertainment but served as the primary venue for popular consultation. Here, citizens directly acclaimed, booed, or forced policy reversals (such as canceling the "German tax") from the emperor.

Systemic Turning Points: Caracalla to Diocletian

  • The Edict of Caracalla (212 AD): This decree (*Constitutio Antoniniana*) extended full Roman citizenship to all free inhabitants of the empire.
      • Radical Inclusivity: Unlike modern empires, Rome fully integrated its conquered populations. Within a generation, the highest levels of administrative and military power—including the emperorship itself—were occupied by provincials.
      • Securing Stability: By granting equal rights to provincials, the edict sapped long-term separatist sentiment, turning provincial rebellions into contests to rule the entire Roman state rather than split from it.
  • The Crisis of the Third Century: A 50-year "perfect storm" featuring a devastating plague (killing up to 5,000 people a day in Rome), hyperinflation, foreign invasions, and extreme political chaos (26 emperors murdered in 50 years).
  • Diocletian’s "Big Government" Reset (284–305 AD): Diocletian stabilized the crumbling state by instituting a series of rigid, long-lasting reforms:
      • The Tetrarchy: Dividing military jurisdiction among four collaborative rulers to swiftly handle frontier threats.
      • Fiscal Overhaul: Implementing a universal census, flat universal taxation (subjecting Italy to taxes for the first time), and creating a massive civil bureaucracy separate from the military.
      • Class Enforcement: Legally binding certain professions (like farming and military service) to their roles to secure tax revenues and recruitment, though high social and physical mobility persisted in practice.

The Geopolitical Clamp of Constantinople and the Rise of Christianity

  • Constantinople's Strategic Location: Founded by Constantine the Great in 330 AD on the site of Byzantion, "New Rome" acted as a strategic clamp.
      • Defensive Advantage: It sat safely between the critical Danube and Euphrates frontiers, allowing emperors to easily redeploy forces.
      • Elite Cohesion: By establishing a New Senate of 2,500–3,000 wealthy elites from the East, the state unified the Balkans, Asia Minor, and Syria, preventing the eastern half from fracturing.
  • The Co-optation of Christianity: Rather than Christianity "triumphing" over the Roman state, the state captured and institutionalized the church.
      • Constantine’s Sincerity: Moving from an estimated 10% Christian population under Constantine, the empire took two centuries to become overwhelmingly Christian.
      • Double-Edged Sword: Christianity was highly polarizing, creating internal theological divisions that political actors exploited, but it also acted as a powerful, unifying, and defining cultural force.
  • Eunuchs in Administration: Castrated men played a critical role in the imperial court as household staff, generals (such as Narses), and diplomats. Because they could not build dynasties or pass on wealth to heirs, emperors trusted them to run parallel networks of power to counter ambitious military families.

Invasions, Flamethrowers, and the Sources of Roman Resilience

  • Exogenous Shocks vs. Internal Decay: Challenging traditional "decline and fall" narratives, Kaldellis argues the empire was internally healthy and characterized by slow, steady growth. Its major crises were caused by sudden, simultaneous external shocks.
      • The 11th-Century Triple Whammy: The empire buckled under simultaneous, unpredicted attacks from the Normans (Western heavy cavalry), the Pechenegs (Northern nomads), and the Seljuk Turks (who permanently terraformed Asia Minor's agriculture into pasturage).
  • Defensive Warfare and "Greek Fire": The Eastern Empire survived existential threats, such as the Arab sieges of Constantinople, through superior fortifications and secret military technologies.
      • Greek Fire: A highly classified liquid incendiary compound deployed via pressurized valves on naval ships and thrown as clay hand grenades. It burned on water and successfully repelled Arab and Viking fleets.
  • Why the Empire Lasted: Kaldellis attributes the empire's exceptional resilience to a two-part foundation:

1. Responsive Government: An extraordinary and genuine effort by the central government to prove to its subjects that it ruled for their benefit.

2. Cohesive Identity: A deeply unified cultural sense of being both Roman and Orthodox, combined with a collective refusal to live under the rule of non-Roman, non-Christian invaders.

***

Notable Quotes

So what they have to do is make sure that doesn't happen to them. And the best way to avoid that fate is to actually do the things that will make people happy." — *Anthony Kaldellis*

So you argue that even though the personalities of the various emperors varied, as we will talk about, and as they are often venerated, celebrated, criticized, and talked about, the late Roman Empire was projecting a persona to its citizens that was consistent throughout its history. Can you describe what you mean by this and describe the different detailed characteristics of this persona? Yes. So this persona is what the government, the emperor, the court, and the spokesmen of the bureaucracy want their subjects to think this whole thing is about—like how is power being used, why, and for whom, right? And they do so in laws that they promulgate throughout the empire. Sometimes they have these laws read in church, for example, or posted publicly. They're definitely eager to have them communicated to as many people as possible. But they're also a function of imperial rhetoric—the way you praise an emperor, the way you talk about an emperor. You find this language in petitions. This is a hugely important glue for this society: that anyone could basically petition the authorities for just about anything. That's not technically true, but there's a lot of petitioning going on. So, you know, if you think something's wrong or you've been treated unjustly, you can petition local officials, even imperial officials. You can petition the emperor. And the expectation is that that petition will be read, addressed, and answered. It might not be answered in the way that you want, but it will be answered. And those responses contain this language also, right? So these are the different media through which the emperors are broadcasting this persona. And what is that persona? Basically, that the authorities are responsive to the needs of their subjects, and that they are accountable. In other words, they understand that they are exercising public power and that their mandate is to exercise it solely for the benefit of their subjects. They keep saying this, renouncing any kind of private interest. In other words, "We're not doing this for our benefit," say the authorities, "we're doing this for your benefit." Also, that they're proactive. So the emperors are constantly saying, "We're planning ahead; we're trying to foresee what problems you might have, and we are solving them before you experience them." And they are also working very hard. So hardworking, in fact, that one of the clichés that they keep using is "sleepless." The emperors keep saying, "I'm losing sleep over this. I'm not sleeping at night because I'm just worrying so much about your problems and how to solve them." Justinian, for example, was publicly known as the sleepless emperor. And not because he was a demon whose head floated around in the palace on its own, which some people claimed... Is that a thing? It was a thing, yes. Okay, that's a whole thing. But sleepless in terms of working so hard that there's no time to sleep. He was a workaholic. That is conceded by even his enemies. All night he was up, just hauling officials in at any old hour to talk about whatever: wars, architecture, laws, religion, theology, and whatever. So the emperors are projecting this image, right? So that's what I call the persona: tireless effort to work on behalf of subjects, to do so responsibly, to be responsive and accountable. In other words, "If some of my officials break the law or oppress you, I'm giving you instruments—like legal instruments—by which to hold them to account." So that is the persona. It's very consistent, and I think it's part of the mechanism of tying the society together. In other words, if subjects think that their emperors are like this, they're more likely to agree to the consensus of essentially paying taxes. And you draw this distinction between rhetoric and action—what the authorities say and what they actually do. So when you say persona, in modern times, some of that could be reframed as propaganda, for example. And propaganda implies that there's a big gap between the rhetoric and the action. One of the things you said you took away from reading Machiavelli is that we should judge people not by what they say, but what they do. So how much of the Roman Empire's government, of what they espoused, was merely rhetoric, and how much was true in how they acted? Yes, this is a key lesson I took from reading Machiavelli, and I find this a very insightful way of thinking about politics. I would recommend that anybody practice this way of looking at the world, right? People and states and powerful people can say anything they want to; at the end of the day, what matters is what they actually do. And this doesn't mean that what they say is irrelevant, even if it's discordant with what they do. But nevertheless, in the case of the East Roman state, my research suggests that the emperors and the authorities were generally sincere in what they said. In other words, I have found that they generally did what they said they were going to do overall—not perfectly, obviously, and of course, these are human beings we're talking about, right? In human societies, there's a lot of corruption and, you know, abuse. Of course, of course there is. But overall, I think that there was a sufficient level of understanding that the emperors are, in fact, doing this. And they were actually incentivized to do this—we can talk about how—because they were also in a pretty vulnerable situation too. So they needed their subjects to be relatively content on this front. Isn't this surprising to you? So, you know, from the outside point of view, Roman emperors, even in the East Roman Empire, technically had a lot of power. Yeah, you can even say in some cases absolute power. And yet they seem to be awfully worried to make everybody in the empire happy. What explains that? Like, what explains the small gap between rhetoric and action in the East Roman Empire? A number of things. But ultimately, it is that there is no right to the throne, and this is a function of the Roman matrix of politics here. In other words—so here's a bit of a paradox. What we call the Roman Republic was the most imperialistic phase of Roman history; like, this is when the Romans did most of their conquering. What we call the empire was much less imperialistic in this way, being much more defensive in its approach, right? And yet our terms are a little mismatched in that way. The empire is more a function of the fact that it has an emperor, rather than because it is engaged in imperialism. And that emperor emerged out of the Republic. But he emerged out of the Republic in a very strange way—in other words, not as a dynasty that created the state. So, for example, if you think of the Ottoman Empire, or many other empires, the whole thing existed because this dynasty engaged in conquest and was successful in creating the state. And so the dynasty was like at the heart of the state. But that's not at all the case in the Roman Empire. The emperor is a figure who emerged within a republic. And in order to create peace after the civil wars, those emperors had to, for a long time, kind of pretend that they were not what they really were, basically by continuing to flatter the idea of a republic that has a first citizen, maybe. "Oh no, no, no, I'm not a king, I just have this office, and that office, and that office, whatever the Senate has given me." In other words, just take the thousand years that I study: there are something like 120 civil wars. Now, these are usually very swift, they're over quickly, and they're never about ideology. So they're not like modern civil wars, right? Is it about slavery, or the rights of kings? No, no, no—they're solely about who has the power. All sides have the same ideology; they talk about the responsibility of government in the same way, right? They're just competing over who's the best man to do the job. And once you have that situation—and it might not be a civil war, it could be a palace coup, it could be a kind of fighting in the capital, something like that—once everyone's taken their side and you have a winner, that's the emperor, and you often overthrow the previous regime. I'll give you some statistics: something like 46 percent of the emperors of Constantinople are overthrown through violence. Forty-six percent. That's almost half, right? So that's a huge, huge number, which means that every emperor is vulnerable and insecure, and they know it. Right? So what they have to do is make sure that that doesn't happen to them. And the way to do that is not by arresting and executing everybody you think might be a threat, or just generally behaving like a Caligula or a Nero—that will just get you killed. They know this. So the best way to avoid that fate is to actually do the things that will make people happy, so that they don't support a rebel if a rebel decides to appear. And sometimes rebels would appear. Now, there's a kind of convention in Constantinople. If you think, "Hmm, that emperor's pretty unpopular, and I think I can overthrow him," what you would do is march down the center boulevard—the Mese, the boulevard—and you'd have your people call the populace of Constantinople out to rally, maybe go to Hagia Sophia, the church, and just create a big crowd and call for the deposition of the emperor. Maybe that would happen and sometimes it did, and sometimes it didn't. The rebel, or one of the rebels, would proceed down the boulevard, and people would just gradually disappear, and then he'd be left alone and go up to Hagia Sophia seeking asylum, right? So that's a bid. That's someone making a bid for the throne, and it just goes disastrously bad. Other times, the people come out because they hate the emperor, and they drag the emperor out and cut his nose off or whatever. So this is where you described that starting with Augustus in the early Imperial period, the government saw itself as a protector of the Roman citizens, which was a minority of the people living in the Roman Empire; it didn't consider the provincials. And then you describe that the Edict of Caracalla extended full Roman citizenship to basically all free inhabitants of the Roman Empire. So what is it? Why was it done? Why is it important, and how did it change the Roman Empire? Right, I agree with you that this is, in fact, a very important—it's a real turning point, in a sense, but it also is part of a process. So, for various reasons, the Roman polity tended to bestow its metropolitan citizenship, that is Roman citizenship, on its allies, and eventually, in time, on many people that it had conquered. And it had various mechanisms for doing so. It could bestow block grants, and sometimes it allowed its generals to bestow citizenship on people they thought would, you know, be necessary players for controlling local societies. Sometimes they even allowed foreign cities—like non-Roman cities—to decide who would get Roman citizenship by basically electing them to certain kinds of offices. Like, they outsourced a lot of this. And so you have, over time, the steady growth of a Roman community of citizens who are not all in Rome and who are not all of Roman origin, whatever that means—they were not descended from people who were from Rome. And this proceeds, you know, it expands, it expands, it expands. By Caracalla's time, so early third century AD, we can estimate that maybe at most a third of the free citizens of the empire are Roman citizens. Everyone else has local citizenship, like you're Alexandrian or Athenian or whatever, and with that, you're subject to those laws. This emperor, Caracalla—now, that's his nickname; his reigning name is Marcus Aurelius Antoninus. So just like Marcus Aurelius, same name, two generations later. In other words, it meant that the rights and opportunities that were available to Roman citizens, say, in the Roman Senate at Rome, are now available to everybody. "Well, also in modern social thought, the fear is that the deep state might be undermining the democracy itself, rather than working with it. It can go both ways.
