So, Diocletian creates these larger bureaucratic structures, and these are the ones that define the society that I study." As a specialist, I focus on the period after him mostly, but he implemented those to solve a lot of these problems. He was mostly successful in terms of generating revenue from a much more troubled and kind of precarious situation, sustaining large armies, and putting down these rebellions and usurpations. He was generally successful at doing that, so he created the model that they kept for the next, well, three centuries or more. But a lot of the elements of that model lasted even beyond that, right? "Yeah, like universal taxation. To give you an example, up until then, Italy was exempt from taxes. Italy was the land of the conquerors. The conquerors don't pay taxes to themselves; the conquerors live off the taxes that others pay, right? So, Italy was this big tax haven. Diocletian, Galerius, his guys—these guys are from Illyria, they're from the Balkans. They're like, 'Why is Italy tax-free, exactly? That doesn't make any sense. It's a very, very wealthy, prosperous part of the world.' And so, no, they taxed Italy." So now, not only do all Romans have the same citizenship and the same law, they all have the same tax system. The Diocletianic tax code, as it were—it's not a code, it's almost like a flat tax—which for us sounds regressive, but by the standards of that time, it's actually kind of progressive in a way. It means that even elites paid the same, right? "Yeah. So, how much of all of this, everything we'll talk about in the coming centuries, is all about taxation?" "I think it's huge. Taxation is the heart of it." "So, I mean the functioning, the success, the flourishing of a society, the way the government works, the way the people are represented, all of it has to do with the civil wars and everything. It feels like taxation is at the core." "Yes, yeah. If you asked me to put my finger on one factor, it would be that." So, the taxation system feels like the early framework for the East Roman Empire. It did create a more homogeneous framework, for sure. By the end of the third century, it's pretty much in place. What Diocletian does is a universal census, and this is continued by his successors. Every taxable asset in the empire is censused, and so you can now start to have a budget. "Now, there is some element, and then you push back against some elements of Diocletian, where he legally limited social and class mobility, in part for the taxation system, and made it in many cases illegal to move and illegal to quit your job. For example, as I read, to guarantee the food supply and land tax, farmers were legally forbidden from moving away from the land they worked so the state always knew who to tax. And then for soldiers, sons of veterans were legally obligated to serve in the army to maintain troop numbers." "You argue that these were tax enforcement mechanisms, not a rigid caste system, and so, in practice, the empire remained a society of high mobility." "This is a complicated matter. So, let's talk, for example, about soldiers. It's probably the case that most soldiers' sons were expected to succeed them, like most professions where you have an apprenticeship, the son will usually succeed, right?" There are a number of other professions that were kind of guild-like in nature, like, for example, shipping, where there were considerable assets involved in order to secure contracts for insurance purposes for the ships and so forth. It made sense for those assets to be passed from father to son, and with them comes the obligation to continue in that line of work. Some of these things are inherently hereditary, and I don't think most people would have objected to it. The farmers is probably the only case where it might have actually involved some coercion. There was a category of farmers—and we don't know how many of them—who were, for fiscal purposes, bound to the land. This doesn't mean they couldn't leave the land physically; no one was preventing them from leaving. But they were fiscally responsible for providing the taxes or rents or whatever because the state wanted to have a secure revenue stream from those lands. Anyway, it's one of these complicated situations. This did create some limitations on those people, and it's a topic of current research: these bound farmers, as it were, who are tied to the land. It's more of a—you know, someone put it that most of the time it's people who own the land, but in this arrangement, it's the land that owns the people. "Well, but you're saying the bigger picture thing is that there was mobility?" "Oh, there's considerable mobility. Class mobility and physical mobility, meaning people moved, and we'll see this from the West into the East and Constantinople and all this kind of stuff." "Oh, yeah, there's tons of mobility. No, this is not a society where people are bound really to—like, we don't find anybody who's like, 'I really don't want to be a soldier, but my father was a soldier and now I'm obligated to be one.' We just don't hear those kinds of complaints of people who don't want to be a soldier." "Why wouldn't you want to be a soldier in the Roman Empire? I mean, it's got tremendous perks." "Right, right. Yeah. So, can we just linger on why is it that Diocletian was able to stabilize that empire? It's like, you know, the Crisis of the Third Century really threatened to tear apart everything. So, why did some of these things that we talked about stabilize?" Well, Aurelian had already united the empire territorially. This was right before Diocletian, and what Diocletian did was put in place a system that reduced the number of rebellions. Simply by having four big guys with four armies, they managed to put out all of those fires, or most of them. And once you have stability—and he also beat the Persians, I mean, Galerius did—starting to beat back foreign invasions, that creates a framework for economic stability. People need to—if they're going to invest in land reclamation or expand production, they need to know that foreigners aren't going to come rampaging through, marauding through, stealing their cattle, enslaving them, and taking them off to work on agricultural farms in Mesopotamia. They need to know this. And if you provide that context of stability, the economic growth and the demographic growth will return. Diocletian created that kind of context. "So, that takes us to Constantine. But first, a quick bathroom break if I could." "Sure." "Quick ten-second thank you to our sponsors. Check them out in the description; it really is the best way to support this podcast. Go to lexfridman.com/sponsors. And now, dear friends, back to our conversation about the Eastern Roman Empire." All right, we're back, and let's talk about Constantine and Constantinople. Let's go to the rise of the East Roman Empire. Who was Constantine? Tell me about his rise to power and the founding of Constantinople in 330 AD. Sure. So, Constantine was the son of one of Diocletian's colleagues, so he comes from within the system. The system was actually designed to be non-hereditary. In other words, the senior emperors would retire, the junior ones would move up to the senior position, and then they would recruit two new ones. They tried to skip the dynastic element so it would be kind of, let's just say, meritocratic—not based on whose son you were. Constantine was a son, and he was passed over, but the armies liked him. He was actually quite competent. And so, there's a series of civil wars among all of these successors of Diocletian, and Constantine emerges as the winner. There are periods when there are quite a few of them—they're like six at a time—but they gradually winnow down to one. So, yeah, it's kind of like you have the quarter-finals, the semi-finals, and then the final war, and Constantine emerges in 324 as the sole ruler of the entire Roman Empire. So, that's who he is. He's definitely an insider, a very, very competent general at civil war, and he gradually moves eastward. As he's conquering his rivals—who are often at times his fathers-in-law or his brothers-in-law, because these people all intermarried, had alliances, and fell out, it's one of those kinds of situations—he moves eastward. By the time he defeats his last rival, an emperor called Licinius in 324, he's in the east. Licinius had his headquarters, or one of his headquarters, at this town called Byzantion. Byzantion is a city right where Constantinople was founded, and it is at exactly the point where Europe meets Asia on the Bosphorus. It's a very strategic location, and it always had been, right? Whoever controls the straits of the Bosphorus—well, we don't need to explain how important that is. And Constantine decides to create a city named after himself, Constantinople, on that spot, Byzantion. So, Byzantion essentially ceases to exist as a city and is replaced by Constantinople, aka New Rome. It's pretty clear that, right from the beginning, Constantine intended this to be a kind of branch office of Rome in the east, a kind of copy and paste of Rome in the east, and it's a plan that his successors continued. Now, there are a number of other cities in the generation or two before this that were also treated as sort of quasi-Romes. Basically, there was a historian in the third century who said, "Well, wherever the emperor is, that's Rome," because emperors are now itinerant. They're no longer staying at Rome; they're mostly on the frontier, and Constantine is one of these frontier emperors. They will sometimes visit Rome, maybe once or twice, maybe once a decade—something like this. So, Rome becomes something that can be sort of copied and pasted in other parts of the empire, and a bunch of other cities get called a new Rome, another Rome, an alternative Rome, or my Rome. Constantinople is the last and most successful of those. Maybe can you speak geographically to the importance of Constantinople? It's an interesting place because when you're talking about a land so large as the West and the East with enemies, with threats from all kinds of different directions, the location where the emperor sits is very important. Look at Rome, right? It's right in the middle of Italy, right in the middle of the Mediterranean. It's a very safe location in a way, but it's very far from the frontiers. This is a period when emperors need to be with the armies, right? They're not senatorial emperors who are sitting in Rome having meetings with senators; they are military emperors who are marching along the frontier, mostly with generals rather than the civilian administration. They're doing their job. Like, this is the hard work; they're slogging through the mud of winter campaigns, not just sitting in a palace enjoying themselves when they could. But Rome just becomes inconvenient because of that. And if you look at Constantinople, it is kind of halfway between the Danube frontier and the Euphrates frontier. So, if you want to prioritize the east, or treat it as an equal part of the Roman Empire as these emperors all did—in fact, almost all emperors who had a choice about whether they would stay in the east or stay in the west chose the east. There's only one who chooses the west, and that's Valentinian later in the fourth century. All the others understand that the east is where it's at. And Constantinople is right between the Danube and the Euphrates, which are two major frontiers. It allows emperors to move between the two. Also, look at its location: north, south, east, west, right? North-south, it is the node between the Black Sea and the Mediterranean. East-west, it is precisely where Europe meets Asia. Because there's a break there—the Bosphorus, the Sea of Marmara, the Hellespont—the Roman Empire, during the civil wars, would tend to break there. You would have one emperor on one side in Asia, and you'd have another emperor on the other side. This just kept happening. Constantinople ultimately functions as a kind of clamp that unifies this whole area, basically the Balkans, Asia Minor, and Syria as a kind of unit. And you know, what is a New Rome? A New Rome is, among other things, a New Senate, right? And Constantine and his successors recruit this new Senate for the New Rome. We're talking about maybe 2,500, 3,000 men at its peak, and these are all recruited from all of these areas. Some come from Rome, but others come from the Aegean region, from Asia Minor, and the Eastern Mediterranean. These are the wealthiest people usually, the most well-connected elites from the whole Eastern Mediterranean, and they come together and form a new purpose, a common purpose, in Constantinople. The empire never breaks there again, right? So, it holds together this part of the world. The breaking point now, interestingly, moves to the Adriatic. When in 395 we talked about the east and west kind of splitting, it breaks at the Adriatic because Constantinople is now holding that whole eastern world together, because all of these elites are now invested in it. They're literally investing in it; they have to invest in it in order to be senators in Constantinople. So, that's kind of one of the functions that it performs: a strategic function, a political function. There's also a cultural one, but yeah, we can talk about that too. That's what you see there on that map: the northwest, east, south. "So, just to linger on it: you do a podcast, 'Byzantium & Friends,' but you're also a guest on podcasts. And so, actually, one way to ask that question, which is fascinating because you said that there's Rome and then there are cultures or traditions like Christianity or being Greek—did Christianity triumph over Rome, or did Rome capture Christianity? Which is the mechanism? I would lean more towards the second. In other words, the religion was co-opted by the imperial system. Now, there are many areas in which that's not the case and where the opposite happened, so I'm not going to be absolute about it. But I think it's very important to recognize that, because Christianity generates this narrative of triumph, which modern historians perpetuate, it's framed in very particular ways. In other words, Christianity triumphs over the ancient religions, displacing Zeus and Apollo and all of that, and animal sacrifice is gone, and that's because Christianity triumphs. That's a very narrow way of looking at it. You pick your enemy, and then you can defeat that enemy, right? But if you look in the other direction, there's the Roman imperial state. You cannot say that Christianity either triumphed over it or even tamed it or anything; it actually became part of it in many ways that determined its history for centuries, and in fact down to today. But he was not like a hardcore Christian all the way through; he was kind of a spiritual, religious kind of person. He thought about religions more generally and just converged towards Christianity as a good religion. He also had ideas about what a good religion is and what a bad religion is. He's thinking in this space. He tends not to be too explicit, depending on the audience. Constantine remains a pragmatist in the sense that he doesn't want to alienate too many of his subjects, and so he's sometimes a bit coy or elusive about what he's getting at. "Constantine and his successors as well, because this was a gradual process, and it takes a very long time. So, let's put it into perspective. We probably can't talk about a majority Christian society until maybe the fifth century—early fifth, mid-fifth. We don't have data, obviously, and we can't talk about a solidly Christian society until the early sixth century." And by solidly, I mean maybe where the non-Christians are 10 or 15 percent, right? So, that's a long time; that's like five centuries after Jesus. That's half a millennium. We're talking about a half-a-millennium process here. It's very long, but it's only a couple of centuries after Constantine, so it takes about two centuries to really make the Roman Empire Christian. Yes, so it's a very slow conversion. Maybe up to 10 percent tops under Constantine, then obviously larger and larger groups join the church, even if only nominally, until you get to the sixth century when you're clearly talking about a mostly or overwhelmingly Christian society. A lot of this is just done through incentives and disincentives, right? Bishops are given funds with which to distribute charity and help to communities in distress. They have lists of widows and whatever they support. Land is given to the church by the emperors or in wills so that they can engage in this kind of social policy. And then there are disincentives. Emperors pass laws that increasingly restrict the exercise of traditional religion. Like, you can't perform animal sacrifices, and then you can't start doing these things like tying ribbons to make wishes, or going out to sacred trees and doing this or that because they say, "Oh, this is kind of nefarious business, it's like magic," or whatever it is. And then they just gradually start outlawing it more and more, putting restrictions like, "If you're not a Christian, you can't hold these high offices," or "There are problems with your wills." "Christianity developed in a number of ways, so it acquired a certain number of forms that were capable of appealing to different constituencies. There is a public ceremony and ritual aspect where you can organize communities in cities to have processions or have all these festivals that take the place of the ancient ones. You have a kind of—not secret, but it's kind of this initiatory aspect where there's baptism, and you learn a sort of creed and you're initiated into the religion with the prospect of a personal afterlife, right? And that appeals to other kinds of people. There are also ways in which it integrated itself into the court and the language of power, and also in the army, right? Soldiers now swear an oath to the Trinity, so it takes all these different forms that enable it to position itself here and there—in the streets, in the court, in the armies, in the churches, in texts and books. And so, it has this kind of manifold diversity, right, that makes it very adaptable. "So, one of the things you mentioned that Constantine did not anticipate, did not realize, is that Christianity creates divisions between various groups." "In part, this is what happens when you have religions that make exclusive claims, right? Like, 'My beliefs are true, and yours are by definition false.' Not just wrong, but false and even kind of evil—like, maybe Satan put them in your mind to lead you astray, right? And this is inherently divisive; it is." So, there's this paradox where Christianity, in contrast to say the ancient religions, is both far more polarizing but also far more powerful as a unifying force at the same time, right? Ancient religions tended not to produce this kind of conflict, or identities, or wars, or these kinds of things. I mean, they could under certain circumstances, but for the most part, they were not drivers of history in this kind of way. But when you have exclusive claims, then suddenly you have these very communities that are consolidated and have this very strong identity, but you also alienate other groups that you then are in conflict with. This is why I, for one, can't come down on whether Christianity is more of a unifying force or a divisive force in the history of the Eastern Empire, because it's both. "You know, also trying to figure out whether the unifying or the divisive aspect is a feature or a bug. Maybe some of the divisive elements are a way for society internally to try to, much like the civil wars, figure itself out. So, sometimes tension and conflict is a good mechanism for figuring out what the people want or what's the truth." "Or what's the truth, yeah." So, the story that it likes to tell itself is that there's this one original truth, and the hardcore of true believers is defending that truth at every moment against all of these evil heretics who are coming in and trying to corrupt it. In reality, what happens is that this truth is something that's evolving in the course of the controversies. Often they don't know that they believed that until in retrospect, once they've had the fight and the winner has this position: "Oh, now that turns out to have been the position we always held," right? And this happens again and again and again. A lot of the time, these Christian theologians who get into so much trouble and get branded as heretics, and sometimes get sent into exile, didn't mean any harm. They often just were stating what they thought everybody believed, and then when they blurted it out, someone—often Alexandria—was like, "What? That's heresy!" and now you're in trouble. Now you're getting into this fight, and you might win, you might lose, but a lot of them were kind of well-intentioned in that way. Or they had no intention of starting a fight, but there's always someone who wants to start a fight. "Yeah, I mean sometimes the fights are extremely intense over things that I could see on the surface as very subtle debates, almost academic, and then a large number of people don't see it as academic. They do see it like the devil put ideas in your head that are completely wrong. And it's fascinating how that kind of division can emerge." "I have an impossible question, and I apologize for asking it, but maybe it is worthwhile to try to think about it: on the grand scale of centuries of the East Roman Empire, is religion a constructive or a destructive force for the empire? Did it help it flourish, or did it hold it back?" This society identified itself increasingly with what it saw as orthodoxy. Orthodoxy just means correct belief—I mean, like every religion ultimately thinks it's orthodox. Let's—but that's the conventional name, right? Orthodoxy today might be called Greek Orthodoxy, and it's such an essential part of the culture. It really is. I can't think of the two of them independently. I mean, I try to, because as a historian, I have to test the models and try to see these things as independent of each other. But ultimately, it's very difficult to disentangle them, and I would say that, to the degree that this is a successful society historically, its religion definitely contributed to that and played a role in it. Not without cost, right? There are costs. It mattered to them, they valued it, but things that matter to you have a cost, and they paid that price. They were willing to pay that price for it. "Yeah, but there are other layers, like taxation and the functions of government that are connected to religion to the degree it interacts with the tax system. So one of the things that briefly you mentioned, just it'd be nice to elaborate on, is this term of "monarchic republic." So we've been throwing around words like kingdom and republic and empire and monarchy. So what—what—what is the actual... what's a good applicable term to what this thing is, to the East Roman Empire? That's an excellent question, and I'm glad you—you flagged that. Because we call it conventionally "Byzantine Empire" and it's neither of those things, really. We've talked about Byzantine, yeah. Now, "empire" is a bit of a problem because, first of all, there's no term in Greek—in the Greek that they use at this period—that corresponds to our notion of empire. It's not part of the official terms; they didn't even have a term for it. You could have—there's some terms that might mean something like state, *arche*, maybe, or *kratos*, but for the most part, it's either *basileia*, which we might translate as monarchy, or *politeia*, which is polity. And *politeia* in Greek—polity—is a Greek translation of Latin *res publica*. *Res publica* doesn't mean republic the way we mean it for the Romans like a non-monarchic regime, right? That's how we mean it, like the American republic or, you know. That's a usage that came in later, like during the Enlightenment. In Latin, a *res publica* is the common affairs, the common interest of a people who are constituted as a political community regardless of the type of regime that they have. Like, even Cicero could imagine the Roman *res publica* governed by a king, or by a senate, or by a democracy. And they continued to use that term. We don't use it according to Roman norms; we use it according to modern norms—the term "republic." So they continued to call it a *res publica* or a *politeia* under the emperors as well, right? And it was understood that the role of the emperor, whether he's Caesar or Basileus or whatever, whatever language, was to serve the polity. Keep saying this. So the terms that occur in the sources most commonly are *basileia* of the Romans, polity of the Romans, or Romania, which is the proper name of this state. Not empire. Now, empire... Empire in modern English, commonly an empire is a state that's created when one group conquers a bunch of others and rules them. And it could be an ethnic group, it could be an ethno-religious group, it could be a political group, whatever, but there's an act of conquest and results in a state where one group is ruling over others. That's not how Romania is actually constituted. We've talked about this; it's for the most part all Roman citizens ruling them—like, Roman subjects ruling themselves through their own government. So it's not an empire strictly speaking. Now, occasionally they will conquer other people, like we talked about the warfare—sometimes it's not just defensive, sometimes it's offensive, right? In that case, you do establish an imperial relationship, like, "we the Romans are ruling over this other group." It could be Slavs, it could be Bulgarians for a while, it could be whatever, or even some Muslims in Northern Syria for a while in the 10th-11th century who, and you are not Romans. And that's an imperial relationship. Big Tech’s Political Fixer On AI’s Unfixable ‘Darth Vader’ Problem

Semafor(29.0K)·53:46·272 views·2026-06-30
Bradley TuskPolitical Fixer and Venture Capitalist Liz HoffmanHost, Semafor Rohan SilvaHost, Semafor
TL;DRSilicon Valley political fixer Bradley Tusk warns that AI companies face unprecedented, bipartisan regulatory backlash because the public lacks enthusiasm for the technology and sees no "hero" in the space.
Consequently, Tusk is adapting his classic "ask forgiveness, not...

TL;DR

Silicon Valley political fixer Bradley Tusk warns that AI companies face unprecedented, bipartisan regulatory backlash because the public lacks enthusiasm for the technology and sees no "hero" in the space. Consequently, Tusk is adapting his classic "ask forgiveness, not permission" playbook, winding down his traditional venture fund to focus on equity-for-advising deals in highly regulated sectors like AI data center energy and alternative compute.

***

The Complex, Bipartisan Regulatory Threats Facing AI

  • Widespread Public Anxiety: Unlike previous tech waves, AI faces a deep, bipartisan undercurrent of public distrust that is already driving aggressive local and state-level legislative action in both red and blue states (including Texas, Tennessee, Minnesota, Indiana, and New York).
      • Bradley Tusk warns that the current regulatory climate is likely the most permissive AI companies will ever experience, with restrictions only tightening from here.
  • The Four Regulatory Buckets of AI: Tusk advises analyzing and tackling AI policy by segmenting the landscape into four distinct categories:

1. Consumer Protection: Managing the direct human impacts of AI, such as local data center resource strain, AI chatbots, and automated workflows in healthcare, insurance, hiring, and education.

2. Catastrophic Risks: High-profile, existential policy concerns surrounding frontier models.

3. Job Displacement: Short-term economic pain and labor displacement, which Tusk notes will cause severe political friction despite the long-term creation of new industries.

4. Positive Utility: Harnessing AI for high-upside public goods like climate change mitigation, drug discovery, and governmental efficiency.

  • The "Luke Skywalker" vs. "Darth Vader" Dynamic: Tusk argues that AI lacks a clear "hero" to combat entrenched incumbents. He notes that Anthropic is successfully positioning itself as the responsible "Luke Skywalker," while OpenAI has willingly embraced a villainous "Darth Vader" persona.

Why the "Ask Forgiveness, Not Permission" Playbook Fails for AI

  • The Problem of a Utility-Like User Base: Uber succeeded in beating local regulations because its early users found the app "magical" and actively lobbied politicians to keep it. In contrast, Tusk notes that services like ChatGPT are treated like utility companies—widely used, but unloved and incapable of inspiring grassroots political defense.
  • High-Stakes Legal Risks: While early Uber could absorb low-stakes penalties like impounded cars to force its way into markets, the regulatory and compliance risks for frontier AI models are massive and potentially criminal, making a "brute force" strategy highly dangerous.
  • Regulatory Moats as a Defense: Tusk advises best-in-class startups in highly regulated sectors (like healthcare or insurance) to lean into public anxiety by actively lobbying for exceptionally high regulatory standards.
      • By helping write stringent laws that they can meet but competitors cannot, startups can build highly defensive regulatory moats.

The Impending Jurisdictional Battle Over Prediction Markets

  • The FanDuel Playbook: During the 2015 fantasy sports crisis, Tusk kept FanDuel alive by mobilizing passionate, highly targeted user bases in key legislative districts, threatening local politicians with direct electoral consequences if they banned the service.
  • An Impending Supreme Court Showdown: Prediction markets (e.g., Polymarket and Kalshi) face a looming 50/50 jurisdictional battle in the Supreme Court over whether they should be regulated by the federal Commodity Futures Trading Commission (CFTC) or individual state gaming boards.
      • State Jurisdiction Scenario: If the Court hands authority to the states, platforms like Polymarket will face sluggish state gaming boards and will need to launch massive, high-budget user advocacy campaigns to secure licenses.
      • Federal Jurisdiction Scenario: If the CFTC wins sole jurisdiction, traditional sports betting giants may pivot to prediction markets to escape high state taxes, creating massive, unexpected tax revenue deficits for state governments.
  • Underage Betting Safeguards: Watching his own teenage children bypass age gates on sports betting apps led Tusk to work with New York Governor Kathy Hochul on a proposal to mandate biometric face-screening for logging into state-regulated betting platforms.

Tusk's Venture Capital Exit and Focus on the Data Center Energy Crisis

  • Winding Down Tusk Venture Partners: Tusk is winding down his $300 million venture fund to return to his original model of advising early-stage startups in exchange for equity rather than managing LP capital.
      • Tusk cited a dislike for LP relations, board seats, and compliance, noting that mid-sized funds are structurally inefficient because they must return their entire pool of LP capital before general partners see a dollar of carry.
  • The Data Center Energy Backlash: Tusk has created a dedicated investment vertical targeting alternative compute and "behind-the-meter" power sources to capitalize on a growing political crisis: local taxpayers rejecting rate hikes driven by AI data center demands.
      • In major data center hubs like Northern Virginia and Georgia, residents are seeing utility bills rise by 30% to 50%, prompting active voter backlash and political defeats for local leaders.
  • Investing in Nuclear and Alternative Compute: Tusk believes data centers will inevitably be forced to pay for their own power infrastructure. He is backing startups focused on behind-the-meter nuclear reactors and high-efficiency compute methods (such as biological or dendritic computing) to bypass the public grid entirely.

***

Notable Quotes

"It is quite possible that this is the most permissive it's ever going to get for AI regulation, and for at least the foreseeable future, it's going to get tougher and tougher." — Bradley Tusk
"Anthropic... are very much trying to position themselves as the Luke Skywalker to OpenAI's Darth Vader. And I think for some reason, OpenAI has very willingly embraced the Vader role." — Bradley Tusk
"Every policy output is the result of a political input. Every politician makes every decision solely based on the next election and nothing else." — Bradley Tusk
"The notion that everyone will be like, 'Oh, it's so important to me that Sam Altman become a trillionaire that I'm just going to gladly pay an extra $30, $40 a month for my energy bill,' is crazy." — Bradley Tusk

And all of a sudden, the walls caved in and state attorneys general were sending cease-and-desist letters in droves—like dozens of them. I sent Christian a text and an email and said, "Listen, I'm sure you're already doing these things, but if it were me, I would do these five things." And he wrote back and said, "Can you start doing them right now?" And so, we started working on it. At the same time, I had just raised my first venture fund, so we both invested and started working on the underlying issues. And, you know, really what it became was: how do we just stay alive in every single state, because effectively right now they're saying that we're operating illegally? And it was very much the Uber playbook, right? People who did fantasy sports betting—this was before the Supreme Court overturned PASPA, which was the federal prohibition on sports betting, so it was fantasy sports betting—it wasn't nearly as voluminous as the number of people who use Uber, but they're really passionate about it, right? Because now we're talking about something that, for the people who do it, it's not even Uber, which is a lot better than taxi but still just getting you from point A to point B, right? For them, this was like culturally who they were. They're the fans—you know, that's their thing. And so, we went basically state by state, started rallying our customers, and did what we did with Uber and a lot of other companies we've done. This means really having the big, expensive public fight in New York, because you guys, meaning the media, all see that, and then you think that's just what it is, so then you reflect it and that becomes sort of the perception nationally or globally. And so, a mega fight in New York, rallies, all this stuff—that created a perception of, "Shit, this is sort of the next Uber." And it helped that I was the person doing each of them. And then, we would go to any individual state and talk to a state rep or state senator, whoever it was, and say, "Listen, you know, here in your district in Tennessee, between DraftKings and Uber we have, you know, 4,142 customers. Let's be honest, they don't know who you are. They probably don't even know that the state senate is even a thing. But they love fantasy sports betting. And if you take this away from them, we are going to make sure they know who you are, that you did it, that they're registered, and we're going to get them to the polls." And in every single case, fundamentally, the politician in question just said, "You know what? I don't care about fantasy sports betting one way or another, I'm not taking this thing on." And we won, pretty much everywhere. And then, the company itself didn't really kind of explode in value until the Supreme Court decision. I want to ask you about one other project you have going, which is mobile voting. What's going on there? You know, everything I do operates on a pretty simple theory, which is: every policy output is the result of a political input. Every politician makes every decision solely based on the next election and nothing else. And yes, there are exceptions, but by and large, that's pretty much the way that they operate. And so, the only way in my mind to align the public's interest with the politicians' interests is to materially increase turnout, especially in primaries where 90-plus percent of elections are effectively determined. And you're only going to do that by meeting the voters where they are, and that's on their phones. So I started this thing called the Mobile Voting Project. We tested the hypothesis by funding elections in seven different states where either deployed military or people with disabilities were able to vote in real elections on their phones. The answer was yes, of course. When you put something on people's phones and make it easier, people like it and they do it. We then spent five years building mobile voting technology that is end-to-end verifiable, end-to-end encrypted, requires multi-factor authentication and biometric screening, and air gapping. It's all open-source code. It's on GitHub. It is free. It's in the public domain. I don't own it; it is free for anyone to use. So why not take the money that I spent and the work that we've done—since it's already built—and use it to allow people to participate at much higher rates? But there may be more people betting on an election than voting in it in a couple of years. Maybe so, yeah. And like, that's a tremendous shame. And so why not take the technology? Look, I've spent $22 million of my own money so far on this thing. You know, everyone in New York and the business community is freaking out about the election results on Tuesday night, but turnout was really low. And when only the people who are on the far left are the ones voting, of course you're going to lose, right? And you know, either you've got to mobilize your voters to the polls—which they're not able to do—or you've got to make voting easier, and that's mobile voting. Grant Sanderson (@3blue1brown) – AI and the future of math

Dwarkesh Patel(1.4M)·93:39·100.9K views·2026-06-30
Grant SandersonCreator, 3blue1brown Dwarkesh PatelHost, Dwarkesh Patel Podcast
TL;DRAI is advancing rapidly in mathematics due to the domain's unique "grindability" and verifiability, but true mathematical breakthroughs require deep conceptual unification and "mountain building"—skills that will likely shift the human role from raw ...
theorem-proving to...

TL;DR

AI is advancing rapidly in mathematics due to the domain's unique "grindability" and verifiability, but true mathematical breakthroughs require deep conceptual unification and "mountain building"—skills that will likely shift the human role from raw theorem-proving to relational curation, translation, and education.

The "Spiky" Frontier of AI Math: Grindability and the IMO

  • The Jagged Frontier of Math Progress: While AI has made rapid strides in mathematical benchmarks, the progress is highly uneven across different sub-disciplines.
      • IMO Performance: In the 2024 International Math Olympiad (IMO), AI models effectively "cold-solved" geometry in seconds using brute-force solvers. However, they continue to struggle with combinatorics, which consists of playful, puzzle-like problems requiring creative leaps.
  • The Concept of "Grindability": Dwarkesh Patel notes that mathematics and coding are uniquely suited for AI training because they are both highly *verifiable* and *grindable*.
      • The Sandbox Advantage: Unlike physical computer use—where bots get blocked by site security (such as Amazon's bot detectors managed by Andy Jassy) and parallel rollouts are compute-expensive—math and coding can be run in thousands of parallel, deterministic, containerized environments. This solves the "credit assignment problem" because success can be mathematically isolated to specific code diffs or logical steps.
  • Lean vs. Natural Language Verification: While some emphasize formalization languages like Lean, current breakthroughs often occur in natural language.
      • The Case for Lean: Lean allows for the creation of self-improving, forever-running loops. For instance, an AI could be tasked to indefinitely extend `mathlib` (the global repository of formalized math proofs) without human oversight.
      • The Natural Language Alternative: Models like DeepSeek Math bypass formalization by training LLM-as-a-judge verifiers and meta-verifiers to evaluate the accuracy of natural language proofs, which has proven highly effective in recent releases.

The Levels of Genius: Conjectures, Definitions, and Galois Theory

  • The Hierarchy of Mathematical Contributions: Grant Sanderson highlights a popular quote in mathematical circles: *"Good mathematicians prove theorems, great mathematicians come up with conjectures, and the greatest mathematicians come up with definitions."*
      • AI's Current Role: Current AI excels at the bottom tier (proving theorems/finding counterexamples) but has not yet mastered generating definitions that unify or spawn entire fields.
  • Galois Theory and the 100-Year Verification Loop: Sanderson recounts the history of Évariste Galois and group theory to illustrate how the most important mathematical concepts do not yield immediate, verifiable utility.
      • The Quest for the Quintic: While Niels Henrik Abel first proved that quintic equations cannot be solved by radicals, Galois explored the underlying symmetries of polynomial roots, inventing group theory.
      • The Delay in Recognition: At the time, Galois’s papers were rejected by the academy because they lacked traditional, clean proofs. It took decades for Joseph Liouville to decode them, and over a century before physicists like Murray Gell-Mann applied group theory to predict the existence of quarks.
      • The Challenge for RLHF: Because the "reward signal" for a brilliant new definition can take 100 years to manifest, traditional Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF) struggle to incentivize the Galois-like instinct over simple theorem-solving.

Human Understanding and the "Theorem Economy"

  • The Fall of the Theorem Economy: Dwarkesh Patel references David Besis's concept that theorem-proving is historically over-credited compared to definition-creation. If AI automates theorem-proving, humans will be forced to shift their focus toward consolidating proofs into higher-level, intuitive insights.
  • Proof vs. Explanation: Sanderson points to Timothy Chow's paper on "forcing" and the continuum hypothesis to emphasize that a rigorous proof is not the same as a conceptual explanation.
      • Expository Problems: Chow introduced the idea of "unsolved expository problems"—cases where a theorem is mathematically proven, but humanity does not yet understand *why* it is true.
      • The Human Role as Curators: Rather than generating new data, future human mathematicians may act like "art museum curators." They will look at the vast universe of AI-generated proofs and decide which concepts are beautiful, meaningful, and worth presenting to the human collective.
  • Bridging Disparate Fields: AI has a massive structural advantage in bridging disparate fields because of its multi-disciplinary training.
      • The Montgomery-Dyson Conjunction: In the 1970s, number theorist Hugh Montgomery and physicist Freeman Dyson realized over tea that the statistical distribution of the zeros of the Riemann zeta function matched the eigenvalues of random Hermitian matrices in quantum physics.
      • AI as a Lightning Bolt: An AI trained deeply on both analytic number theory and quantum mechanics can find these "lightning bolt" connections systematically, without relying on accidental, real-world social encounters.

The Limits of LLMs: Writing, Theory of Mind, and Education

  • Why AI Struggles with High-Quality Writing: Patel and Sanderson discuss why writing remains highly resistant to LLM automation compared to coding and math.
      • Lack of Modularity: Code is highly modular; a function can be written sloppily but still run successfully to produce a functional end product. In contrast, the writing *is* the end product. Every sentence and word choice directly constitutes the substance.
      • The Botox Metaphor for Theory of Mind: Writing requires predicting the reader's mental state months down the line. Sanderson cites a study showing that individuals who receive Botox injections (which freeze facial muscles) perform worse on emotional reading tests because they can no longer physically mimic and empathize with facial expressions. LLMs, lacking physical "hardware" for human experience, struggle with this deep theory of mind.
      • Failing at Flashcards: Renowned researchers like Andy Matuschak have struggled to train LLMs to write effective spaced-repetition flashcards. A good flashcard requires projecting what a human mind will remember three months from now—a task LLMs fail to model accurately.
  • Career Advice for Aspiring Mathematicians and Coders: Sanderson urges students to look past academic hype and evaluate where real-world value is generated.
      • Follow the Money: Students must ask what value they are adding to a university, firm, or grant committee. Often, prestigious mathematicians are funded for brand value, teaching, or basic-science public relations rather than raw theorem output.
      • Teaching as the Ultimate Safe Haven: Teaching and mentorship are inherently relational and emotional activities. Even if AI can explain a concept perfectly, parents will always pay a premium for human coaching, making education one of the most stable post-AGI career paths.

Notable Quotes

"Good mathematicians prove theorems, great mathematicians come up with conjectures, and the greatest mathematicians come up with definitions." — *Grant Sanderson (quoting a mathematical maxim)*
"There is a difference between proof and explanation." — *Grant Sanderson*
"What mathematicians will end up being is actually more analogous to art museum curators than anything else... You still want someone to help you navigate in this nearly infinite space of what ideas are worth engaging with." — *Grant Sanderson*
"Teaching is one of the most stable post-AGI jobs that there is, because it's so relational. Even if LLMs are good explainers, the thing that a teacher is doing is a social, coaching, mentor-type thing." — *Grant Sanderson*

He was studying the question and he recognized being able to solve these polynomials is actually very related to understanding the way that certain algebraic expressions are symmetric, like more or less so. Like if I write down a + b + c + d, just adding four variables, if I permute those, it doesn't change the value of the expression. Whereas if I write like (a + b) multiplied by (c + d), some of the permutations don't change it, but some of them do. And he had this really, really nice insight about how if you can find expressions like this that have four free variables but all the permutations take on three distinct values, that had this unexpected relationship with being able to reduce degree four into degree three. So he started approaching the "can we find a quintic polynomial" by saying, "Hmm, I wonder if I can extend that." And to extend that method, you would have to have an expression that has five free variables such that as you permute them over all the 5! permutations, it takes on only four values or fewer. So that's like you could put that in a puzzle book, you could put that in a brain teaser that a 12-year-old could engage with, and it's not too hard to find yourself feeling like that's an impossible task. And so Lagrange is sitting here saying, "Hmm, here's a strategy that I'm trying to solve this problem: can I find a quintic polynomial? This strategy doesn't... it seems like it might be impossible, at least from this strategy." But that was the first time in history that people had the instinct that some kind of question about symmetry was the right way to be studying these polynomials. In his mind it was just a way—it had yet to be discovered that actually there's a tighter connection. And also, maybe rather than searching for the formula, we should be asking the opposite question: can you prove that it's impossible? So he sort of planted that seed. There's never like he solved the problem and therefore we know that that was the right question to ask. He asked it; there's some intrinsically interesting thing. It also wasn't very important for math at the time—like most people were more interested in the applications to physics. This is almost in that side, almost recreational, hobbyist-type thing. Like Abel, you know, he started working on quintic stuff but then he was advised to spend more of his effort studying elliptic functions, and so more of his work was on that before he died young at 26 from tuberculosis. And then Galois, he pushed both of those ideas in the right direction where he really understood the nature of abstraction. So he had this really nice piece that he wrote while he was in prison actually—we could talk all about his life story, it's pretty wild—but he's like this teenager, he's in prison, he had tried to submit his math papers and they had been rejected. And so, again, it's like verifiable reward—the verifier function that is the academy at that time is rejecting what he wrote because, frankly, it was not very coherent. It wasn't a complete proof, he wasn't giving like a clear thought of what the theory actually was, he was just like a young fledgling mathematician getting his bearings. It's like, well, Abel proved that quintics are unsolvable. And you say, "What was Galois doing?" Well, in principle, the thing that Galois theory will let you do is take a specific polynomial and it gives you the rules to say, "Does that specific polynomial have roots that you could write down?" For example, like x^5 - 1, you know that a solution is 1, or x^5 - 2, you can write down the 5th root of 2. So it's not that for every quintic polynomial you can't write down the solution. The Two Harvard Dropouts Who raised $800M to take on NVIDIA

Invest Like The Best(79.7K)·92:32·18.8K views·2026-06-30
Gavin UbertiCo-founder, Etched Robert WachenCo-founder, Etched
TL;DREtched, co-founded by Harvard dropouts Gavin Uberti and Robert Wachen, has raised $800M to challenge NVIDIA's hardware dominance with a specialized, rack-scale inference architecture.
By betting on low-voltage processing and custom cluster-scale memory interconnects,...

TL;DR

Etched, co-founded by Harvard dropouts Gavin Uberti and Robert Wachen, has raised $800M to challenge NVIDIA's hardware dominance with a specialized, rack-scale inference architecture. By betting on low-voltage processing and custom cluster-scale memory interconnects, their first-generation "Sohu" system delivers up to 160x the throughput of legacy GPUs for modern transformer models under the same power budget.

The Technical Bets: Low-Voltage Inference and Cluster-Scale Memory

  • Prefill and Decode Disaggregation: To optimize performance, Etched separates the two phases of LLM inference into dedicated compute clusters.
      • Prefill Cluster: Specialized in reading input data and preparing the model’s Key-Value (KV) cache.
      • Decode Cluster: Receives the KV cache to rapidly generate subsequent output tokens.
  • Low-Voltage Inference: Etched tackles the thermal throttling limitations of modern GPUs by dramatically lowering operating voltages.
      • Dennard Scaling: Because voltage is quadratically proportional to power, cutting voltage in half reduces power draw to one-quarter, allowing more flops to be packed onto the silicon without overheating.
      • Precedent: The team borrowed low-voltage design principles from Bitcoin mining chips, allowing their first-generation hardware to run at less than half the voltage of standard AI chips.
  • Cluster-Scale Memory: To eliminate memory bandwidth bottlenecks during token generation, Etched treats the memory of an entire cluster as a single pool.
      • Custom Interconnect: While modern architectures like NVIDIA's Blackwell experience up to 4,000 nanoseconds of point-to-point latency, Etched built a custom interconnect stack that cuts latency by more than 5x, unlocking massive parallel scaling.
  • Sohu Performance: Designed specifically for post-ChatGPT transformer models, a single rack of Etched "Sohu" processors can serve Llama 3 70B to as many concurrent users as 160 H100 GPUs under the same power budget.

Vertical Integration and Velocity Over Everything

  • The Product is the Rack: Rather than just selling silicon, Etched vertically integrates the entire system—including the chip, board design, custom cold plates, interconnects, power delivery, and cabinet assembly.
  • Extreme Parallelization: The team parallelizes hardware and software schedules to avoid the lengthy delays typical of semiconductor launches.
      • Thermal Emulation: Etched built specialized thermal chips mimicking exact transistor hotspots to validate their liquid-cooling cold plates and racks before real silicon arrived.
      • FPGA Validation: The software stack was fully built and tested running models across a cluster of 700 FPGAs ahead of chip delivery.
      • Rapid Bring-Up: Thanks to this pre-fetching strategy and 24/7 engineering shifts, Etched went from receiving their first silicon to running live rack-scale inference in just 40 days—compared to an industry average of 10 months.
  • The Bangalore Intervention: Faced with a critical physical-design vendor falling a year behind schedule, Etched bypassed remote delays by shipping a dozen core engineers to Bangalore, India for several months. By running continuous 24-hour handoff cycles with the US team, they successfully taped out the chip on schedule.

Talent Strategy: "Legends" and First-Principles Builders

  • Bimodal Talent Philosophy: Etched recruits using a strict two-pronged approach, pairing elite industry veterans with hungry, highly motivated young engineers.
  • The "Legends" Layer: Etched targets the world’s absolute top technical domain experts for zero-to-one problems.
      • Mark Ross: The former CTO of Cypress Semi (which sold for $9B) initially dismissed the founders' dorm-room pitch but joined as full-time CTO after auditing their functional simulation.
      • Brian Loyler: The creator of NVIDIA’s multi-billion dollar HGX and DGX systems was recruited to design and build Etched's entire rack-scale infrastructure.
  • The "Chips on Shoulders" Layer: Young, highly driven talent is brought in to aggressively challenge industry assumptions.
      • Sanford: Gavin's high school robotics partner was brought in to rapidly design and pressure-test the company’s first thermal cold plates in a fraction of the standard industry timeline.
  • Mission-Driven Self-Selection: The company's contrarian goal of beating NVIDIA filters out passive hires. The difficulty of the task naturally attracts talent willing to relocate to San Jose and work intense schedules to prove the architecture’s feasibility.

The Macro Outlook: The Token Economy and Gigawatt Scale

  • Tokens as the Primary Metric: Etched operates on the thesis that the most valuable company in the future will be the one that can manufacture and deliver the highest volume of cheap, high-speed tokens.
  • Accelerating Long-Horizon Agents: Drastically reducing the cost and latency of inference allows agentic workflows to run longer and more complex chain-of-thought loops. Tasks that currently require six months of wall-clock time can be compressed into a single month or days.
  • The Agentic Workforce Shift: Robert Wachen predicts that by 2027, there will be more active AI agents doing knowledge work than humans, shifting national productivity metrics from "GDP per capita" to "agents per megawatt."
  • Smart Supply Chain Allocation: To guarantee physical availability, Etched strategically designed its first-generation products around a 4nm TSMC node and alternative HBM supply lines, bypassing the highly congested 3nm nodes and packaging steps utilized by next-generation GPUs.

<blockquote>"We know inference is going to be the biggest market in the world. Whoever produces the most tokens is going to be the most valuable company in the world." <br>— Robert Wachen</blockquote>

<blockquote>"Ultimately, this is our product. It is completely unsurprising that the best chip in the world is built by a company that only builds that chip." <br>— Robert Wachen</blockquote>

<blockquote>"If you want to go ahead and be way better than everything else that’s out there today, we can't do things the same way. We have to be willing to go out and throw out the old rules, throw out the old ways of doing things, and say, 'How can we do this better?'" <br>— Gavin Uberti</blockquote>

We know inference is going to be the biggest market in the world. Whoever produces the most tokens is going to be the most valuable company in the world. We had people quit. People literally were like, "This problem is unsolvable, and best of luck, guys." You kind of have to be sick in the head to join our company. You're going to convince your family to move to San Jose for a semiconductor company run by two, what, 24-year-olds now? You are going against the biggest companies in the world with a design that they're saying is not going to be like 10% better, but is going to be 10x better. We need a hundred million dollars to do this. If we do this, we think this could be one of the most important companies of all time. You're sitting in that moment and you think, "Holy crap, we can't afford this." And I'd be looking at it like, "Huh, how hard is it to go back to Harvard?" All right, gentlemen. It's been three years or so, Gavin, since you and I last did this, which is nuts. At the time, I was just wildly intrigued by your story and what you were going to build. I didn't know a lot about chips at the time. I was considering investing in the company, so I was calling everyone I could conceive of who could give me an opinion. At the time, basically, the consensus was that these kinds of companies are not built by young people. In the semis world, the best companies are founded by 40- or 50-year-old people who have had a whole career's worth of experience, have learned all the problems, and have shipped multiple chips. People thought two 21-year-olds were just not going to do this, that it was just not going to work. It was indicative of a theme, which was "nobody believes in us." That's obviously changed a lot now. You know, just walk the halls and talk to the people who have chosen to come work here. But in the early days, it felt like this was something that you had to face down. What was that like, facing down a set of incumbents and an industry worth of people and investors who sort of didn't believe in you? What did that anneal in you to build the company the way that you are? What was the impact of that? I think there's a certain level of naivety required to think that you could build a chip better than every other AI chip ever built, and build a company to do it way faster than it has ever been done. We have that naivety. There were many times where we would say, "Why isn't this possible?" and really push on it. It turns out that everybody's answers are extremely siloed to a set of constraints that aren't true anymore. The reality is the entire semiconductors and data center industry is built on buffer. What I mean by that is every part of the stack, from the EDA tools to the power modules to the circuit boards to the chip design and standard cells, is built to be general-purpose. Everything is made for IoT, the edge, and so forth, not just the data center. When you have a specific use case you're really trying to design for, you can change the constraints a lot. I'll give you just a very simple example that we're not the only ones to exploit, which is the clock speed of your chip. Clock speed is proportional to the throughput of your system. When you are doing sign-off for different timing to see what clock speed you're actually going to be able to run on when you tape out your chip, there's a concept called corners. This refers to what temperatures you are going to be able to run at for a given clock speed. The default configurations for a lot of these EDA tools assume that you're going to be running your chips in freezing temperatures. Now, I don't know about you, but I've never seen an AI data center with ice in it. So, we can feel pretty confident that our chips don't need to run at full speed at zero degrees Celsius. In fact, they're never really going to be running below 80 degrees Celsius anyway. Just by knowing that that is a constraint that doesn't matter, we can make a ton of changes throughout the entire system. That is a very simple example, but there are many more where you get 20% here, 50% there, 2x here. These compound into a system that can be radically better for inference. Well, I think you found two kinds of people. There are some folks who went purely on heuristics, thinking, "Okay, young founders, they claim they can go beat the biggest company in the world on performance. It cannot happen, and there is nothing you could say to me that would make me change my mind." But there are also people out there who were of course skeptical, but were willing to say, "I'll spend the time. I'll do the work. Is it actually possible?" For example, one of our earliest supporters was a guy named Mark Ross. Mark was a very prestigious semiconductor expert, the former CTO at Cypress Semi, which sold for nine billion dollars. When we met him, we were just a couple of guys in a dorm room. We came to him and said, "Hey, we want to go build hardware for inference. We think we can be much faster than NVIDIA." Mark said, "No, you can't. It will not work. But if you want to convince me, you should write a white paper. You should build a functional simulation and show me." So after a lot of very long nights, we went back to Mark and said, "Hey, here's a simulation. What do you think?" He was like, "Huh. This works. But to build a company like this, you'll need a huge amount of capital—at least three million dollars even to get started." We ended up raising five million, and a lot more after that. He was again surprised, but got more involved. He became an advisor, then a half-time advisor, and eventually our full-time CTO as he saw more of the development progress. I think, in general, this skepticism has filtered really heavily for folks who want to be right regardless, versus folks who are willing to be very truth-seeking. The latter say, "Sure, I'm skeptical, but I will go work through the numbers myself. And if I can figure out why this is possible, well, let's go build it." The specifics that you've made bets on, the way that you've built this system, are immensely interesting to me. Because so many people are trying to do this now—build new chips that will do a better job of serving inference at massive scale—the world is interested in the research approaches and the different architectural paths people are taking. I'd love you to start by describing what this thing is and what it does. Perhaps more interestingly and more importantly, what was the process you went through to decide what bets to take and what technologies to invent? How do those compare and contrast with what you've seen the rest of the marketplace try to do? Yeah, I think to start with the product, we're not just building a chip. We're building a full inference solution, and that means a rack. It means the chip, the power delivery into the chip, the board on which it sits, the interconnect by which the chips talk to each other, and the production for this mass volume of racks. Really, the production is the product. When we think about how we get our advantage, there are two key parts of running inference: there's prefill, and there's decode. We have two key tech bets for both of these things. Prefill is reading in a huge volume of text, and decode is using that data to generate output tokens. When you run prefill, your key job is not to predict tokens; you already know the text. Your job is to get the model's memory, what we call the KV cache, into the right state. Then you can run decode with that same KV cache. So what we will often do is what we call PD disaggregation—prefill-decode disaggregation. You will have one cluster of servers running these prefills. You'll then transfer those model memories, those KV caches, over to the decode cluster and use that cluster to generate the next tokens. So this is sort of like loading the gun and then firing it, if I think about it in super simple terms. You got it. It's getting the models to remember the right things and then using those things to go do tasks. Generally, people think about this market a bit lazily. They say, "Are you a prefill chip? Are you a decode chip? If you're a decode chip, are you an HBM chip? Are you an SRAM chip? Are you a 3D DRAM chip? Are you using optics? Are you using copper?" When we started this, we just wanted to understand why extremely smart people were working on these different directions. We seriously looked at architectures like having a bunch of DDR memory in a shared memory pool, and we looked at advanced packaging to basically break out of the shoreline. We looked at things like putting memory dies on top of compute dies. In doing so, we realized that there is no free lunch. Everything has a tradeoff. With 3D DRAM, you have a thermal issue, a supply chain issue, and you have to figure out hybrid bonding and flops—so now you're a decode chip. We went through everything on both the prefill and the decode side. We realized there were a few design spaces that nobody had seriously tried to explore because they were never done in AI chips. We asked ourselves, what are the actual metrics that are going to matter the most? On the prefill side, the thing that matters is flops, or flops density. People talk about flops often as a headline number. In reality, you should care about the flops you're getting when you are running real workloads. There's a concept called MFU, or model flops utilization. For every peak flop advertised, how many cents on the dollar are you actually getting? On GPUs, you often get somewhere between 20 and 50 percent, depending on the workload. You actually can provably not run at 100 percent because you have a thermal issue. As you increase the flop utilization, you have more transistors going on and off, drawing more power. The chip will self-regulate and lower its clock speed to make sure it doesn't overheat. As we looked at inference, we said, "If we want way more flops because we want to run at way higher throughputs, we fundamentally need to solve the thermal problem before we even think about adding flops to the chip." If I just add more flops to a GPU today or another AI chip, I'm not actually going to get more performance because it's just going to thermal throttle. Fundamentally, the essence of that is Dennard scaling: voltage is quadratically proportional to power. If I 2x my voltage, my power goes up by 4x. If I cut my voltage in half, my power goes down by a quarter. So we asked ourselves, "How could we run voltages lower than GPUs?" We talked to a lot of people about this. We flew out to Silicon Valley after dropping out and asked dozens of people in semiconductors at all these different chip companies how they did it. The answer we got was, "You can't. You can't run at voltages lower than GPUs." This was very dissatisfying because there are many different industries of chips that run at voltages lower than GPUs. Bitcoin miners run at under a quarter of the voltage of GPUs, so this is obviously physically possible. The question is, are there issues with GPU architectures that make them unable to run at these voltages? When we looked at the problem for a long time, we were able to create a new mechanism of running at much lower voltages—a new type of power delivery that we call low-voltage inference. We think all AI chips in the future are going to be low-voltage chips. They are going to have to cram way more flops in the same silicon area and, without thermal throttling, run at way lower voltages. So that's prefill. For decode, it is all a memory game. With more memory bandwidth, you can load the model faster, load the KV cache faster, and serve more tokens per second per user. We think people ask the wrong question here. People often ask, "How much memory bandwidth is on your chip?" You should be asking, "How much memory bandwidth is on your full scale-up cluster?" What we were able to do is add way more bandwidth and a much lower latency from chip to chip to our interconnect. It allows us to serve models at this much higher speed because you can use the SRAM and the HBM from the full scale-up cluster as a single pool. That is our second key technical bet, which we call cluster-scale memory. On GPUs today, the cluster memory bandwidth is often very badly utilized because the time to hop from one GPU to another is extremely long. For example, on Blackwell chips, it can be about 4,000 nanoseconds to go point-to-point. That means if you go to an 8x TP setup, you will get way less than an 8x improvement in your tokens per second per user. What we did is build our own totally custom interconnect stack. We took everything above the second-layer Ethernet and built it full custom. We can achieve far better latencies and bandwidths this way. We can cut this by more than a factor of 5x, which allows us to use the memory of other chips much more effectively. As you scale the world size, your time per token goes down proportionally. It is not that surprising, given all these architectures were built before ChatGPT. If we are trying to build a chip for modern workloads, it is going to look very different. The way we organize our flops, the way we do our voltage domains, and the way we do our power planes are going to look super different. The way we do the packaging is going to look super different. The way we do the board design is going to look different. On the decode side, the way we connect everything is going to look very different. We're now bringing forward our first generation of this low-voltage inference technology, which is running at under half the voltage of any other AI chip.
