Learn more at rogo.ai/felix. If you zoom all the way out, why is this so important? Why is the delivery of much higher throughput, much lower cost per token, and better tokens per watt—all of these metrics that the universe is going to start talking about more and more—the critical path? Everyone knows the supply side of the equation is a big problem right now. Why is this, looking at the bigger picture over a decade, the bottleneck in the technology world? Well, I think it comes down to productivity. We are at this extremely interesting moment in the history of civilization where there is real artificial intelligence. It's not like sci-fi stuff; these models can solve problems that most humans can't. It's going to create new scientific discoveries, instant access to medical care, and instant access to education. Now it's just about how many people can use this at the same time, how many products can serve this concurrently, and also the speed of doing different tasks. When you think about wall-clock time, consider an agent that runs at a certain model quality and takes a year to solve a task using inference-time compute. If you have way faster decode speed, you can compress that into a month. Because of that, the amount of scientific innovation and the actual proliferation of technology will happen much faster. The second part is concurrency. Today, it is just not possible for a billion people to use these models concurrently. Ultimately, some people are going to get downgraded, some people's models are going to be slower, and some people just won't be able to access the hardware. In a few years, there are going to be giant models serving billions of users. We're very much in the early innings of AI today, where only a few million users in the world use paid plans for AI models. We're at 1/1000th of the global population actually using this stuff. If you want to serve at a giant scale, a lot of things change. One of them is the number of chips that communicate together. People usually think about this in the context of training, where you have these giant training clusters, like Colossus with over a hundred thousand GPUs all networked together. On the inference side, today people usually think about it as an eight-chip cluster, or maybe just NVL72 as the scale-up domain. But very quickly, this is going to become thousands of chips and tens of thousands of chips. The way to get the most performance there relies on the time between sending data from one chip to another. That primitive matters way more than it is getting credit for right now. When we think about optimizing memory bandwidth for this system, you have to think about how fast these chips can communicate together. If they can only communicate really quickly with themselves and very slowly with other chips, you're not going to actually be able to serve giant models at 10,000 or 20,000 tokens per second. We need multiple orders of magnitude of infrastructure built out throughout the entire stack—from the wafer to the watt, from the transistor to the token—to actually bring this stuff to the world. If you look at most other goods—like the iPhone, for example—they've gotten to these economies of scale where, as a result, more money does not really buy a better iPhone. Whether you are a billionaire or just the average American, you buy the same phone. Tokens aren't like that yet. We are still in the very early days where a relatively small, general-purpose system is kind of hand-crafting each token, like they made screws back in the Renaissance. I want to live in a world where you have the same economies of scale for token making that you do for making iPhones, cars, or anything else. I think that is one of the huge unlocks that allows a massive group of people to use the best quality models. Economies of scale have made capitalism very, I don't know, fair. It allows you to have the same product in many, many different hands. If you are able to serve way more users on a single scale-up cluster, it allows you to get closer to that point for token serving, too. Yeah, and also just certain products aren't usable if they're slow. If you want to serve coding models and you want people to actually use them, there's a certain number of tokens per second you need to hit. So the question is: while maintaining that per-token speed, how many users can I serve at the same time? You can basically decide, "I'm going to shut off a bunch of the world from using this stuff," or "everyone's going to get a worse experience." Fundamentally, you need to find ways to push out the curve, and that's why there's such a pressure for new hardware. I'd like to take some time to step back and hear both of your stories for how you came to this idea and this company, and kind of walk through what it's been like to build it. In doing so, we'll understand the system that you've built for the company itself, which will then be able to power subsequent generations of products like this one for this crazy inference future we're staring down. Rob, maybe starting with you. Just take it however far back you want. I'm curious because your personal story was actually the very first thing I ever heard from either one of you, many years ago now, and it really blew me away. I'm most interested in your motivation, ultimately, for being here doing this thing. It starts back in high school for me. I've been very unlucky and lucky at different points in life, and this was one of the tougher times. At the end of my sophomore year of high school, I got injured at a martial arts tournament. The next day, I couldn't walk for some reason. They thought it was something wrong with my SI joint or something. I went through physical therapy, I did different types of scans, and they couldn't figure it out. Eventually, they found this big bump on my back in an MRI and told me it was a tumor. It was stage four bone cancer. I was told I had under a 30% chance of survival. It was like a two-year, crazy chemotherapy, surgery, and learning to walk again experience. When you go through something like that, it really changes the Overton window of human experience and makes you appreciate what actually matters. You also ask yourself what you are going to do if you have the chance to live. If you actually want to get through something like that, you need to be hoping for something. I always knew I wanted to do something very impactful if I had the chance to get through it. It took me a couple of years to figure out what that was going to be. As I got to college and met a bunch of other people building cool tech, I got extremely excited by AI models, especially once GPT-3 came out. I was like, "Wow, this is the first model that can kind of speak English." And these things are going to get really smart. What happened was, when GPT-4 came out, there was GPT-4V, which was the first model with image upload. I went through my camera roll and found a picture of my back with this bump on it before I was diagnosed. I said, "Hey, ChatGPT, pretend you're an expert doctor. A patient comes in and says they have this bump on their back. What could it be?" It immediately said, "This could be a tumor. You should get an MRI immediately. Go to the doctor." And I just kind of sat there still. This took six months. Yeah, that took me six months. Yesterday, this feature wasn't there. Today, it's here. I went to show my parents and I got this notification saying, "You're all out of image credits today. You need to get a pro plan." I was like, "Holy crap. This is going to change everything." We clearly don't have the infrastructure to serve it. There are very few things you could work on that can actually bring this technology at scale to the world faster. There are plenty of people who are super smart working on models, and the fabs seem maybe unreachable to work on. But it seemed like the hardware was all designed before ChatGPT. Every GPU, every TPU, every AI chip that was serving these models was fundamentally built before this and was retrofit to serve these modern models. There's going to be an entire new wave of architectures coming out. What a more exciting thing to work on than bringing this to everybody? From a very different angle at the same time, I was running a startup incubator called Prod, which has incubated a bunch of different companies—some of the earliest ones being Cursor, Anyscale (which merged), Merqor, Etched, and a handful of others. As these models were getting smarter in 2022, I realized all of these companies were spending all the money they raised on compute. I had this realization as I was working on some of my own stuff: "Oh my god, all the products I want to build are going to cost tens of millions of dollars a year in inference." This was not going to be tenable. The cost structure of every software company and the COGS was not going to be zero anymore for an incremental user. It's going to be quite high, and it's going to be a function of inference. The OPEX of every business is also going to be inference as people use more and more coding agents. Fundamentally, it seems like inference is going to be really important. It feels like we are on a decade-long march for inference to become the biggest market in the world. When you think about it, ten years from now there are going to be these giant projects where everything in that data center fundamentally hasn't been designed today. We felt like we should go pick something and work on it. That is kind of how it got started. Gavin, I'm really excited for you to go back about as far—probably early in high school, maybe even earlier—and tell your favorite hash marks on the timeline that ultimately led to your ambition to drop out of Harvard and start this company. My first job ever was at a company called Xnor, where I did kernels development. I was 17, and a 17-year-old can't sign legally binding contracts. So rather than do a traditional CIIA, they sat me down and said, "Gavin, don't share this information." Xnor was one of the only companies that saw, "Hey, maybe this is a good trade," and I got to work building kernels. I've done this at a number of other companies since. Xnor got bought by Apple for 200 million. I did the same thing at Octo, which got bought by NVIDIA for hundreds of millions of dollars. When you do this sort of kernels work, what you realize is that the math is relatively easy. To get high-speed decode, the thing that matters is data movement. Almost all the work you do is optimizing how you move data around a single chip or across multiple chips. That is why we went ahead and built this cluster-scale memory tech. We bring that interconnect time way, way lower. You can do way more data movement and, as a result, get a much faster time to generate each subsequent token. This lets us build these crazy things Rob was talking about for doing a year's worth of work in a month, or even more than that in the future. Can you talk about the competitive drive that's evident in some of the high school competitions that you participated in and won? We did a couple. For example, I was very active in FTC robotics. I was lucky to have a very talented partner, Sanford. For a long time, we were part of our traditional school team, which had about 20 guys all working together, as is often typical in the FIRST Tech Challenge. The goal is to go out and build a robot that scores the most points, along with a bunch of other things. In FIRST, they put a lot of emphasis around collaborating with other teams, trying to do really good documentation, and trying to get others inspired to do the same thing. Sanford and I decided that rather than doing it this way, we were going to win. We did nothing else besides build the robot that scored the most points. As a two-person team. As a two-person team, rather than a 20-person team. We were much, much smaller than almost every other team in the competition. But we figured that if we were going to specialize, if we were going to go out and win the games really well, we wouldn't need to advance based on the quality of our documentation or our outreach. We were just going to go win. So we did. We branched off, built this two-person team, built a robot, and decided we were going to redesign it every three months. We actually had the world record for the highest score during this competition at one point, and we were rated by OPR third in the world for software development. It was a damn good machine. What from that episode can I translate as an analogy onto how you've built Etched the company? When thinking about how you want to go ahead and do a full rack-scale product like this, there's a couple of key ideas. One of them is velocity, velocity, velocity. You win by shipping. You're not going to go out and win by having the best outreach or the best communications. You win by having the best product. Which you've chosen—to have no communications—exactly like your... I'm just realizing how exactly like the robotics thing that is. There are many ways you can win in business, but we'd rather focus on just building the best product. Similarly, we think we can do it with a lot fewer folks. If you're willing to just focus on product, product, product and parallelize relentlessly, you don't need 20,000 people like the big companies have. You can make the best product in the world with far fewer people. Yeah, there's a saying: "The best part is no part." I think for us, it's also "the best vendor is no vendor." As much as possible, we want to vertically integrate the entire product, both because we get more performance and because we can move way faster. Everything from the chips to the boards, to the cold plates, to the interconnects, to even the production—we want to do all of it as in-house as possible. We are actually, I think, the only startup right now that is building its own rack as well as its own chips, and we did it all at the same time. A couple of years ago was the last time we were public. At that point, we had just started building our rack team. We brought over Brian Loyler, who built all of NVIDIA's HGX and DGX systems, which is about 80% of their revenue. We said, "We're going to build the rack at the same time." We actually went through multiple iterations of the rack before the chips even came back. Before they came back, we made thermal chips that had the exact same hotspots we expected our actual chips to have so we could build the cold plates. We could over-pressurize them and blow them up. We haven't had a single leak since our chips came back with the cold plates because we had already validated them. We have a factory in Taiwan and a few dozen people out there. We built a clone of a bunch of the test stations in our office, and we have a two-megawatt data center on this floor. We did 24/7 development cycles where people did day shifts and night shifts to get the hardware up and running as quickly as possible. It is that extreme vertical integration and extreme parallelization of the schedule that lets you get products to market way faster. If you think about the building of the early team and what it required, as two young guys building this company—there are lots of very talented young entrepreneurs out there, maybe for the first time of this scope or magnitude in a long time. All of them could probably benefit from the lessons that you've learned getting very sophisticated, talented people to come join you, even after careers at other great companies. If you were teaching this as a class—like, "Here's how to get elite talent when you're young, inexperienced, and naive"—what would be the syllabus? We have a pretty bimodal talent philosophy. It starts with what we call the "legends." When we're trying to solve an incredibly hard technical problem and do something that hasn't been done before, we need to find the very best person in the world. Often, the difference between the number one guy in the world versus the number 10 guy versus the number 100 guy is a huge factor in whether it's actually possible to solve the problem. So we created a system we call project-based recruiting. We map out all of the hardest technical problems across all industries that anyone has ever had to solve. We look at temporality: who are the people who did the zero-to-one? Who was "in charge," quote-unquote? Who actually did the work? We talk to as many people as possible, and then we just track it. You'd be surprised: the number of people who will say yes after the first conversation is pretty low, but the number of people who say yes after the 20th conversation is surprisingly high. You really have to keep at them. When you hear "no" from somebody who really is the best in the world, that usually just means, "Hey, you should come back when you have a few more milestones proven out." I think one of the most convincing things to see is when we make bold claims, and then we go ahead and hit them again and again and again. That is really belief-inspiring. When we decided we wanted to build a rack and not just a chip, we looked at the market and asked, "How many products have actually shipped at scale for a rack-scale system that have the power density we're trying to solve?" We said, "If we were going to wave a magic wand, what would the best possible person in the world look like?" We realized we wanted someone who started at NVIDIA, built the entire rack team through all its different generations, learned all this stuff, but is still scrappy, still understands startup culture, and has seen scale. That would be the best possible person. So we mapped all of the different teams related to all the different rack-scale products at NVIDIA. We found three people we thought could fit the bill. We talked to all of them; two of them had just retired, and one of whom was planning to do one more generation for NVIDIA and then retire. His name is Brian. Over time, we convinced him to join. Brian started the HGX and DGX team at NVIDIA, which represents a majority of NVIDIA's revenue—tens of billions of dollars a quarter. The other two guys ended up investing, by the way. When you have somebody like that, they just know what good looks like because they've seen it. There are so many times we would talk to Brian and he'd just point and say, "That's a billion-dollar lesson I learned. Billion-dollar lesson I learned." That just saves us cycles. And then you pair someone like Brian with someone like Sanford. Do you have a name for them? So Brian's a "legend." What is Sanford? We say, "Chips on shoulders put chips in data centers." Sanford and Gavin in high school were world robotics champions. Sanford was finishing his senior year of college, and we called him up a couple of years ago. We said, "Hey, can you come check out what we're doing? We need some help on the platform side." He came for a week and we said, "Can you build the cold plate this week?" If you asked any thermal engineer about that, they would think you're just totally naive. These things take months to do—and to be clear, they do. But you can make real progress in a week if you put your mind to it and think it's possible. He built a contraption in a week that actually derisked a pretty key power question we had. You put those two together and they've done incredible things. One is not possible without the other. You need the extremely driven people who just keep asking "why" and don't know where the bodies are buried to take tons of aggressive risks. Then you need the people who have seen scale and still have the startup scrappy mentality to help them along the way. It's really the combination of the "legends" plus the raw, somewhat naive, first-principles-type talent. It's not just that you have both in the company, but that they're working together. That's right. If I think about that funnel, is there anything else interesting to say about how much better you've gotten at recruiting and why those metrics keep getting better? One of the shocking things is that Etched being such a contrarian bet kind of self-selects. If you're the kind of person who is somewhat opportunistic, who is going to go join whatever the hot company is rather than do deep diligence, you will not come work here. It is one of the things I worry about as we announce more of the product and the specs; we may lose some of this if we're not very careful. You kind of have to be sick in the head to join our company when you look at it on paper. You're a person who is probably a very accomplished engineer, making a good amount of money that is liquid and predictable somewhere else. You're going to convince your family to move to San Jose to live in an apartment on a housing program for a semiconductor company run by two 24-year-olds. You are pre-product, going against the biggest companies in the world in the most supply-constrained environment ever created, with a design that you claim is not going to be 10% better, but 10x better. Something must be wrong with you to do that. People are just wired differently here. They really want to prove the people right who do believe, rather than just proving the skeptics wrong. They take it personally. It's really fun to find those people, and frankly, the very nature of the company makes it easy to weed out the people who aren't like that. One of the very first things you and I talked about, Rob, was when I started asking about Sohu, which is the name of the first product here. You said, "We could talk about that in great detail, but the thing you should know is that we're really focused on building a machine that can produce these things at scale, generation after generation, as efficiently and at the highest possible quality level." We want to build the company itself as a machine that will produce this product and then subsequent things. I'd like to talk about a few principles or cornerstones of the company. You've alluded to some of them already: velocity and vertical integration. These have become more popular topics, but parallelization is something we should also discuss. I'm especially interested in your willingness to take huge risks to go faster. Maybe you could tell your favorite story about why this is the philosophy, and what it has allowed you to do that other companies haven't done. There are a number of stories here, but one of my favorites happened when we were getting close to taping out the chip. We realized, "Wait a minute, one of our vendors is way behind schedule." We had two very bad options. One option was to keep the current vendor and push our timelines out by about a year. Another option was to switch vendors, start over, and also push our timelines out by a year. Neither of these was a good option, so we had to look for option number three. We figured out that the team actually doing the work was in Bangalore. So, we shipped a dozen of our top engineers across the world to Bangalore for six months. I was there as well; I lived in Bangalore for four and a half months personally. Every morning, we'd walk across the crazy, busy Bangalore streets into the office. We'd be the first ones in. We built a wide variety of tools, auditing a huge amount of the code that was going in and building tools to make the process go even faster. We made the right design decisions on the spot, right then and there. There was no 12-hour back-and-forth; we could decide immediately. Then, at 2:00 a.m., we'd walk back through the now-empty Bangalore streets and do it all again the next day. We still had a bunch of the team in the U.S. We ran 12-hour handoffs on each side, maintaining a 24-hour development cycle. At 9:00 a.m. and 9:00 p.m. every day, we'd all get on Zoom, share all the data, and say, "When I wake up, this must be done. We must get this chip out." It was extremely intense. At the same time, we saw other chips at the same stage as us with that same vendor that ended up taking years. They still aren't out today; they haven't even taped out. It's that level of extreme urgency that is required to bring products to market. What is the key to doing this well? This has become a trope, mostly because of Elon. His special skill, which others who seek to emulate him try to do as well, is figuring out what the binding constraint is and then flooding the zone personally on that thing—which is kind of like going to Bangalore. It seems like this is a central tenet of your business, and of any business that is going to achieve this kind of vertical integration. What is the key to doing that well? What have you learned about that specific act? For me, I think there were two key tricks to this. The first one is that you can't build a chip alone; it has to be a team effort. Your most important job is to get great people to go with you—great people who are inspired and excited to do crazy things like this. It is a huge ask to say, "Hey, guys, uproot your lives for six months," or in one case, 12 months (we sent one guy out well ahead). It sucks, but we're lucky to have team members who are in it for the right reasons. I think the second big thing is being able to make decisions very fast. One of the worst situations is when a factory or a vendor is waiting for you to make a call and is just stalled. This happens all the time, even for very small things. So, send folks out, delegate a huge amount of responsibility to them, and say, "Make a reasonable call. It's okay if you're wrong every now and then, but I would much rather be right most of the time and give an answer immediately than wait every time for the perfect response." Speed wins. What about spending money to go faster? There's this "learn-by-doing" approach, which has become so interesting as the technology world has pivoted from software back toward hardware. We've historically outsourced so much of that learning by shipping production overseas and effectively just being the "idea guys" here in the U.S. That is obviously reversing, and you've adopted this hands-on iteration loop. Part of that is a willingness to spend and take risks with capital. Can you talk about that a little bit? I think there's a great quote: "The biggest risk is not taking risk." It's very similar here, where every day there is over a billion dollars in revenue in this category, and a lot of it is inference. Every day we don't ship, we are leaving tons of opportunity on the table. Your willingness to spend money should be extremely high if you can get a very clear ROI out of it. We have this concept called "pre-fetching." When you're waiting for one thing to get done, but you know you'll need to do other things once you have it, you find ways to parallelize the entire schedule. For example, we knew our chip was going to come back on a certain date. We wanted everything possible that could be done without the chip to be finished before the silicon landed. This costs a lot of money. It meant building our entire software stack beforehand. It meant we actually shipped racks to customer data centers without our chips in them—with all of the networking, CPUs, and storage already set up—so we could bring the data center software up before the chips arrived. It meant taking over 700 FPGAs to put the entire full-reticle chip on an FPGA cluster and running a dozen different models with our full inference stack before the silicon came back. It meant we built a thermal dummy chip to mock our chip's thermal profile, and we designed and tested cold plates based on that before the chips came back. It meant the entire production line was ready, we had done many revisions of the circuit board, and the entire product was good to go. This is what that prep gives you. There is another very famous AI chip company that took 10 months to go from getting their silicon back to running inference in a rack. This was publicly announced to their investors as a really big deal. We were able to do it in 40 days. By the time our chip came back, everything was boring. The software was already written, the rack was already there, and the production line was set up. We were just ready to assemble and go. You don't always catch everything; you make some tweaks on the fly and then off you go. In that particular case, that prep was a big part of it, but the shift work made a massive difference too. We literally had a day shift and a night shift. There were team members who would come in at 8:00 a.m. and leave at around midnight, and others who would come in at midnight and leave at 8:00 a.m. We were running around the clock to get to those 40 days. I mean, over half the company lives right next to the office, so that makes it easier to do that type of thing. You pay them to do that, right? You paid them extra. Do you still do that? Yeah. The invisible hand does wonders. I mean, hey, it works for me, too. We're both there. I'd love to take a big step back and talk about the broader ecosystem. The shortages on the supply side and the exposure of risks in the global supply chain around this stuff have become everyday Wall Street Journal front-page news. Look at the stocks people are watching, investing in, and excited about—like memory stocks. These were boring, commodity nothing-burgers five years ago, and now they are the center of global attention. Since you've been building within it, what is your assessment of the globally connected supply chain required to make things like this possible? What scares you, what's working well, and what needs to change? What do you hope to change by virtue of how you build this thing? I think one of the most undervalued pieces of the supply chain story is that none of these components are things where you just buy them and never talk to the vendor again. You have to collaborate. That is the most important part of being successful in chips, whether it's with TSMC or with memory vendors. You need that partnership. For TSMC in particular, people don't understand why they are so valuable. People look at the technology, and the technology is indeed the best in the world. But for me, the real value is all in their service. TSMC's customer service is way better than I have seen at any other company in any other industry. If you say, "Hey, we think we can improve your yield by making this change," you can make a recommendation and they will actually run an experiment—on their own dime, in our case—to see if they can get a higher yield. When they found that we were right and the experiment worked, they moved the change over to the rest of the line. That kind of thing just doesn't happen in most industries. If I went to a steelworks plant and said, "Hey, I want you to change the composition of the steel," they would say, "Screw you." Not TSMC. It is why they are number one and why they are going to win. One of the things that matters a ton is power availability and time to power. The problem is that the more power you want, the more shortage there is. It's actually very similar to chip clusters where it's, like, "Why is Colossus charging 12 dollars an hour for Blackwells?" It's because they're the only place you can buy 20,000 of them at once, right? Like, why is the, like, 500-megawatt data center so hard to find? It's the exact same reason. You know, one of the things we need to think about is, like, how do we get way more juice out of each megawatt? And, like, people are looking throughout the entire stack, whether it's just improving the PUE, but also entirely new hardware to get the most tokens per megawatt to solve this problem. But fundamentally, like, building new buildings is hard. It's much easier to go from 100 megawatts to a gigawatt, from a gigawatt to 10, and 10 to 100. And, you know, we are pushing the limits of what's possible on these timelines. So there's a lot of people trying to scale in their data centers as much as trying to scale them out. One of the—one of the interesting things about a system like this is what it replaces. So if I think about a rack like this versus, I don't know, a set of Blackwells or something or Rubens or—or whatever's coming next, how should I conceptualize that? Like, because it's not just watts, it's also physical space to your—to your point that people all of a sudden Cerebras talked about this in their—in their recent earnings call, that literally, this is the problem, that there's literally no space to put the systems. Yeah. How should I conceptualize what this represents or replaces in terms of other units of compute? Here's how customers think about stuff—deploying models generally, which is, you know, when I'm building a data center or I'm building a cluster, it's not like in the abstract of, "Oh, I like these chips" and, like, "this is the power footprint and so forth." It's like, "I have a real production workload I'm trying to serve." And, you know, for my product to be useful, there's a certain speed I need to serve for that. And for certain products, it's really fast, and certain products, it's really slow. Whatever the speed is, this is my speed. The question is, in a given amount of power, how many users can I serve while guaranteeing that speed? So, another way to put it is ISO, what's called interactivity. What is my throughput? We are just finishing kind of the early innings of the AI infrastructure boom, where people really just cared about speed. You know, GPUs were not able to reach a lot of the speeds of other types of chips, like all these SRAM chips, you know, thousands of tokens per second. And that enabled tons of new use cases that got people very excited. There's an entirely new wave of AI chips, you know, us being one of them, that are all going to be able to hit these speeds. The question then is, if you're hitting these speeds, what is the number of users you can serve at the same time? And by proxy, how much money are you spending per user? And this is where—where the big delta comes from. How do you conceptualize—like, in NVIDIA land, so you've said Brian—Brian Loyler has joined the company, he helped build the DGX and HGX platforms—if I think about a rack of those versus a rack of yours, what is—what is the replacement look like? Yeah, so for—for Llama 3 70B, which is one of the most common models used today, we can serve—a single rack of Etched can serve as many users as 160 H100s. And that's if you use the same power budget. If you used like—if you just wanted to replace H100s for H100s, it's even more. But ISO power, you know, it's many, many clusters of GPUs. And it's really that density of flops on the prefill side and memory bandwidth on the decode side that lets you serve way more users per rack. Gavin, take a crack at—actually, Rob, take a crack at it, too. One final big takeaway for people. Like, you're at this really interesting moment. I was super happy to reach back out and come see you guys here in San Jose, see the energy of the team, get a quick tour, walk around. If you just sum it up, what is the final bit of wisdom or story or point that you'd like people to walk away from this discussion with? Well, I think it comes down to being on the right side of history, where—where as I look at AI over the next decade, there are very few technologies that I can look at and say this is going to be 10 times better every year. And inference is one of them. Flops, I can see flops getting 10 times better every year. I can see efficiency getting 10 times better. I can see model quality getting better. And there's no reason we should think we're going to slow down. If anything, the pressure is to speed up. So if you're a builder, if you're an investor, if you're just somebody who cares about the future, you should be looking at how do we enable the next 10x, the next 100x, the next 1000x of this stuff. And it's going to happen way faster than you think. And for me, it's that we take big risks. That if we want to go out and be way better than everything else that's out there today, we can't do things the same way. We have to be willing to go out and throw out the old rules, throw out the old ways of doing things, and say, "How can we do this better?" And that means taking big technical risks. It means taking big scheduling risks, taking big people risks. And that's what makes it exciting. That's why we're here. We want to do the things that everyone else says is impossible. If I have a 100 megawatt data center, how many, you know, software agents can I run at the same time? So, when people are doing that evaluation, our hardware is going to generally be able to get you an order of magnitude more concurrency at a given level of interactivity. So that directly translates into, you know, tokens per watt, tokens per dollar, all the things people care about when they're actually serving, you know, these giant mixture of expert models at scale. There's these now famous interactivity curves, right? So you can plot—not many people publish them—but you can see a Blackwell curve, you can see an AMD curve, which is a little bit worse than Blackwell's and it's still an 800 billion dollar company. So if you think about what then the impacts are of shifting that curve, not just a little bit further out but, but much further out, what are the things that most excite you about what this will enable? I mean, I want to go out and solve some of the hardest problems and I want to go solve these in much less time. I mean, there were things growing up that I was not sure I'd be able to live to see. For example, the unit disk conjecture, one of the things that we talked about in college. And I was not sure I'd see that proven in my life. And this was done by an AI model and it was done over a long period of time. But if you're able to then run the same model 10 times faster, you can go shrink the time to go have these breakthroughs. And there's a huge number of other problems in math like this as well that I worry it will take a hundred, a thousand years to go prove a thing like this, but you can either have a much smarter model or a model of the same intelligence running much faster, you can then shrink that and I can see it. And it's so cool seeing these breakthroughs get made. I am so, so excited to see much more of this happen. I think too often people think about tasks and applications and stuff in these very short time horizons. So, you know, doing a chat and it's like 50 percent faster is nice but it's not like game-changing. As these agents go longer and longer time horizon and the models get more and more capable, you're going to see gigantic bodies of work that would take months of compute. And we think about this in wall-clock time. Like if you talk to a pre-training researcher at a lab, they'll tell you that wall-clock time often is one of the most important things that matters. And what wall-clock time means is the time from, you know, starting your run to finishing it to actually get data back. If, if you can shrink this time, you know, from a six-month run to a, you know, a two-month experiment, you're going to be able to do many more iterations and people will make changes on the model architecture to actually improve the wall-clock time. It's very similar here in terms of how we think about the use cases, which is, you know, the exciting part about super low-latency decode is wall-clock time on long-horizon tasks becomes much shorter. So a year-long compute build would now take a month, and that month-long compute build will now take three days, and that three-day compute build will now take seven hours and so forth and so forth. So that's the thing that I think is really hard to internalize because the models are just getting capable, capable enough to do this stuff. I thought it was really cool months ago when Cursor published that they had a bunch of coding agents build an entire browser from scratch in a week. Totally nuts. And like that will soon happen in under an hour. And there's going to be many of those types of things that are going to happen with these massive parallel agents all working on a given task. What are the ultimate limitations of these systems? Is it just like a physics question? Like, like how many times faster, cheaper can we get theoretically? Like how do you think? There's a lot. There's a lot of room at the bottom, as they say. That if you think about chip-to-chip latencies, on a NVIDIA product you're looking at 4,000 nanoseconds to go from one chip to another. We'll be able to do much better than that. What's the mathematical limit? Is speed of light. You can do it in just a handful, like two, three nanoseconds. And there are 4,000. 4,000 today. There is a lot of room at the bottom. Same thing for things like power efficiency that, sure, we're able to go out and save a huge amount by bringing the voltage down by so much. But you could go lower. You could go much, much lower. It's very challenging but when I think about 20, 30 years in the future, then I think it's inevitable. And also for economies of scale, for cluster scale-up. For a long time, eight chips was the biggest scale-up domain. NVIDIA then had the NVL72, bringing it to well, 72. But you could be way, way bigger. You look at like a fab, for example, you have a 40 billion dollar single monolithic building with only a handful of lines running through it. You could have the same kind of thing for some futuristic mega cluster. 40 billion dollars, 100 billion dollars as a giant mega token factory, serving one or a handful of models for a massive number of users to get that same economies of scale thing. Same model, massive number of people. You, you mentioned kernels engineering and that being your first job. That has emerged as a thing that nobody had ever heard of in their lives to now something that you hear about all the time, the importance of it to eke more performance out of the, you know, the bare raw metal. When will that just be something that AI does entirely as well? Are humans still the best kernel engineers? Are they doing it with the assistance of AI systems? Like, how far down will humans still be in the loop of designing these things? Like when will that go away? Today it's all very hybrid. And the best kernels are still written by human-AI collaborations. Also, any AI model is built up of these fundamental primitives, like matmuls, like convolutions, like chip-to-chip operations, collectives. And making these overlap and making these really fast matters enormously. And it's the kernel designer's job to go ahead and figure out where can I overlap, how do I allocate memory, how do I verify that if there's some issue, like a retransmit needed, it doesn't stall the whole pipeline. These things are very challenging but they can go and make your model performance be say three, four percent better per optimization and you can do so many. And we thought about our software stack—wanted to go skate to where the puck is going to be. And three years ago, there were kind of two ways you could build software. One of them was to invest heavily in graph compilers. These things are not very performant but they work out of the box. They don't require a human to go come in and tweak all the kernels. But we went the opposite direction. We are kernels-first programming, and that means that, yeah, it for a long time did not work out of the box. But if you were a kernels expert, you could get incredibly, incredibly high performance. And the thing about this is that now as the coding models get better and better, they're doing more and more of the kernel generation task. And when the models keep getting smarter, they'll eventually do all of it. They will become superhuman. So we're going to build for where the world is going. And even today, we think about our profiling tools or debugging stack, we think about it from how will the model use these tools more than we think about how will humans use these tools. We sometimes run experiments internally and we had Codex actually get GPT-OSS running from scratch just based off of our docs, completely by itself. Wow. And they did it I think overnight. We think about game selection a lot, and what we mean by that is making sure we're investing our energy in the right bets. Because regardless of what you choose to work on, it will take tremendous effort. And you know, one of the things that we started with was, you know, the decision explicitly not to build an arbitrary graph compiler, not to support arbitrary PyTorch, not to support arbitrary CUDA, not to support arbitrary ONNX graphs. But instead we envisioned a world where there was going to be under a hundred models that actually mattered, and they were all going to look very similar from the underlying mathematical perspective, and that we were going to build primitives using physics that were going to accelerate these as much as humanly possible, and we were going to allow the most sophisticated customers to have direct access to the hardware and do whatever they want. And that has saved us a tremendous amount of time not having to build a compiler and that has allowed us to actually get much more performance. And funnily enough, when we started, a lot of people dismissed this idea, and the only people that took us seriously were in high-frequency trading, 'cause they all—they all hate compilers too, they all write their own kernels. And we've had dozens of people from high-frequency trading join the team because they saw this philosophy too. What are the limits to vertical integration? Like where do you—how do you know where to draw the line? And I—I'm starting with this question to talk a bit about the broader market. The circumstances of the broader market are really interesting to me where the vast majority of chips, of AI chips, get bought by a very small set of customers. Many of those customers are themselves trying to design their own AI chips. OpenAI announced Tlaloc. It seems like this very funny circumstance where like the most valuable thing in the world all kind of flows through a couple chip makers, a couple chip buyers. They all seem to be kind of doing—thinking about doing each other's job. And then you've got this circumstance where like, okay, then these things go into a data center and then you've got neoclouds and inference providers and this other part of the stack. You've got model builders and providers. Like I can imagine a world where because you have the best hardware, you design models and you build data centers. You know, like—like you leak outside of your current vertical. So like, how do you think about where to draw the lines for the business? We have a saying that production is the product. Ultimately what matters here is, we know inference is going to be the biggest market in the world. Whoever produces the most tokens is going to be the most valuable company in the world. So all the decisions we make is how do we get the most token capacity online as possible? And part of that is building a really good product that has way more throughput, that can run at way better latencies and so forth, so we can, you know, per chip we make get way more tokens online. Another part of it is like not doing parts of the stack unless we absolutely have to to get to giant scale. So there are parts that we decided to do because it was absolutely required to get to scale. Like building the rack instead of just building the chips. Like doing a CM model instead of doing a JDM model. But you know, there are parts of it that are kind of noise to us right now. Like we're not going and building our own data centers today, that doesn't actually help us get more capacity online. You know, in general our customers are actually making power and moving their clusters around to get our chips online because they're such high throughput. If there was a world where other things were a constraint, we would totally go and integrate with them. But the reality is we're just purely focused on getting as many tokens online as possible. And I think this comes down to economies of scale again. That at certain parts of the stack there are huge economies of scale and others there aren't. For example, on designing models, huge economies of scale there. For chip fabrication, same story. But for example, if you think about building some small metal part inside of that rack, there's not that same effect. So we think the natural boundaries are on the chip side, on the bottom, and at the model layer at the top. And we'll fill the whole gap between. A few weeks ago, there was a guy who was running a next-generation AI chip for, you know, one of—one of the like frontier companies. And he was trying to recruit one of our architects. And this person actually kind of did an Uno reverse card and started recruiting the person trying to recruit our—our guy. And within a week we hired him. And I was going on a walk as like we were kind of finalizing the offer. I was like, "Well you're leading this super important project, why are you deciding to join?" And his answer was super interesting, which was, "It fundamentally is not existential for my company for this product to win." For Google with TPUs, like their—their revenue comes from search. Google won't fail if TPUs fail. That's right. Meta won't fail if MTIA fails, Microsoft won't fail if Maia fails, and OpenAI won't fail if Tlaloc fails. Ultimately, this is our product. It is like completely unsurprising that the best chip in the world is built by a company that only builds that chip. It's NVIDIA, right? And like for us, like it is completely existential for us to get as many—as much token capacity online as possible, and, you know, it recruits a set of talent and it recruits the support from suppliers and from customers that view it with the level of intensity that we do. Look at the raw flop stats. You go compare any of these chips built by the labs or by the hyperscalers. The flop density for say FP8 times FP8 is lower than the Blackwell B300. Yep. And that makes sense because they don't have to go take the risk. They just have to go out and build a similar enough product and not pay the NVIDIA tax. As I think about you guys building the solution, your—the process of doing so is—is solving a sequence of really hard challenges. What has been the single episode that was the hardest to overcome? When we were designing the chip, we built this massive, massive FPGA cluster to go out and verify the full chip worked as is. And FPGAs are digital entities. You can go test digital logic but not analog logic. And it turns out that when the chip came back we began to go see issues in our attention computation of incorrect results. And we realized, wait a minute, there's a problem where the back-pressuring logic across a clock-domain crossing is failing. And this is going to cause the chip to produce wrong results and it is very, very hard to solve. And we realized there was one and only one way to solve it is we had to go line up two clock signals on our chip to within 50 picoseconds. That is literally 50 trillionths of a second. And we had to go get these signals aligned to this super small granularity and do it on every chip, two billion times a second. A lot of people said this was impossible. We had people quit. Yeah. That people literally were like, "This problem is unsolvable and, best of luck guys." Well, when you have a problem like that, step one is okay, let's assume the problem is solvable. How would it be solved? Well first we realized what we have to be able to do is find a way to go ahead and move our clock phase by a picosecond, 10 picoseconds. And we had an idea of what if we had these two clocks and we hit them just a little bit apart from each other. And we figured out that, hey, if you go out and figure out the phase, if we then go out and use a drifting mechanism to go ahead and wait for just the right amount of time to get that 50 picoseconds always lined up, we can go do this extremely reliably and then lock the phases exactly where they have to be, so we can guarantee this never happens. Ooh. People were I think somewhat blown away that this worked and that worked as well as it actually did but we made it work. How long did that take? That was actually about two weeks. That—it was a dark two weeks. It was a very scary two weeks. But it was a kind of thing where when that kind of thing happens, that is the most important time to go ahead and be investing effort. That that is the hardest time to go do it when you feel like things are hopeless. But like the sooner you solve that problem, the sooner you can get back to building and scaling up production to mass volumes. I think a lot of our story is like as Gavin says, assume it is possible. Like assume it is way possible—possible to have a chip with way more flops on it. Assume it is possible to have a system with way lower latency between chips. Assume it is possible to create a shared memory pool that can run at way higher bandwidth. Like, how would one do it? A lot of the time when we do experiments, we will do dozens of experiments and all of them will fail. But like we only need one to work. There was multiple times—I mean Gavin I think was—was leading the charge on our—on our chip bring-up with I think three different board experiments and three of them worked. And all three of them were worth their weight in gold. This is one of the things where people come to me and say, "Gavin, almost none of your experiments work." And there's only so much memory being produced. And that's why actually for our first-gen product we built it on a different supply chain than the Rubens. So you know we're on 4 nanometer, Rubens on 3 nanometer, we're on a different HBM than Rubens and so forth. So it actually is not a zero-sum thing. It's a positive-sum where more is more. That when you think about how mechanical devices have to work it's often very different.
